The Classification of Existents: Necessary and Possible
Razi defines the necessary and the possible as the two categories of existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental division of all existents into the necessary and the possible based on their essences.

Core Idea
Existence is divided into what cannot cease to exist and what can.

This lesson introduces the first step in the philosophical proof for the existence of God: the classification of all things into those that are necessary in themselves and those that are possible. This dichotomy is based on whether the essence of a thing inherently prevents non-existence or not. Understanding this classification is crucial for following the subsequent argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to get a sense of the argument.
Notice the succinct definitions of necessary and possible.
2
Grasp the definitions
Focus on the definitions: the necessary is that whose essence prevents non-existence; the possible is that whose essence allows non-existence.
These definitions are based on the essence, not on temporal considerations.
3
See the logical outcome
The argument concludes that every existent must be either necessary or possible. This sets up the next step of the proof.
This classification is exhaustive and exclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القصات الأولي في مراتب مقد ماتت هذه الدلائل على الوجه المشهور عند الحكماء (۱) نقول : لا شك في وجود موجود ، وكل موجود فإما أن تكون حقيقته مانعة من قبول العدم، وإما أن لا تكون . فالأول هو الواجب لذاته . والثاني هو الممكن ) لذاته ( (٣) . فثبت أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بوجود موجود ، وثبت أن كل موجود ، فهو إما واجب لذاته ( وإما ممكن لذاته ( ينتج أن في الوجود ، إما موجود واجب الوجود (٤) لذاته ، وإما موجود لذاته ممكن لذاته ، كان الأول فهو المطلوب ، وإن كان الثاني فنقول الممكن لذاته لا يترجح طرفيه على الآخر ، ( إلا بمرجح ( ) وذلك المرجح إن كان واجب لذاته فهو المطلوب يترجح أحد وإن كان ممكناً لذاته عاد التقسيم الأول فيه ، فإما أن يتسلسل أو يدور ، وهما محالان ، وإما أن ينتهي إلى أن ينتهي إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته وهو المطلوب . واعلم أن هذا الدليل مبني على مقدمات : أولها : أن الممكن لا يترجح أحد طرفيه على الآخر إلا المرجح . وثانيها : بيان (٦) أن هذه الحاجـة حاصلة في
The first classifications [on] the ranks of the premises of these proofs according to the famous method of the philosophers. We say: There is no doubt about the existence of an existent. Every existent is either such that its reality prevents the acceptance of non-existence, or not. The first is the necessary in itself (wājib li-dhātihi). The second is the possible in itself (mumkin li-dhātihi). So it is established that we must acknowledge the existence of an existent, and it is established that every existent is either necessary in itself or possible in itself. The result is that in existence there is either an existent necessary in itself, or an existent possible in itself. If the first, then it is the sought. And if the second, we say: The possible in itself does not have its two sides (existence and non-existence) weighted towards one without a determinant (murajjih). If that determinant is necessary in itself, then it is the sought. And if it is possible in itself, the first division returns to it. Then either there is an infinite regress (tasalsul) or a circularity (dawr), both of which are impossible, or it ends at an existent necessary in itself, which is the sought. Know that this proof is built on premises: First: that the possible does not have one of its two sides weighted except by a determinant. Second: the explanation that this need is present in
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the first classification of premises for the proof of the necessary being according to the philosophers. The argument begins with a dichotomy: every existent is either necessary (its essence prevents non-existence) or possible (its essence allows non-existence). Since existence is self-evident, there must be an existent. If it is necessary, the proof is complete. If it is possible, then its existence and non-existence are equally possible; it needs a determinant to tip the scale. That determinant, if necessary, is the sought. If it is also possible, then the same reasoning applies, leading to either an infinite regress or circularity, both impossible. Therefore, the chain must terminate in a necessary being. The author then notes that this proof rests on two premises (though the second is cut off in this window): first, that the possible needs a determinant; second, that this need is actual. The commentary notes the incomplete second premise.

Key Terms
واجب لذاته
ممكن لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument begins by assuming the existence of a necessary being.
Correction: Rather, it begins with the observation that something exists, and then divides all existents into necessary and possible based on their essences. The necessary being is not assumed but is the conclusion of the subsequent argument.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of the necessary in itself?
A) That which exists always
B) That whose reality prevents the acceptance of non-existence
C) That which is uncaused
D) That which is self-sufficient

Key Takeaway
All existents are either necessary in themselves (cannot not exist) or possible in themselves (can exist or not).

The Proof of the Necessary Being from Possibility
Razi demonstrates why the existence of a possible being entails the existence of a necessary being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of a possible being leads necessarily to the existence of a necessary being, through the need for a determinant and the impossibility of infinite regress or circularity.

Core Idea
Every possible being requires a necessary being to bring it into existence.

This lesson continues the classic philosophical proof by examining what happens if the existing thing we encounter is a possible being. The argument shows that such a being cannot be self-sufficient; it requires a determinant to tip the scales from non-existence to existence. That determinant itself must either be necessary or lead to an impossibility like infinite regress or circularity, forcing the conclusion that a necessary being exists.

Guided Path
1
Follow the conditional structure
Read the passage and notice the 'if-then' structure: if the existent is possible, then it needs a determinant.
The argument is built on a conditional syllogism.
2
Understand the need for a determinant
A possible being has equal potential for existence and non-existence; it needs an external cause to tip the balance.
This is the first premise of the proof.
3
Trace the options for the determinant
The determinant is either necessary or possible. If necessary, conclusion reached. If possible, the same reasoning applies to it.
This leads to a chain of possibilities.
4
Recognize the impossibility of regress and circularity
An infinite regress or circular dependency is logically impossible, so the chain must stop.
The author explicitly states both are impossible.
5
Reach the conclusion
Therefore, the chain terminates at a necessary being, which is the sought conclusion.
This is the desired proof of God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Proof from the Division of Existence into Necessary and Possible
The author presents the first proof: division into necessary and possible leads to a Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the division of existence into necessary and possible beings leads to the affirmation of a Necessary Being.

Core Idea
Every possible being needs a preponderator, ending in a Necessary Being.

This lesson presents the first proof in the text for the existence of Allah. The argument is logical and begins with the simple observation that something exists. By dividing every existent into two categories—necessary in itself and possible in itself—the argument demonstrates that the existence of a possible being requires an external cause, which must ultimately be a necessary being.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic division
Read the passage carefully and note that all existents are divided into two categories: necessary in itself (its essence prevents non-existence) and possible in itself (its essence does not prevent non-existence).
This division is exhaustive and mutually exclusive.
2
Follow the argument for a possible being
If an existent is possible, it requires a preponderator to tip the scales from non-existence to existence. Identify that the preponderator must itself be either necessary or possible.
The argument then applies the same division to the preponderator.
3
Recognize why regress is impossible
If the preponderator is possible, it leads to either an infinite regress (tasalsul) or a circularity (dawr), both of which are logically impossible. Therefore, the chain must end in a necessary being.
This is the key step that forces the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقول : لا شك في وجود موجود ، وكل موجود فإما أن تكون حقيقته مانعة من قبول العدم، وإما أن لا تكون . فالأول هو الواجب لذاته . والثاني هو الممكن لذاته . فثبت أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بوجود موجود ، وثبت أن كل موجود ، فهو إما واجب لذاته وإما ممكن لذاته ينتج أن في الوجود ، إما موجود واجب الوجود لذاته ، وإما موجود لذاته ممكن لذاته ، كان الأول فهو المطلوب ، وإن كان الثاني فنقول الممكن لذاته لا يترجح طرفيه على الآخر ، إلا بمرجح وذلك المرجح إن كان واجب لذاته فهو المطلوب وإن كان ممكناً لذاته عاد التقسيم الأول فيه ، فإما أن يتسلسل أو يدور ، وهما محالان ، وإما أن ينتهي إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته وهو المطلوب .
We say: There is no doubt about the existence of an existent, and every existent is such that either its essence prevents the reception of non-existence, or it does not. The first is the Necessary in itself (al-wājib li-dhātih). The second is the Possible in itself (al-mumkin li-dhātih). So it is established that one must acknowledge the existence of an existent, and it is established that every existent is either necessary in itself or possible in itself, resulting that in existence there is either an existent that is necessary in itself, or an existent that is possible in itself. If it is the first, then it is what is sought. If it is the second, we say: the possible in itself does not have one of its two sides preponderating over the other except by a preponderator. That preponderator, if it is necessary in itself, then it is what is sought. If it is possible in itself, the first division applies to it again, so either there is an infinite regress or a circularity, and both are impossible, or it terminates at an existent that is necessary in itself, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational proof for the existence of a Necessary Being (Wājib al-Wujūd). The argument begins by noting that every existent must be either such that its very essence prevents non-existence (the Necessary in itself) or not (the Possible in itself). The author then establishes that we must acknowledge existence itself. Dividing every existent into these two categories, the argument proceeds: if the existent in question is necessary, the goal is reached. If it is possible, then a possible being, being equally open to existence and non-existence, requires an external preponderator to tip the scales. That preponderator, if necessary, is the sought-after being; if itself possible, the same division applies, leading to an infinite regress or circularity, both absurd. Therefore, the chain must end in a necessary being, which is the desired conclusion.

Key Terms
واحب لذاته (wājib li-dhātih)
ممكن لذاته (mumkin li-dhātih)
مرجح (murajjiḥ)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
دور (dawr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes the existence of a necessary being from the start.
Correction: The argument does not assume a necessary being; it derives it from the mere acknowledgement of existence and the logical analysis of what existence entails.

Checkpoint
What is the immediate consequence if the preponderator of a possible being is itself possible?
A) The preponderator is necessary.
B) The same division applies, leading either to infinite regress or circularity.
C) The possible being becomes necessary.
D) The argument stops.

Key Takeaway
The existence of a Necessary Being is proven by the impossibility of an infinite regress of causes for possible beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on many things for your existence: your job, health, relationships, even your own body. When you truly consider that each of these is a possible being that needs a preponderator, are you placing your ultimate trust in the Necessary Being or in these fragile possibles? How would your daily anxieties change if you truly believed that your existence hangs solely on the Necessary?
?
The argument shows that without the Necessary Being, there would be an infinite regress or circularity—no real explanation for existence. Reflect on a time when you felt your life was 'circular' or without purpose. How does this proof challenge you to ground your life in the Only One who is self-sufficient?
?
The passage states there is 'no doubt about the existence of an existent.' Yet you often live as if your own existence is self-explanatory. How does acknowledging that you are a 'possible being'—needing a preponderator every moment—change your view of your own fragility and your need for the Necessary Being?

The Six Premises of the Proof for the Necessary Being
The author enumerates the six premises on which the preceding proof rests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six logical premises on which the famous kalam cosmological argument for the Necessary Being rests, and why establishing each premise is crucial for the proof.

Core Idea
The proof for the Necessary Being stands on six premises; if even one fails, the entire argument collapses.

In the previous lesson, you encountered the foundational proof that the existence of a necessary being is rationally required. Now, the author unpacks that proof by listing the six specific premises that support it. These premises form the skeleton of the argument, and each one will be examined in detail. This passage gives you the big picture before diving into the technical discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the English translation. Notice how the author presents each premise in a numbered sequence.
The author uses ordinal 'أولها', 'ثانيها', etc.
2
Identify each premise
List the six premises in your own words. Pay attention to the logical order: from possibility, need, existence, timing, to refuting circularity and infinite regress.
3
Understand the conclusion
After the premises are established, the author concludes that a necessary being must exist. Then he adds a further step: showing the sensible world is not necessary, so it needs that being.
4
Reflect on the structure
Ask yourself why these six premises are essential. What would happen if one were missing? This will help you see the logical dependency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذا الدليل مبني على مقدمات : أولها : أن الممكن لا يترجح أحد طرفيه على الآخر إلا المرجح . وثانيها : بيان أن هذه الحاجة حاصلة في حال الحدوث أو في حال البقاء ؟ وثالثها: أن ذلك المرجح يجب أن يكون موجوداً . ورابعها : أنه يجب أن يكون موجوداً حال حصول الأثر . وخامسها : أن الدور باطل . وسادسها : أن التسلسل باطل . وعند تمام الكلام في تقرير هذه المقدمات الست يحصل الجزم بأنه لا بد من الاعتراف بوجود موجود واجب الوجود لذاته . ثم إذا بينا بعد ذلك أن هذا العالم المحسوس يمتنع أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ، فعند ذلك نعلم أن هذا العالم المحسوس يحتاج في وجوده إلى وجود موجود واجب الوجود لذاته ، وهو المطلوب . فلنفرد لتقرير كل مقدمة واحدة من هذه المقدمات : فصلاً .
Know that this proof is based on premises: First: that the possible does not have one of its two sides preponderated except by a preponderator. Second: clarifying whether this need occurs in the state of origination (ḥudūth) or in the state of continuance (baqāʾ). Third: that that preponderator must be existent. Fourth: that it must be existent at the time of the effect's occurrence. Fifth: that circularity (dawr) is invalid. Sixth: that infinite regress (tasalsul) is invalid. Upon the completion of the discussion in establishing these six premises, certainty is obtained that one must acknowledge the existence of an existent that is necessary in itself. Then if we afterwards clarify that this sensible world cannot be necessary in itself, then at that point we know that this sensible world needs, in its existence, the existence of an existent that is necessary in itself, and that is what is sought. So let us devote a separate section for establishing each of these premises.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage enumerates the six premises on which the previously presented proof depends. The author explicitly states that the proof is built upon these premises, and only when they are fully established can certainty about the Necessary Being be attained. The six premises are: (1) A possible being requires a preponderator to determine its existence or non-existence. (2) It must be clarified whether this need is only at the moment of origination or also during continuance. (3) The preponderator must itself be existent. (4) The preponderator must be existent at the time the effect occurs. (5) Circular reasoning (dawr) is impossible. (6) Infinite regress (tasalsul) is impossible. After establishing these, we conclude that a necessary being must exist. Then, if we further show that the sensible world cannot be necessary, we know the world needs that necessary being. The author announces that each premise will be treated in its own separate section.

حاجّة (ḥājah)
الحدوث (ḥudūth)
البقاء (baqāʾ)
دور (dawr)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
عالم محسوس (ʿālam maḥsūs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The six premises are merely optional or decorative; the proof could work without them.
Correction: The author explicitly states that the proof is 'based on' these premises, and only after their full establishment does certainty arise. Each premise is essential.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of fully establishing the six premises?
A) The sensible world is shown to be necessary.
B) We obtain certainty that a necessary being exists.
C) The argument for a necessary being is weakened.
D) The premises are all proven false.

Key Takeaway
The proof for the Necessary Being depends on six specific premises, each of which must be established to reach certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Third to Sixth Premises: Establishing the Necessary Existent
Razi enumerates premises and conclusion for proving necessary existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third through sixth premises that Razi uses to prove the existence of a necessary being, and how they culminate in the conclusion that the world depends on a necessary existent.

Core Idea
Six logical premises culminate in the necessary existent.

Razi has been setting up a proof for the existence of a necessary being. In this passage, he lists the remaining premises after the first two. These premises are about the preponderant, its existence at the time of effect, and the invalidity of circularity and infinite regress. Establishing them leads to certainty about a necessary existent. Let's examine each premise.

Guided Path

Premises are: preponderant must exist, must exist at time of effect, circularity invalid, infinite regress invalid.
3
Understand the conclusion
After listing premises, Razi states that establishing these six premises gives certainty in a necessary existent. Then he adds that if we also show the world is not necessary, we conclude the world depends on a necessary being.
This is the overall argument structure.

Razi announces he will dedicate a chapter to each premise. This prepares us for detailed arguments.

حال الحدوث أو في حال البقاء ؟ وثالثها: أن ذلك المرجح يجب أن يكون موجوداً . ورابعها: أنه يجب أن يكون موجوداً حال حصول الأثر . وخامسها: أن الدور باطل . وسادسها: أن التسلسل باطل . وعند تمام الكلام في تقرير هذه المقدمات الست يحصل الجزم بأنه لا بد من الاعتراف بوجود موجود واجب الوجود لذاته . ثم إذا بينا بعد ذلك أن هذا العالم المحسوس يمتنع أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ، فعند ذلك نعلم أن هذا العالم المحسوس يحتاج في وجوده إلى وجود موجود واجب الوجود لذاته ، وهو المطلوب . فلنفرد لتقرير كل مقدمة واحدة من هذه المقدمات : فصلاً .

The Fourteenth Shubha: The World Must Have an Effecter
The fourteenth sophistical objection arguing that the world must have an effecter, whether necessary or voluntary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the fourteenth sophistical objection and how it attempts to disprove the need for an effecter by showing that any assumption leads to the absurdity of preference without a preponderator.

Core Idea
The fourteenth shubha argues that in every scenario, the world's existence involves a preference without a cause.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection against the proof from contingency. It systematically examines all possibilities for the world's cause and argues that each leads to the problem of 'preference without a preponderator.' Understanding this objection is key to appreciating the depth of theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Let the argument's flow become clear before analyzing.
Note the footnotes (۳) and (٤) which indicate textual variants.
2
Identify the two main branches
The objection divides the possibilities: either the world has an effecter or not. If it does, the effecter is either necessitating or voluntary. Identify these three cases.
3
Examine the necessitating branch
If the effecter is necessitating, the objection gives several examples where a specific outcome is preferred over equals without a cause. List these examples.
Examples include the pole of the sphere, planets' sides, and the world's location in the void.
4
Examine the voluntary branch
If the effecter is voluntary, the objection points to the choice of a specific time for creation. Understand why this is also seen as a preference without a cause.
5
Understand the conclusion
The objection concludes that in all suppositions, the claim that the contingent is independent of an effecter is necessary (i.e., the need for an effecter leads to absurdity). Grasp the rhetorical force of this argument.
This is a shubha, meaning a sophistical objection that must be refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة عشر : العالم إما أن يكون له مؤثر أو لا يكون ، فإن لم يكن له مؤثر ، مع أن العالم ممكن لذاته ، فقد وقع الممكن لا عن مؤثر . وان كان له مؤثر فذلك المؤثر ، إما أن يكون واجباً أو مختاراً . فإن كان موجباً فاختصاص النقطة المعينة ، بأن تكون قطباً للفلك دون سائر النقط المتساوية لها : رجحان للمكن لا لمرجح ، وكذلك القول في اختصاص كل واحد من الكواكب الجانب معين من الفلك، دون سائر الجوانب ، وكذا القول في اختصاص بعض جوانب المتمم (۳) بالرقة دون سائر الجوانب وكذا القول في حصول كرة العالم في جانب معين من الخلاء الذي لا نهاية له عند من يقول بذلك الخلاء . وإما إن كان مختاراً كان تخصيص إحداث العالم بالوقت المعين دون [ سائر ] (٤) ما قبله ، وما بعده ترجيحاً لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر من غير مرجح فثبت : أن القول باستغناء الممكن عن المؤثر لازم على كل التقديرات .
The fourteenth sophistical objection: The world either has an effecter or does not have one. If it does not have an effecter, while the world is contingent in itself, then the contingent has occurred without an effecter. And if it has an effecter, then that effecter is either necessitating or voluntary. If it is necessitating, then the specification of a particular point to be the pole of the sphere rather than other equivalent points is a preponderance of the possible without a preponderator. Similarly for the specification of each of the planets to a particular side of the sphere rather than other sides. Similarly for the specification of some sides of the supposed [?] with thinness rather than other sides. Similarly for the occurrence of the sphere of the world in a particular side of the infinite void, according to those who affirm that void. And if it is voluntary, then the specification of the creation of the world at a particular time rather than all that preceded it and all that follows it is a preponderance of one of the two possibilities over the other without a preponderator. So it is established: The claim that the contingent is independent of an effecter is necessary on all suppositions.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents the fourteenth sophistical objection against the argument that the world requires an effecter. It starts by dividing the possibilities: either the world has an effecter or not. If not, then a contingent thing exists without an effecter, which is problematic. If it has an effecter, that effecter is either necessitating (produces necessarily) or voluntary (chooses). If necessitating, then various specifications (like a point being the pole, planets having specific orbits, etc.) would involve a preference for one possible over another without a cause, which is absurd. If voluntary, then the choice of a specific time for creation over others is also a preference without a cause. Thus, in all cases, the objection claims that the notion that the contingent does not need an effecter (i.e., the proponents' view that the contingent needs an effecter leads to absurdities) is inevitable. This objection is likely meant to undermine the proof from contingency.

Key Terms
مؤثر (effecter)
ممكن (contingent)
موجب (necessitating)
مختار (voluntary)
ترجيح بلا مرجح (preference without a preponderator)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourteenth shubha, if the effecter of the world is necessitating, what problem arises?
A) The world would be eternal.
B) The effecter would be contingent.
C) There would be a preference for one possible over another without a cause.
D) The effecter would have no will.

Key Takeaway
The fourteenth shubha attempts to disprove the necessity of an effecter by showing that any possible cause leads to the absurdity of preference without a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The shubha presents a dilemma: either the world has a cause or not, and if it does, that cause must either necessitate or choose. When you consider your own existence, do you sense that you are merely the product of blind necessity or of a purposeful choice? What difference does your answer make to how you live today?
?
This passage argues that every option leads to an absurd 'preference without a preponderator'. Have you ever been paralyzed by a decision because you felt there was no sufficient reason to choose one way over another? How does the reality of God's will—as the ultimate preponderator—free you from that paralysis?
?
If God's action is voluntary, the shubha claims that the specific time of creation is arbitrary. But what if God's choice is not arbitrary but wise? Reflect on a moment in your life when you doubted God's timing. Can you now see that His choice, though mysterious, was not without reason? Are you willing to trust His timing even when you cannot see the reason?

Why Cause and Effect Must Coexist
The author explains why the proof of the Necessary Existent requires the cause to exist simultaneously with the effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' proof of the Necessary Existent depends on the premise that the cause must exist simultaneously with its effect, and how without this premise an infinite regress of contingent beings is possible.

Core Idea
The proof of the Necessary Existent stands or falls on the simultaneity of cause and effect.

The author argues that a subtle but crucial premise underpins the philosophical proof for God's existence. Without assuming that a cause must be present at the same time as its effect, the argument cannot rule out an infinite chain of contingent beings. This passage unpacks why this premise is necessary and how it saves the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the logical flow from stating the premise to its implications.
2
Identify the key terms
Pay attention to terms like al-hukama', al-tasalsul, and al-'illah al-mu'aththirah. These are central to the argument.
3
Understand the philosophers' position on regress
The author highlights that the philosophers accept infinite regress of cycles. This is important because it shows why the proof needs the simultaneity premise.
4
See how the premise closes the loophole
Once the premise is accepted, an infinite regress would imply an actual infinite chain existing simultaneously, which is problematic for the philosophers.
This is the subtle point (al-daqiqa) the author refers to.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع في بيان أن هذا البرهان المذكور في اثبات معرفة واجب الوجود لايتم على أصول الحكماء إلا بعد إقامة الدلالة على أن العلمة واجبة الحصول حال حصول المعلول. أعلم أنه لو لم يجب كون العلة المؤثرة موجودة حال وجود المعلول ، لم يمتنع أن يكون هذا الممكن إنما وجد لأجل شيء كان موجودا قبله، ولم يبق معه، ولو جاز ذلك، لجاز أن يقال: إن كل ممكن فإنه مستند إلى ممكن آخر قبله، لا إلى أول، والتسلسل على هذا الوجه ليس باطلا عند الحكماء، بل هو حق. لأن مذهبهم، أن كل دورة فإنها مسبوقة بدورة أخرى، لا إلى أول، وإذا كان التسلسل على هذا الوجه ليس ممتنعا عند القوم، فحينئذ لا يمكنهم بيان انتهاء الممكنات إلى موجود واجب الوجود، أما إذا ثبت أن العلة المؤثرة يجب كونها موجودة حال وجود المعلول، فعند هذا نقول: لو استند كل ممكن إلى ممكن آخر، إلى غير النهاية، لحصل أسباب ومسببات لا نهاية لها دفعة واحدة، وحينئذ لا يتمكن الفيلسوف من إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته، فظهر أنه لولا صحة هذه المقدمة، لم يقدر أحد من الفلاسفة على إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته، ولأجل هذه الدقيقة فإن الشيخ الرئيس لما شرع في كتاب النجاة في إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته اشتغل بإقامة الدلالة على أن ...
Chapter Seven: On explaining that this proof mentioned for establishing knowledge of the Necessary Existence (wajib al-wujud) is not complete according to the principles of the philosophers (al-hukama') except after establishing the evidence that the cause must exist at the time of the existence of the effect. Know that if it were not necessary for the effective cause (al-'illah al-mu'aththirah) to exist at the time of the existence of the effect, it would not be impossible that this contingent came into existence due to something that existed before it and did not remain with it. And if that were permissible, then it would be permissible to say that every contingent depends on another contingent before it, not on a first, and infinite regress (al-tasalsul) in this manner is not invalid according to the philosophers, but rather it is true. Because their doctrine is that every cycle is preceded by another cycle, without a first. And if infinite regress in this manner is not impossible according to them, then they cannot demonstrate that contingent things terminate at an existent that is necessary of existence. However, if it is established that the effective cause must exist at the time of the existence of the effect, then at this point we say: if every contingent depended on another contingent ad infinitum, then there would occur causes and effects without end all at once, and then the philosopher cannot prove the Necessary Existence in itself. So it becomes apparent that without the soundness of this premise, none of the philosophers would be able to prove the Necessary Existence in itself. And for this subtle point, the Chief Master (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) when he began in the Book of Deliverance (Kitab al-Najat) to prove the Necessary Existence in itself, he occupied himself with establishing the evidence that...
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This is a chapter that explains a crucial premise for the proof of the Necessary Existent. The author argues that if the cause does not have to exist simultaneously with its effect, then an infinite regress of contingent beings is possible (each contingent depends on a prior contingent that no longer exists). Since the philosophers themselves accept such a regress of cycles, they cannot prove a First Cause without the premise of simultaneous causation. Once the premise is established, an infinite regress would imply an actual infinite simultaneous chain, which is problematic. Thus the premise is necessary for the proof.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof of the Necessary Existent works without any additional premises.
Correction: The author shows that it depends on a specific premise about simultaneous causation; otherwise, an infinite regress of contingents is possible and the proof fails.

Checkpoint
Why can the philosophers not prove the Necessary Existent without the premise that the cause exists simultaneously with the effect?
A) Because then the cause would have to be infinite.
B) Because without it, an infinite regress of contingents is not impossible for them, so they cannot posit a first cause.
C) Because the effect would not exist at all.
D) Because the cause would be independent of the effect.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' proof of the Necessary Existent depends on the premise that a cause must exist simultaneously with its effect, otherwise an infinite regress of contingents is possible and the proof fails.

The Necessity of Cause-Effect Simultaneity for the Proof of the Necessary Being
Author establishes that the proof requires cause-effect simultaneity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' proof of the Necessary Being requires the premise that the cause must exist simultaneously with its effect, and how this premise blocks infinite regress.

Core Idea
Without cause-effect simultaneity, infinite regress is not impossible for the philosophers.

The author identifies a critical gap in the standard philosophical argument for the Necessary Being. Without the premise that an efficient cause must coexist with its effect, the argument fails because the philosophers do not reject all infinite regresses. This lesson examines that gap and shows how establishing simultaneity closes it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional scenario
Read the passage and identify what happens if the cause is not required to exist at the same time as its effect. The author shows this leads to a possible infinite regress.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لو لم يجب كون العلة المؤثرة موجودة حال وجود المعلول'.
2
Recognize the philosophers' stance on regress
Notice that the philosophers accept a certain type of infinite regress—cycles without a first—as true. This means they cannot rule out an endless chain of causes.
The text says 'والتسلسل على هذا الوجه ليس باطلا عند الحكماء، بل هو حق'.
3
See how simultaneity changes the argument
Once simultaneity is established, an attempted infinite regress would require all causes and effects to exist at once, which is impossible. Therefore, the chain must terminate in a Necessary Being.
The logical conclusion is that without this premise, the proof fails; with it, the proof succeeds.
4
Note the difference in Ibn Sina's works
The author points out that Ibn Sina included this premise in al-Najat but omitted it in al-Isharat and other works. The author supports the approach in al-Najat.
This shows the subtlety of the argument and its importance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع
في
بيان أن هذا البرهان المذكور في اثبات معرفة واجب الوجود لايتم على أصول الحكماء إلا بعد إقامة الدلالة على أن العلة واجبة الحصول حال حصول المعلول.
أعلم أنه لو لم يجب كون العلة المؤثرة موجودة حال وجود المعلول ، لم يمتنع أن يكون هذا الممكن إنما وجد لأجل شيء كان موجودا قبله ، ولم يبق معه ، ولو جاز ذلك ، لجاز أن يقال : إن كل ممكن فإنه مستند إلى ممكن آخر قبله ، لا إلى أول ، والتسلسل على هذا الوجه ليس باطلا عند الحكماء ، بل هو حق . لأن مذهبهم ، أن كل دورة فإنها مسبوقة بدورة أخرى ، لا إلى أول ، وإذا كان التسلسل على هذا الوجه ليس ممتنعا عند القوم ، فحينئذ لا يمكنهم بيان انتهاء الممكنات إلى موجود واجب الوجود ، أما إذا ثبت أن العلة المؤثرة يجب كونها موجودة حال وجود المعلول ، فعند هذا نقول : لو استند كل ممكن إلى ممكن آخر ، إلى غير النهاية، لحصل أسباب ومسببات لا نهاية لها دفعة واحدة ، وحينئذ لا يتمكن الفيلسوف من إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته ، فظهر أنه لولا صحة هذه المقدمة ، لم يقدر أحد من الفلاسفة على إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته ، ولأجل هذه الدقيقة فإن الشيخ الرئيس لما شرع في كتاب النجاة في إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته اشتغل بإقامة الدلالة على أن العلة المؤثرة يجب أن تكون موجودة حال وجود المعلول ، أما في كتاب الإشارات وسائر الكتب فلم يذكر هذه المقدمة ، والحق ما ذكره في كتاب النجاة بالبيان الذي ذكرناه.
Chapter Seven: On explaining that this proof mentioned for establishing knowledge of the Necessary Being is not complete upon the principles of the philosophers except after establishing the evidence that the cause must exist at the time the effect exists. Know that if it were not necessary for the efficient cause to exist at the time of the existence of the effect, it would not be impossible that this possible being came into existence for the sake of something that existed before it and did not remain with it. And if that were permissible, then it would be permissible to say that every possible being depends on another possible being before it, not on a first. And regress in this manner is not invalid according to the philosophers; rather it is true. Because their doctrine is that every cycle is preceded by another cycle, without a first. And if regress in this manner is not impossible for them, then they cannot demonstrate that possible beings terminate at a being that is Necessary of Existence. But if it is established that the efficient cause must exist at the time of the existence of the effect, then we say: if every possible being depended on another possible being ad infinitum, then there would be causes and effects without end all at once, and then the philosopher would not be able to prove the Necessary Being in itself. So it becomes clear that were it not for the soundness of this premise, none of the philosophers would be able to prove the Necessary Being in itself. And for this subtle point, the Chief Master (Ibn Sina) when he began in the Book of Deliverance (Kitab al-Najat) to prove the Necessary Being in itself, he occupied himself with establishing the evidence that the efficient cause must exist at the time of the existence of the effect. But in the Book of Indications (Kitab al-Isharat) and other books, he did not mention this premise. And the truth is what he mentioned in the Book of Deliverance with the explanation we have stated.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the seventh chapter. The author states that the standard philosophical proof for the Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud) cannot be completed on the principles of the philosophers (al-hukama') unless a certain premise is established: that the efficient cause must exist simultaneously with its effect. Without this premise, the philosophers would not consider an infinite regress of causes impossible. They accept an infinite series of cycles, each preceded by another, with no first cause. Hence, without simultaneity, they cannot prove that the series ends with a self-necessary being. Once simultaneity is affirmed, an infinite regress would require an infinite number of causes and effects existing all at once, which is impossible, and so the proof of the Necessary Being goes through. The author then notes a difference among philosophers: Avicenna (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) included this premise in his Kitab al-Najat but omitted it in al-Isharat and other works. The author asserts that the correct approach is what Avicenna did in al-Najat.

العلة المؤثرة
المعلول
التسلسل
الحكماء
الشيخ الرئيس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All philosophers categorically reject any form of infinite regress.
Correction: The author explains that the philosophers accept an infinite regress of cycles without a first cause; they only reject certain types of regress, such as simultaneous infinite regress.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the philosophers cannot prove the Necessary Being without the premise of cause-effect simultaneity?
A) Because they deny the existence of efficient causes.
B) Because without it, infinite regress is not impossible for them, so the chain may never reach a first cause.
C) Because they believe causes exist after effects.
D) Because they reject the concept of a Necessary Being entirely.

Key Takeaway
The premise of cause-effect simultaneity is essential for the philosophers to prove a self-necessary being, as it blocks the infinite regress they otherwise accept.

Alternative Definition of Necessary and Possible Being
Proposes a new definition to avoid objections in the argument for a necessary being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how redefining the necessary and possible being as independent or needy of a cause avoids previous objections, and why this approach is not immune to all criticism.

Core Idea
Redefining necessary being as 'independent of cause' sidesteps some objections but not all.

This lesson introduces an alternative approach to defining the necessary and possible being. The author proposes definitions based on causal independence instead of susceptibility to non-existence, aiming to avoid the difficulties that plagued earlier formulations. However, he warns that this belief is mistaken — the new definitions do not make the proof immune to all questions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and the English translation to understand the flow of the argument. Notice the author's critique of old definitions and the presentation of a new approach.
2
Understand the old definitions
The author explains that previous definitions defined the necessary being as 'not susceptible to non-existence' and the possible being as 'susceptible to non-existence'. These definitions led to many objections.
Reflect on why definitions based on non-existence might cause difficulties.
3
Grasp the new definitions
The new definitions are: necessary being = independent in existence from a cause; possible being = in need of a cause. This shifts the basis from non-existence to causality.
This is the core of the alternative approach.
4
Recognize the misconception
Some people think this redefinition avoids all objections, but the author explicitly says this belief is mistaken. The new definitions may reduce some questions but do not eliminate them entirely.
5
Examine the proof structure
The proof begins: something exists; that existent is either independent or needy; if independent, the necessary being is proven; if needy, it must have a cause. The argument then continues (but the text cuts off).
Notice that the proof is incomplete in this passage.

Chapter Eight: On presenting this proof in another manner. And it is thought that presenting it in that manner causes most of these questions to fall away. And it will be shown that this thought is mistaken among people.
Some people said: These questions were only raised, and these difficulties only occurred, because at the beginning of the discussion we defined the Necessary of Existence in Itself as: the existent whose essence is absolutely not susceptible to non-existence. And we defined the Possible of Existence in Itself as: the existent whose essence is susceptible to non-existence. And when we defined the Necessary and the Possible with these two definitions, these questions came about and these investigations were directed. So the matter became great, and the inquiry became narrow.
And here is another way, closer to precision and farther from confusion than what preceded, which is to define the Necessary of Existence in Itself as that which is independent in its existence from the cause, and to define the Possible of Existence in Itself as that which is in need in its existence of the cause. With this definition, the assertion of proving a Necessary of Existence in Itself becomes one of the clearest objectives and most evident ends, and none of the mentioned questions are directed at it. For we say: There is no doubt that in existence there is an existent. That existent is either independent in its existence from the cause, or it is in need in its existence of the cause. Commentary
This passage begins Chapter Eight, which aims to present the proof for a necessary being in an alternative way. The author first notes a common misconception: that redefining terms avoids all objections. He then identifies the source of previous difficulties: the earlier definitions based on susceptibility or lack thereof to non-existence. The new approach defines the necessary being as 'independent of a cause' and the possible being as 'needy of a cause.' With these definitions, the proof becomes straightforward: something exists; it must be either independent or needy; if independent, the necessary being is established; if needy, it requires a cause, and the argument continues (though the text cuts off). The passage sets up the proof but does not complete it.

Alternative Causal Proof for the Necessary Being
Alternative causal proof for the Necessary Being, with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an alternative causal proof for the existence of a Necessary Being and why redefining terms does not evade deeper objections.

Core Idea
Redefining 'necessary' as 'independent of a cause' simplifies the proof but does not resolve the core issues.

This lesson presents a revised version of the proof for the Necessary Being, using a different definition of necessity. The author shows how this alternative formulation seems to avoid earlier objections, but then an objector points out that changing definitions does not change the underlying rational problems. We will trace this debate and see how it reinforces the need for rigorous metaphysical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with the alternative definitions
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the shift from defining necessary as 'not receptive to non-existence' to 'independent of a cause'.
Pay attention to the author's claim that this new definition avoids previous objections.
2
Analyze the simplified proof
Identify the two-part proof: (1) any existent is either independent or dependent on a cause; (2) if dependent, it requires a cause, and an infinite regress is impossible, so an independent existent must exist.
The proof uses the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress.
3
Examine the objection
The objector argues that even with the new definition, it is possible that the supposedly independent being has an essence equally receptive to existence and non-existence, but existence is preponderated without a preponderator.
This objection challenges the certainty of the proof.
4
Understand the author's response
The author responds that verbal distinctions do not change rational realities; they only shift the discussion to another level with little benefit.
Consider whether this response adequately addresses the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثامن في إيراد هذا البرهان على وجه آخر. ويظن أن إيراده على ذلك الوجه، يوجب سقوط أكثر هذه الأسئلة عنه. ويبان أن ذلك الظن خطأ من الناس. من الناس من قال: إن هذه الأسئلة إنما توجهت، وهذه المضائق له من سبب، لأن التقدير هو تقدير كونه محتاجا إلى السبب، ثم يعود التقسيم الأول في سببه، والدور والتسلسل محالان، فوجب الانتهاء بالآخرة إلى وجود موجود غني عن السبب، وذلك هو الموجود الواجب لذاته، وهو المطلوب. فظهر بهذا الكلام: أنا إذا فسرنا الواجب والممكن بهذا التفسير، سقطت عنا تلك السؤالات، وصار الكلام في غاية الاختصار، وكان إيراد الحجة على هذا الوجه أولى. ولقائل أن يقول: إن المباحث العقلية، لا تختلف باختلاف الألفاظ والعبارات. فنقول: هب أنكم فسرتم الواجب لذاته بما يكون غنيا في وجوده عن السبب المنفصل، إلا أنه يقال: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن هذا الوجود الغني في وجوده عن السبب تكون ماهيته وحقيقته قابلة للعدم وللوجود قبولا على التساوي، إلا أنه ترجح وجوده على عدمه، لا لمرجح أصلا لا لذاته ولا لغيره؟ وأيضا: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن تلك الحقيقة، وإن كانت قابلة للعدم إلا أن الوجود بها أولى، فلاجل حصول هذه الأولوية يستغنى عن المؤثر المنفصل؟ فيثبت بما ذكرنا: أنه لا يمكن الجزم بوجود موجود لا تقبل حقيقته العدم البتة إلا بتقدير تلك المقدمات. واعلم أن المباحث اللفظية لا تدفع الحقائق العقلية، وإنما يكون تأثيرها في انتقال البحث من مقام إلى مقام آخر، وذلك قليل الفائدة.
Chapter Eight: On presenting this proof in another way. And it is thought that presenting it in that way causes the fall of most of these questions (objections) from it. And it is shown that that thought is a mistake of the people. Some people said: These questions only arose, and these difficulties only became necessary, because at the beginning of the discourse we defined the Necessary Existent in itself as: the existent whose essence is not at all receptive to non-existence. And we defined the Possible Existent in itself as: the existent whose essence is receptive to non-existence. And when we defined the Necessary and the Possible with these two definitions, these questions came, and these investigations arose. So the matter became serious, and the inquiry became constrained. And here is another way, closer to precision and farther from confusion than what preceded: that the definition of the Necessary in itself is that it is independent in its existence from the cause, and the definition of the Possible in itself is that it is in need in its existence of the cause. On this definition, the statement affirming the Necessary Existent in itself becomes among the most evident of goals and among the clearest of aims, and none of the mentioned questions attaches to it. For we say: There is no doubt that there is an existent in existence. That existent is either independent in its existence from the cause, or it is in need of the cause. If it is the first, then the statement affirming an existent that is Necessary Existent in itself is established. And if it is the second, then it must have a cause, because the supposition is that it is supposed to be in need of a cause. Then the first division returns concerning its cause, and circularity and infinite regress are impossible. So it becomes necessary to end ultimately at the existence of an existent independent of the cause, and that is the existent Necessary in itself, and that is the sought. So it became apparent from this speech: that when we defined the Necessary and the Possible with this definition, those questions fall away from us, and the discourse becomes extremely concise, and presenting the argument in this way is more appropriate. And someone may say: Rational investigations do not differ with differences of words and expressions. So we say: Suppose you defined the Necessary in itself as that which is independent in its existence from the separate cause. Nevertheless, it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that this existence, independent in its existence from the cause, has its quiddity and essence receptive to non-existence and existence with equal acceptance, except that its existence is preponderated over its non-existence, with no preponderator at all, neither from itself nor from another? And also: Why is it not permissible to say that that essence, even if it is receptive to non-existence, existence is more appropriate for it, and due to the occurrence of this appropriateness, it becomes independent of the separate efficient cause? It is established by what we mentioned: that it is not possible to be certain of the existence of an existent whose essence does not at all accept non-existence except by positing those premises. And know that verbal investigations do not repel rational realities; rather, their effect is only to transfer investigation from one station to another station, and that is of little benefit.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative formulation of the proof for the existence of a necessary being, intended to bypass objections that arose from an earlier definition. The author begins by recalling that previous definitions (necessary as that which cannot admit non-existence, possible as that which can admit non-existence) led to many difficulties. He then offers a new definition: the necessary is that which is independent in its existence from a cause (غني عن السبب), and the possible is that which is in need of a cause (محتاج إلى السبب). Using this definition, the proof becomes straightforward: any existent is either independent or dependent; if independent, it is the necessary; if dependent, it requires a cause, and that cause must itself be either independent or dependent; an infinite regress or circularity is impossible, so we must end with something independent—that is the necessary being. The author claims this avoids the previous objections. Then an objector raises a point: changing definitions does not change the rational issues. Even with this new definition, one could argue that the supposedly independent being might still have an essence that is equally receptive to existence and non-existence, and its existence might be preponderated without any preponderator, or that existence is simply more appropriate for it, making it independent of an external cause. The author responds that such verbal distinctions do not change realities; they only shift the discussion from one level to another with little benefit. The passage thus critiques the attempt to evade theological difficulties through redefinition, while still affirming the soundness of the causal proof.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود لذاته
ممكن الوجود لذاته
السبب
غني عن السبب
محتاج إلى السبب
الدور
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Changing definitions or wording can resolve metaphysical problems without addressing the underlying rational issues.
Correction: As the author states, 'verbal investigations do not repel rational realities'; redefinition only shifts the inquiry to another level.

Checkpoint
According to the alternative definition presented in this passage, what does it mean for something to be 'واجب الوجود لذاته' (necessary in itself)?
A) Its essence is not receptive to non-existence at all.
B) It is independent in its existence from a cause.
C) It is the first cause of everything.
D) It is eternal and unchanging.

Key Takeaway
The alternative causal proof does not escape the fundamental problem of explaining why the supposed necessary being has existence rather than non-existence on its own.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult theological question—like why God exists rather than not—are you tempted to quickly accept a redefinition that makes the problem seem to vanish? In your own life, where do you rely on words to mask unresolved doubts about your faith? This passage warns that verbal shifts don't change realities. Will you press deeper until your belief rests on truth, not on clever phrasing?
?
The objector challenges the alternative proof by asking why the independent being's existence might not simply be preponderated without any cause. Have you ever assumed that once you label something 'necessary' or 'self-sufficient', all deeper questions disappear? What if the very thing you call 'necessary' is still, at its core, fragile? How does this passage expose any such assumption in your own thinking about God?
?
The author concludes that verbal investigations only shift the discussion, yielding little benefit. Take a moment to examine your own study of aqeedah: are you more concerned with words and definitions, or with grasping the underlying rational realities that determine truth? What specific change will you make today to move from verbal comfort to genuine certainty?

The Proof of a Necessary Existent
Author argues for a necessary existent via impossibility of circularity and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress leads to the conclusion that existence must terminate in a being that is necessary in itself (wājib li-dhātihi).

Core Idea
Existence cannot go on forever — it must end in a self-sufficient source.

In this passage, the author presents a concise argument for the existence of a necessary existent. He begins by considering any existent that depends on a cause. If that cause in turn depends on another, we face an infinite regress or a circular chain, both of which are rationally impossible. Therefore, there must be an endpoint: a being whose existence is not dependent on any external cause. This is the necessary existent, the focus of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice how the author uses very concise language.
2
Identify the logical structure
Trace the conditional argument: if something is possible (contingent), it needs a cause; if that cause is also possible, we get circularity or regress; both are impossible; so there must be a necessary existent.
The key is that circularity and infinite regress are deemed impossible.
3
Understand the conciseness remark
The author notes that this formulation avoids many unnecessary questions. Reflect on why this might be so.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that the necessary existent is what is sought. Connect this to the overall goal of proving God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من سبب ، لأن التقدير هو تقدير كونه محتاجاً إلى السبب ، ثم يعود التقسيم الأول في سببه ، والدور والتسلسل محالان ، فوجب الانتهاء بالآخرة إلى وجود موجود غني عن السبب ، وذلك هو الموجود الواجب لذاته ، وهو المطلوب فظهر بهذا الكلام : أنا إذا فسرنا الواجب والممكن بهذا التفسير ، سقطت عنا تلك السؤالات ، وصار الكلام في غاية الاختصار ، وكان إيراد الحجة على هذا الوجه أولى. ولقائل أن يقول : إن المباحث العقلية ، لا تختلف باختلاف الألفاظ والعبارات فنقول : هب أنكم فسرتم الواجب لذاته بما يكون غنيا في وجوده عن السبب المنفصل ، إلا أنه يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن هذا الوجود الغني في وجوده عن السبب تكون ماهيته وحقيقته قابلة للعدم وللوجود قبولا على التساوي ، إلا أنه ترجح وجوده على عدمه، لا لمرجح أصلا لا لذاته ولا لغيره ؟ وأيضا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن تلك الحقيقة ، وإن كانت قابلة للعدم إلا أن الوجود بها أولى ، فلاجل حصول هذه الأولوية يستغنى عن المؤثر المنفصل ؟ فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه لا يمكن الجزم بوجود موجود لا تقبل حقيقته العدم البتة إلا بتقدير تلك المقدمات واعلم أن المباحث اللفظية لا تدفع الحقائق العقلية ، وإنما يكون تأثيرها في انتقال البحث من مقام إلى مقام آخر ، وذلك قليل الفائدة
From a cause, because the estimation is the estimation of it being in need of a cause, then the first division returns concerning its cause, and circularity and infinite regress are impossible. So it is necessary to terminate at the existence of an existent that is independent of a cause, and that is the existent necessary in itself (al-wājib li-dhātihi), and that is what is sought. So it appears from this speech: if we interpret the necessary (al-wājib) and the possible (al-mumkin) with this interpretation, those questions fall away from us, and the speech becomes extremely concise, and presenting the argument in this manner is more appropriate. And someone may say: intellectual investigations do not differ with differences of words and expressions. So we say: grant that you have interpreted the necessary in itself as that which is independent in its existence from the separate cause, but it is said: why is it not permissible to say that this existence, which is independent in its existence from a cause, its quiddity and reality are receptive to non-existence and existence with equal receptivity, but its existence outweighs its non-existence, not by any determinant (murjīḥ) at all, neither by itself nor by another? And also: why is it not permissible to say that that reality, although receptive to non-existence, existence is more fitting to it, and due to the occurrence of this fittingness it becomes independent of the separate efficient cause? So it is established by what we mentioned that it is impossible to be certain of the existence of an existent whose reality absolutely does not receive non-existence except by supposing those premises. And know that verbal investigations do not refute intellectual realities; rather their effect is only in transferring the investigation from one station to another, and that is of little benefit.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a proof and defense of the existence of a necessary existent (al-wājib li-dhātihi). The author first argues that if we consider any existent, its existence may be through a cause, leading to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both of which are impossible. Therefore existence must terminate in a being that is independent of any cause—the necessary existent. The author then notes that this concise formulation avoids many questions. An objection is raised: intellectual truths are not affected by verbal distinctions; even if we define the necessary as independent of a cause, why can't an essence be equally receptive to existence and non-existence yet exist without a determinant? Another objection: why can't existence be more fitting to an essence, making it independent of an external cause? The author responds that these objections fail because they presuppose the same premises (such as the impossibility of a thing existing without a determinant). Finally, the author warns that mere verbal debates do not overturn rational truths; they only shift the discussion without real benefit.

Key Terms
سبب (cause)
دور (circularity)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
واجب لذاته (necessary in itself)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an infinite regress of causes is possible and does not lead to a necessary existent.
Correction: The author argues that infinite regress is rationally impossible because it would never allow the chain to exist as a whole; thus existence must terminate in a necessary existent.

Checkpoint

A) Because circularity and infinite regress are impossible.
B) Because everything has a cause.
C) Because possibilities are limited.
D) Because the author says so.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of circularity and infinite regress forces the conclusion that existence culminates in a necessary existent independent of any cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you placing your trust in a chain that could collapse? This lesson forces you to see that only a self-sufficient being can be truly reliable. Where is your bedrock?
?
The argument shows that an infinite regress of causes is impossible. In your own life, do you live as if there is always another chance, another cause, another tomorrow? What would change if you truly accepted that your existence must terminate in a necessary being—that you cannot postpone accountability indefinitely?
?
The author says this interpretation makes 'those questions fall away.' What questions about your own existence—about purpose, meaning, and ultimate dependence—does this proof answer? Are you willing to let go of the comfortable assumption that things just are, and admit that you are contingent on a necessary being who is the source of all existence?

Responding to Objections about Possibility and Causality
Author replies to two objections about a possible essence existing without a determinant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the proof of the necessary existent and the author's responses, particularly regarding the possibility of an essence existing without a determinant.

Core Idea
Mere possibility does not negate the need for a cause — an equal possibility requires a determinant.

After presenting the initial proof, the author anticipates two objections. The first objection questions why an essence that is equally receptive to existence and non-existence cannot exist without a determinant. The second objection suggests that existence might be more fitting to the essence, eliminating the need for an external cause. The author responds by showing that these objections fail because they still rely on the same rational premises. He also warns that verbal debates cannot overturn intellectual realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections
Identify the two objections presented by the hypothetical objector. Note that they challenge the proof by suggesting an essence could exist without a cause.
The first objection: equal possibility without a determinant. The second: existence is more fitting.
2
Analyze the author's response
The author says that these objections still presuppose the same premises, like the need for a determinant. Reflect on why they fail.
The author implies that the objector is still assuming the necessity of a cause in some form.
3
Understand the final warning
The author states that verbal investigations do not refute intellectual realities; they only shift the discussion. Consider the significance of this remark.
4
Connect back to the proof
Realize that the objections do not undermine the original proof. The necessary existent remains established.

Refutation of Self-Causation
Refutation of self-causation with three arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a thing cannot be the cause of itself, as proven through three logical arguments.

Core Idea
A thing cannot be its own cause without contradiction.

The proof that the aggregate of all possible beings must have an external cause begins by eliminating the possibility that it is self-caused. The author presents three crisp arguments demonstrating the impossibility of self-causation. These arguments rely on the concepts of possibility, need, and relation.

Guided Path
1
Read the three arguments
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three numbered arguments against self-causation.
The arguments are introduced with 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'.
2
Understand the first argument
The first argument equates self-causation with independence, but the aggregate is possible and therefore depends on a cause, contradicting independence.
This argument refers to previous premises.
3
Understand the second argument
The second argument shows that self-causation would require the same thing to be both needy and independent under one consideration, a contradiction.
This is a classic contradiction in terms.
4
Understand the third argument
The third argument argues that dependence is a relation requiring two distinct things, so a thing cannot depend on itself.
Relational nature of dependence.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this refutation is crucial: if self-causation is impossible, the cause of the aggregate must be external.
This sets up the next division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من وجوه : أما القسم الأول : وهو أن يقال إن ذلك المجموع علة لنفسه ، فهذا الأول : إنه لا معنى لقولنا إنه علة لوجود نفسه ، إلا أنه غير محتاج إلى الغير ، وقد دللنا على أن ذلك المجموع ممكن لذاته، فيرجع حاصل هذا الكلام إلى أن الممكن غني عن السبب ، فيكون هذا رجوعا إلى المقدمات السالفة ، من أن الممكنات ، هل تتوقف على السبب أم لا ؟ ونحن إنما نتكلم في هذا المقام بعد أثبات أن الممكن لا بد له من سبب . الثاني : إن المحتاج إلى الشيء ممكن بالنسبة إلى المحتاج إليه ، والمحتاج إليه غني بالنسبة إلى المحتاج فلو كان الشيء الواحد علة لنفسه لزم كون الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد محتاجا وغنيا ، وذلك يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين الثالث : إن المعلول مفتقر إلى العلة ، فلو كان الشيء الواحد علة لنفسه لزم كونه مفتقراً إلى نفسه. والافتقار إلى الشيء نسبة ، والنسبة لا تحصل إلا بين الأمرين ، فأما الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد ، فيمتنع كونه منسوبا إلى نفسه . وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يقال : علة ذلك المجموع فرد من أفراد ذلك المجموع ، فهذا أيضا باطل ، لأن كل ما كان علة للمجموع، وجب كونه علة لجميع آحاد ذلك المجموع ، ولا شك أن أحد آحاد ذلك المجموع ذلك الواحد ، الذي فرض كونه علة لذلك المجموع ، فحينئذ يلزم في ذلك الواحد كونه علة لنفسه ، وقد بينا أن ذلك محال ، ويلزم منه أيضاً أن يكون علة لعلة نفسه وذلك يوجب الدور ، وقد بينا أنه محال ، فثبت أن هذا القسم أيضا باطل ، ولما بطل هذان القسمان ، ثبت أن علة ذلك المجموع يجب أن يكون أمرا خارجاً عن ذلك المجموع ، والخارج عن مجموع الممكنات لا يكون ممكنا ، والموجود الذي لا يكون ممكنا لذاته يكون واجبا لذاته . ، فثبت بهذا البرهان وجوب انتهاء جميع الممكنات في سلسلة الحاجة إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته، وهو المطلوب ، هذا تمام تقرير هذا البرهان .
From aspects: As for the first division: that it is said that that aggregate is a cause for itself, so this [has] the first [argument]: that there is no meaning to our saying that it is a cause for its own existence except that it is not needful of the other, and we have proven that that aggregate is possible in itself, so the gist of this speech reduces to that the possible is independent of the cause, so this is a return to the previous premises, concerning whether possibles depend on a cause or not? And we only speak in this station after establishing that the possible must have a cause. The second [argument]: that that which needs a thing is possible relative to that which is needed, and that which is needed is independent relative to the needy; so if the one thing were a cause for itself, it would necessitate that the one thing under one consideration be both needy and independent, and that entails combining two contradictories. The third [argument]: that the effect is dependent on the cause, so if the one thing were a cause for itself, it would necessitate its being dependent on itself. And dependence on a thing is a relation, and a relation cannot occur except between two things; as for one thing under one consideration, it is impossible that it be related to itself. And as for the second division: that it is said: the cause of that aggregate is an individual from among the individuals of that aggregate, then this is also false, because everything that is a cause for the aggregate must be a cause for all the individuals of that aggregate, and there is no doubt that one of the individuals of that aggregate is that very one which is supposed to be the cause for that aggregate; therefore it necessarily follows concerning that one that it be a cause for itself, and we have shown that that is impossible. And it also follows from it that it be a cause for its own cause, and that entails circularity, and we have shown that it is impossible. So it is established that this division is also false. And when these two divisions are false, it is established that the cause of that aggregate must be a thing external to that aggregate, and the external to the aggregate of possibles cannot be possible, and the existent that is not possible in itself is necessary in itself. So it is established by this proof the necessity of all possibles terminating in the chain of need to an existent necessary of existence in itself, and that is the desired. This is the complete formulation of this proof.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a proof (برهان) demonstrating the necessity of a first cause (واجب الوجود). The author refutes two possible scenarios for the causation of the aggregate of all possible beings (مجموع الممكنات). First, he considers the claim that the aggregate is a cause of itself. He gives three arguments against this: (1) Saying that something is a cause of its own existence simply means it is independent of others, but the aggregate is possible and therefore dependent, so claiming it is self-caused contradicts its possibility. (2) If a thing were its own cause, it would be both needful (as an effect) and independent (as a cause) under the same consideration, which is a contradiction. (3) An effect depends on its cause; if a thing were its own cause, it would depend on itself, but dependence is a relation requiring two distinct relata, which is impossible for a single thing. Second, he considers the claim that a member of the aggregate causes the whole aggregate. This also fails because if a member is a cause of the aggregate, it must be a cause of all its parts, including itself, leading it to be its own cause (already refuted) and also a cause of its own cause, which results in circular causation (دور). Both divisions being false, the cause must be external to the aggregate. Whatever is external to the aggregate of possibles cannot itself be possible, so it must be necessary in itself (واجب الوجود). Thus the proof establishes that all possible beings in the chain of need terminate in a necessary being.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
ممكن
نسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible for something to be self-sufficient and thus its own cause.
Correction: But if something is possible (contingent), it is not self-sufficient; it requires an external cause. The aggregate of all possibles is itself possible, so it cannot be self-caused.

Checkpoint
Why does the third argument consider self-causation impossible?
A) Because a cause must be external to its effect.
B) Because dependence is a relation that requires two distinct things.
C) Because the aggregate is not one thing.
D) Because we already proved that possibles need a cause.

Key Takeaway
Self-causation is impossible because it leads to logical contradictions regarding independence, duality of attributes, and the relational nature of dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself living as if you are self-sufficient, independent of Allah? How does the first argument’s assertion that 'the possible must have a cause' challenge that illusion right now?
?
The second argument says the same thing cannot be both needy and independent under one consideration. In what areas of your life are you trying to claim independence while being deeply needy of Allah? Will you confess that need today?
?
The third argument shows that dependence requires two distinct entities. Who or what are you relying on that is not Allah? If you are dependent on anything else, doesn't it need a cause itself? Will you turn your dependence entirely to the Necessary Being who alone has no cause?
