Refutation of Member-Causation and Proof of the Necessary Being
Refutation of member-causation and conclusion: external necessary being

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the cause of the aggregate of possibles cannot be a member and how this leads to the conclusion that the cause must be an external necessary being.

Core Idea
The cause cannot be part of the aggregate; it must be a necessary being outside it.

Having ruled out self-causation, the author now considers whether a single member of the aggregate could be the cause of the whole. This also fails because it leads to self-causation and circular causation. With both possibilities refuted, the only remaining option is an external cause, which must be necessary in itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the second division
Read the passage that presents the claim that a member of the aggregate causes the aggregate, and its refutation.
The refutation shows two consequences: self-causation and circularity.
2
Understand the self-causation consequence
If a member causes the aggregate, it must cause all individuals, including itself, making it self-caused, which is already proven impossible.
This is a direct reduction to the first division.
3
Understand the circularity consequence
The member would also be a cause of its own cause, leading to circular causation (دور), which is impossible.
Circular causation is logically invalid.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since both divisions are false, the cause must be external to the aggregate, and what is external to possibles cannot be possible; it must be necessary in itself.
This establishes the existence of a necessary being.
5
Reflect on the proof's structure
Notice how the proof systematically eliminates internal causes to necessitate an external cause.
This is a classic argument for God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من وجوه : أما القسم الأول : وهو أن يقال إن ذلك المجموع علة لنفسه ، فهذا الأول : إنه لا معنى لقولنا إنه علة لوجود نفسه ، إلا أنه غير محتاج إلى الغير ، وقد دللنا على أن ذلك المجموع ممكن لذاته، فيرجع حاصل هذا الكلام إلى أن الممكن غني عن السبب ، فيكون هذا رجوعا إلى المقدمات السالفة ، من أن الممكنات ، هل تتوقف على السبب أم لا ؟ ونحن إنما نتكلم في هذا المقام بعد أثبات أن الممكن لا بد له من سبب . الثاني : إن المحتاج إلى الشيء ممكن بالنسبة إلى المحتاج إليه ، والمحتاج إليه غني بالنسبة إلى المحتاج فلو كان الشيء الواحد علة لنفسه لزم كون الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد محتاجا وغنيا ، وذلك يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين الثالث : إن المعلول مفتقر إلى العلة ، فلو كان الشيء الواحد علة لنفسه لزم كونه مفتقراً إلى نفسه. والافتقار إلى الشيء نسبة ، والنسبة لا تحصل إلا بين الأمرين ، فأما الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد ، فيمتنع كونه منسوبا إلى نفسه . وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يقال : علة ذلك المجموع فرد من أفراد ذلك المجموع ، فهذا أيضا باطل ، لأن كل ما كان علة للمجموع، وجب كونه علة لجميع آحاد ذلك المجموع ، ولا شك أن أحد آحاد ذلك المجموع ذلك الواحد ، الذي فرض كونه علة لذلك المجموع ، فحينئذ يلزم في ذلك الواحد كونه علة لنفسه ، وقد بينا أن ذلك محال ، ويلزم منه أيضاً أن يكون علة لعلة نفسه وذلك يوجب الدور ، وقد بينا أنه محال ، فثبت أن هذا القسم أيضا باطل ، ولما بطل هذان القسمان ، ثبت أن علة ذلك المجموع يجب أن يكون أمرا خارجاً عن ذلك المجموع ، والخارج عن مجموع الممكنات لا يكون ممكنا ، والموجود الذي لا يكون ممكنا لذاته يكون واجبا لذاته . ، فثبت بهذا البرهان وجوب انتهاء جميع الممكنات في سلسلة الحاجة إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته، وهو المطلوب ، هذا تمام تقرير هذا البرهان .
From aspects: As for the first division: that it is said that that aggregate is a cause for itself, so this [has] the first [argument]: that there is no meaning to our saying that it is a cause for its own existence except that it is not needful of the other, and we have proven that that aggregate is possible in itself, so the gist of this speech reduces to that the possible is independent of the cause, so this is a return to the previous premises, concerning whether possibles depend on a cause or not? And we only speak in this station after establishing that the possible must have a cause. The second [argument]: that that which needs a thing is possible relative to that which is needed, and that which is needed is independent relative to the needy; so if the one thing were a cause for itself, it would necessitate that the one thing under one consideration be both needy and independent, and that entails combining two contradictories. The third [argument]: that the effect is dependent on the cause, so if the one thing were a cause for itself, it would necessitate its being dependent on itself. And dependence on a thing is a relation, and a relation cannot occur except between two things; as for one thing under one consideration, it is impossible that it be related to itself. And as for the second division: that it is said: the cause of that aggregate is an individual from among the individuals of that aggregate, then this is also false, because everything that is a cause for the aggregate must be a cause for all the individuals of that aggregate, and there is no doubt that one of the individuals of that aggregate is that very one which is supposed to be the cause for that aggregate; therefore it necessarily follows concerning that one that it be a cause for itself, and we have shown that that is impossible. And it also follows from it that it be a cause for its own cause, and that entails circularity, and we have shown that it is impossible. So it is established that this division is also false. And when these two divisions are false, it is established that the cause of that aggregate must be a thing external to that aggregate, and the external to the aggregate of possibles cannot be possible, and the existent that is not possible in itself is necessary in itself. So it is established by this proof the necessity of all possibles terminating in the chain of need to an existent necessary of existence in itself, and that is the desired. This is the complete formulation of this proof.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a proof (برهان) demonstrating the necessity of a first cause (واجب الوجود). The author refutes two possible scenarios for the causation of the aggregate of all possible beings (مجموع الممكنات). First, he considers the claim that the aggregate is a cause of itself. He gives three arguments against this: (1) Saying that something is a cause of its own existence simply means it is independent of others, but the aggregate is possible and therefore dependent, so claiming it is self-caused contradicts its possibility. (2) If a thing were its own cause, it would be both needful (as an effect) and independent (as a cause) under the same consideration, which is a contradiction. (3) An effect depends on its cause; if a thing were its own cause, it would depend on itself, but dependence is a relation requiring two distinct relata, which is impossible for a single thing. Second, he considers the claim that a member of the aggregate causes the whole aggregate. This also fails because if a member is a cause of the aggregate, it must be a cause of all its parts, including itself, leading it to be its own cause (already refuted) and also a cause of its own cause, which results in circular causation (دور). Both divisions being false, the cause must be external to the aggregate. Whatever is external to the aggregate of possibles cannot itself be possible, so it must be necessary in itself (واجب الوجود). Thus the proof establishes that all possible beings in the chain of need terminate in a necessary being.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
ممكن
واجب
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof might itself be circular because it assumes the necessity of a cause.
Correction: The proof does not assume the necessity of a cause; it refutes internal causation and then deduces an external cause. Circularity is explicitly ruled out.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why must the cause of the aggregate be external?
A) Because the aggregate is self-caused.
B) Because neither self-causation nor member-causation is possible.
C) Because the aggregate has no members.
D) Because the aggregate is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The only consistent cause for the aggregate of possible beings is an external necessary being, thus proving God's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson proves that nothing within creation can be the ultimate cause of all things. Is there any created thing—your career, relationships, or even your own self—that you treat as self-sufficient or as the source of your meaning? How can you realign your heart to seek the Necessary Being alone?
?
The argument shows that a part cannot cause the whole. In your spiritual life, are you guilty of making a single part of your deen (like salah or charity) the whole of your faith? How can you ensure your entire being is oriented toward Allah, the external cause, rather than relying on a subset of practices?

The Inevitable Conclusion: All Contingents End in a Necessary Being
The author concludes the proof of the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof of the Necessary Being concludes by establishing that the cause of all contingent things must be an external, self-necessary existent.

Core Idea
Contingents cannot cause themselves; only a Necessary Being can.

The author has been demonstrating that an infinite regress of causes is impossible. After refuting two possible false divisions, he now arrives at the inevitable conclusion: there must be a cause outside the aggregate of all contingent things, and that cause cannot itself be contingent — it must be necessary in itself. This passage seals the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical flow: falsehood of two divisions leads to establishment of external cause.
The passage uses 'ثبت' (it is established) to indicate a logical conclusion.
2
Identify the logical structure
The passage presents a conditional chain: if two divisions are false, then the cause is external; if external, it cannot be possible; therefore it is necessary.
The proof is a syllogism.
3
Understand the key terms
Focus on 'علة' (cause), 'مجموع الممكنات' (aggregate of possibles), and 'واجب الوجود' (necessary of existence).
These terms are central to the proof.
4

The final sentence declares that the proof establishes the termination of all possibles in the chain of need to a self-necessary existent.
This is the desired conclusion (المطلوب).

Al-Razi Refutes Two Objections to the Regress Proof
Al-Razi refutes two objections against the first proof for the impossibility of regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi refutes two objections against the impossibility of an infinite regress: the aggregate objection and the accident-causality objection.

Core Idea
Neither the whole chain nor its relations can explain infinite regress without circularity or priority paradox.

Al-Razi has presented a proof that an infinite regress of causes is impossible. Now he anticipates two objections: one suggesting the entire chain can be explained by the aggregate of its links, another suggesting the causal relations themselves are the cause. In this passage, he dismantles both objections, showing they lead to logical absurdities. The first collapses into self-causation, the second into a priority contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the argument. Notice how Al-Razi systematically refutes each objection.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two objections
The passage starts with 'والأول باطل' and 'والثاني أيضاً باطل', indicating two objections. Identify what each objection proposes as an explanation for the infinite regress.
The first objection involves the aggregate of individuals; the second involves causal dependencies.
3
Understand the refutations
For each objection, Al-Razi provides a reason why it fails. The first leads to self-causation; the second to a priority violation between substance and accident. Grasp these logical principles.
Self-causation (تعليل الشيء بنفسه) and priority of substance over accident are key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأول باطل لأن مجموع تلك الآحاد هو عين ذلك المجموع ، فتعليل ذلك المجموع بتلك الأحاد يقتضي تعليل الشيء بنفسه ، وهو محال . والثاني أيضاً باطل ، لأن تلك الاستنادات أحوال عارضة لتلك الآحاد ، ولو جعلنا هذه الاستنادات علل لتلك الآحاد ، لزم جعل العوارض عللا للمعروضات ، وذلك محال ، لأن المعروض متقدم بالرتبة على العارض ، فلو جعلنا العارض علة للمعروض ، لزم تقدم كل واحد منها على الآخر وهو محال ، فيثبت سقوط هذا السؤال ، وههنا آخر الكلام في تقرير هذا البرهان والله ولي الرحمة والغفران
The first [possibility] is invalid because the aggregate of those individuals is exactly that aggregate; so making that aggregate depend on those individuals entails causing a thing by itself, and that is impossible. The second [possibility] is also invalid because those dependencies are accidental states ('awāriḍ) of those individuals, and if we make these dependencies causes for those individuals, that would make accidents causes for the substances, and that is impossible because the substance is prior in rank to the accident; so if we make the accident a cause for the substance, it would entail each of them being prior to the other, and that is impossible. So it is established that this objection falls away, and here ends the discussion in establishing this proof. And Allah is the Guardian of mercy and forgiveness.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage concludes the first proof for the impossibility of an infinite regress by refuting two possible objections. The author, likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, first considers the possibility that the entire chain of causes and effects could be explained by the aggregate of all individual links. He argues that this is circular because the aggregate is nothing but the sum of those individuals; explaining the aggregate by the individuals is explaining a thing by itself, which is impossible. Next, he considers the possibility that the causal dependency relationships (al-istinādāt) among the links are themselves the causes of the individuals. He refutes this by noting that these dependencies are accidental states ('awāriḍ) that inhere in the individuals (the substances). Making an accident the cause of its substance would violate the principle that the substance is ontologically prior to the accident. If an accident were the cause of its substance, then the accident would have to exist before the substance (since cause precedes effect), but the accident depends on the substance for its existence (since accidents inhere in substances). This would require each to be prior to the other, which is impossible. Thus both objections are invalid, and the first proof stands. The passage ends with a pious phrase.

Key Terms
الآحاد
المجموع
الاستنادات
أحوال عارضة
العوارض
المعروضات
تعليل الشيء بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the entire chain of causes can be explained by the sum of its parts without circularity.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the aggregate of parts is identical to the whole, so explaining the whole by the parts is explaining a thing by itself, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it invalid to make the aggregate of individuals the cause of the entire chain?
A) Because the aggregate is prior to the individuals.
B) Because it would entail causing a thing by itself.
C) Because the individuals are impossible.
D) Because the aggregate is not a real entity.

Key Takeaway
Both objections are invalid because they lead to logical contradictions: self-causation and priority paradox, thus the first regress proof stands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Think about the dependencies in your life—relationships, habits, or emotions that you treat as ultimate causes. Are any of them actually accidents that derive from a deeper source? How can you realign your priorities to reflect the true ontological order?
?
Al-Razi shows that making an accident a cause of its substance leads to impossibility. In your spiritual journey, do you sometimes prioritize secondary rituals over the essence of sincere faith? What concrete step will you take today to ensure your worship flows from the heart?

The Third Proof: Infinite Regress Necessitates an External Cause
Al-Razi proves that an infinite regress of causes leads to impossible outcomes, requiring an external necessary cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof against infinite regress, which shows that an infinite chain of possible beings requires a Necessary Being distinct from them.

Core Idea
An infinite chain of possibles cannot exist without an external Necessary Cause.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a third argument to demonstrate that an infinite regress of causes is impossible. In this passage, he analyzes what would exist in such an infinite chain — individual essences and their relationships — and shows that both are possible in existence, therefore requiring an external effecting agent that is necessary in essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Al-Razi separates the discussion into two parts: the individuals and their relations.
2
Identify the two components of the chain
The passage examines two things in the infinite chain: the individual essences (آحاد الذوات) themselves, and the relations (تعلق بعضها ببعض) between them. Both are analyzed separately.
The relations are called 'aḥwāl i'tibāriyya (mental/relational states).
3
Understand why both are possible
The individuals are possible because they could be otherwise. The relations are even more possible because they depend on the individuals. Therefore everything in the chain is possible.
This step is crucial for the conclusion.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since the whole chain is possible, it needs an external agent. That agent cannot be part of the chain (otherwise it would be possible too). Hence it must be necessary in essence (wājib li-dhātihi).
This proof complements the first two proofs by focusing on the impossibility of an infinite chain of possibles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثالث : إذا فرضنا الأسباب والمسببات متسلسلة ، إلى غير النهاية ، فالموجود هناك ليس إلا آحاد تلك الذوات ، وإلا كون بعضها متعلقا بالبعض ، أما آحاد الذوات ، فهي بأسرها ممكنة الوجود ، فلو دخلت في الوجود من غير مؤثر يؤثر فيها ، كان هذا قولا بوقوع الممكن لا لمؤثر ، وهو محال ، وأما تعلق بعضها بالبعض فهي أحوال اعتبارية عارضة لتلك الذوات ، والعارض للشيء مفتقر إلى المعروض ، ومعروضات هذه الأحوال ليست إلا تلك الأحاد ، وهي بأسرها ممكنة، فهذه الأحوال الاعتبارية والإضافية مفتقرة إلى أمور هي ممكنة الوجود ، والمفتقر إلى الممكن أولى بالامكان ، فهذه الأحوال الاعتبارية بأسرها ممكنة الوجود ، إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إن تلك الأحاد بأسرها ممكنة الموجود لذواتها ، واستناد بعضها إلى بعض أحوال اعتبارية عارضة لتلك الأحاد بأسرها أيضاً ممكنة ، وهي الوجود فجملة هذه الموجودات ممكنة الوجود في ذواتها ، وممكنة الوجود في جميع اعتباراتها ، والممكن لا بد له من مؤثر يؤثر فيه ، ويجب كون ذلك المؤثر مغايرا لها، على ما ثبت أن كل ممكن فلا بد له من مؤثر فيثبت أن جملة هذه الممكنات مفتقرة إلى مؤثر مغاير لها ، والمغاير لكل الممكنات لا يكون ممكنا ، فثبت افتقار جملة الممكنات إلى موجود يؤثر فيها ويكون ذلك مغايرا لمجموعها ، ولكل واحد من أحادها ، وذلك هو الموجود الواجب لذاته ، وهو المطلوب
The Third Proof: If we suppose causes and effects to be arranged in a chain without end, then what exists there is nothing but the individuals of those essences, and also the fact that some of them are related to others. As for the individuals of the essences, they are all possible in existence. So if they came into existence without an effecting agent effecting them, this would be a statement of the occurrence of the possible without an effecting agent, and that is impossible. As for the relation of some of them to others, these are mental and relational states ('aḥwāl i'tibāriyya) that are accidental to those essences. And that which is accidental to a thing is dependent on the substratum. And the substrata of these states are nothing but those individuals, and they are all possible. So these mental and relational states are dependent on things that are possible in existence, and that which depends on the possible is even more deserving of possibility. Thus these mental states are all possible in existence. Once you know this, we say: Those individuals are all possible in existence by themselves, and the dependence of some upon others are mental states accidental to those individuals, which themselves are also possible, and they are existence. So the totality of these existents is possible in existence in their essences and possible in existence in all their relations. And the possible must have an effecting agent affecting it, and it is necessary that this effecting agent be distinct from it, based on what has been established: that every possible must have an effecting agent. So it is established that the totality of these possibles is dependent on an effecting agent distinct from them. And that which is distinct from all possibles cannot be possible. So it is established that the totality of possibles is dependent on an existent that effects them, and that is distinct from their entirety and from every one of their individuals. And that is the existent necessary in essence, and it is what is sought.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This lesson presents the third proof against infinite regress. The argument examines what exists in an assumed infinite chain: only the individual essences themselves and the relationships (tacalluq) between them. First, the individual essences are all possible in existence. If they came to be without a cause (mu'aththir), that would mean a possible occurs without an agent, which is impossible. Second, the relationships between them are merely mental/relational states ('aḥwāl i'tibāriyya) that inhere in the individuals. These states depend on the individuals (the substrata). Since the individuals are possible, the states are even more possible (because they depend on possibles). Thus everything in the chain—both the individuals and their relations—is possible. Therefore, the entire totality of these possible things requires an effecting agent that brings them into existence. This agent must be distinct from them, because if it were part of the chain it would be possible and need another agent. An agent distinct from all possibles cannot itself be possible (otherwise it would be among them). So it is necessary in essence (wājib li-dhātihi). Thus the infinite chain of possibles necessarily ends in a Necessary Being.

Key Terms

أحوال اعتبارية
عارضة
المعروض
ممكن الوجود
مؤثر
مغاير
واجب لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the relationships among the causes could themselves serve as the cause of the chain's existence.
Correction: The passage shows that these relations are merely accidental states dependent on the individuals. Since the individuals are possible, the relations are even more possible and cannot be the ultimate cause.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, why are the relational states (ta'alluq) among individuals not sufficient to explain the existence of the chain?
A) Because they are mental and accidental, hence dependent on the individuals which are possible.
B) Because they are necessary in themselves.
C) Because they are part of the chain and thus eternal.
D) Because they are prior to the individuals.

Key Takeaway
The third proof establishes that an infinite regress of possible beings is impossible without a Necessary Being distinct from the chain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that every possible being—including you—depends on a Necessary Being distinct from all creation. In your daily life, do you truly live as if your existence is held by Him alone, or do you feel self-sufficient? What would change if you internalized this dependence?
?
Al-Razi says the relations between causes are merely accidental states that depend on the individuals. Think of the relationships you rely on—friends, family, career. Are they ultimately stable, or are they also contingent? How does this challenge your sense of security?
?
The proof ends with 'that is the existent necessary in essence, and it is what is sought.' Have you ever earnestly sought that Being beyond all causes? What distracts you from seeking Him, and what step can you take today to turn your attention to the Necessary One?

Proof from the Contingency of Individual Essences and Their Relations
A proof that all possible beings demand a necessary cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contingency of individual essences and their relational states proves the existence of a necessary being.

Core Idea
Everything possible, including relations, points to a necessary cause distinct from them.

In this passage, the author presents a sophisticated argument for the existence of Allah based on the very nature of contingent beings. He shows that not only individual essences but also their interrelations are contingent, and thus require an external cause. This cause cannot itself be contingent, leading to the affirmation of a Necessary Existent. Let's unpack the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise: contingency of individuals
Recognize that all individual essences are possible in themselves and cannot come into existence without a cause.
The author states this clearly at the beginning.
2
Analyze the contingency of relations
Relations are accidental states that depend on possible substrates, making them also possible.
This extends the contingency to all aspects of existence.
3
Combine to show total contingency
The totality of existents—both essences and relations—is contingent in every respect.
No part of the collection escapes contingency.
4
Conclude the necessity of a cause distinct from all possibles
Since every possible needs an effecting cause, the totality requires a cause that is other than itself and cannot be possible, thus necessary.
This cause is what we call Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النهاية ، فالموجود هناك ليس إلا آحاد تلك الذوات ، وإلا كون بعضها متعلقا بالبعض ، أما آحاد الذوات ، فهي بأسرها ممكنة الوجود ، فلو دخلت في الوجود من غير مؤثر يؤثر فيها ، كان هذا قولا بوقوع الممكن ( لا ) لمؤثر ، وهو محال ، وأما تعلق بعضها بالبعض فهي أحوال اعتبارية عارضة لتلك الذوات ، والعارض للشيء مفتقر إلى المعروض ، ومعروضات هذه الأحوال ليست إلا تلك الأحاد ، وهي بأسرها ممكنة، فهذه الأحوال الاعتبارية والإضافية مفتقرة إلى أمور هي ممكنة الوجود ، والمفتقر إلى الممكن أولى بالامكان ، فهذه الأحوال الاعتبارية بأسرها ممكنة الوجود ، إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إن تلك الأحاد بأسرها ممكنة الموجود لذواتها ، واستناد بعضها إلى بعض أحوال اعتبارية عارضة لتلك الأحاد بأسرها أيضاً ممكنة ، وهي ( الوجود ) (٢) فجملة هذه الموجودات ممكنة الوجود في ذواتها ، وممكنة الوجود في جميع اعتباراتها ، والممكن لا بد له من مؤثر ( يؤثر فيه ، ويجب كون ذلك المؤثر مغايرا لها، على ما ثبت أن كل ممكن فلا بد له من مؤثر )) فيثبت أن جملة هذه الممكنات مفتقرة إلى مؤثر ( مغاير لها ، والمغاير لكل الممكنات لا يكون ممكنا ، فثبت افتقار جملة الممكنات إلى ( (٤) موجود يؤثر فيها ويكون (٥) ذلك مغايرا لمجموعها ، ولكل واحد من أحادها ، وذلك هو الموجود الواجب لذاته ، وهو المطلوب
The end: The existent there is nothing but the individuals of those essences, and otherwise some of them being related to others. As for the individuals of the essences, they are all possible of existence. If they came into existence without an effecting cause affecting them, this would be a statement that the possible occurs without an effecting cause, which is impossible. As for the relation of some of them to others, they are accidental states attaching to those essences, and the accidental upon a thing is dependent on the substrate. The substrates of these states are nothing but those individuals, and they are all possible. So these accidental and relative states are dependent on things that are possible of existence, and that which is dependent on the possible is more deserving of possibility. Thus these accidental states are all possible of existence. When you know this, we say: Those individuals are all possible of existence in themselves, and the dependence of some of them upon others are accidental states attaching to those individuals, all of them also possible. So the totality of these existents is possible of existence in themselves and possible of existence in all their considerations. The possible necessarily requires an effecting cause that affects it, and it is necessary that that effecting cause be other than them, as it is established that every possible has an effecting cause. Thus it is established that the totality of these possibles is dependent on an effecting cause other than them, and that which is other than all possibles cannot be possible. So it is established that the totality of possibles is dependent on an existent that affects them, and that is other than the whole and each of its individuals. That is the existent necessary by itself, which is what is sought.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of a necessary being (wajib al-wujud) based on the contingency of all individual essences and their relations. The author begins by stating that the existent things we observe are nothing but individual essences and their relations. He argues that these individual essences are all possible in themselves—they could exist or not exist. If they came into existence without any external cause effecting them, that would be tantamount to claiming that a possible thing came into being without a cause, which is absurd. Then he turns to the relations (such as dependence of one on another). These relations are accidental states that attach to the individual essences. Every accident depends on its substrate, and the substrates here are the individual possibles. Since the substrates are possible, the accidents depending on them are even more deserving of possibility. Therefore, all these accidental states are also possible. Consequently, the entire collection of existents—both the individual essences and their relations—is possible in every respect. Now, it is established that every possible thing requires an effecting cause (mu'aththir) that brings it into existence. That cause must be distinct from the effect. So the totality of possible beings requires a cause that is other than them. That cause cannot itself be possible, because if it were possible, it would be part of the collection and would need another cause, leading to infinite regress. Therefore, the cause must be necessary in itself (wajib li-dhatihi). That is the desired conclusion.

Misconception: The cause could be one of the possible beings.
Correction: The cause must be other than all possibles because if it were possible, it would itself need a cause, leading to regress.

C) Because possibles do not need a cause.
D) Because the cause is part of the collection.

Key Takeaway
The totality of contingent beings, including their relations, necessitates a necessary being who is the cause of all.

?
This argument shows that the entire universe of contingent beings points to a Necessary Cause. Have you ever looked at the web of dependencies in your own life and traced them back to that Cause? Or do you stop at secondary causes, never letting your heart ascend to the Creator? What would it look like to live each moment conscious that you are held in existence by the One who needs no cause?

The proof requires that the cause must be other than the whole and each individual. Are you tempted to idolize creation—whether people, possessions, or status—as if they are ultimate? The Fourth Proof: Impossibility of Infinite Regress
Author presents the fourth proof against actual infinite regress using the argument from correspondence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof from correspondence that demonstrates the impossibility of an actual infinite regress, establishing the need for a first cause.

Core Idea
An actual infinite regress is impossible because removing finite steps leads to a contradiction.

In this lesson, we examine a sophisticated proof by correspondence (burhan al-tatbiq) that refutes the possibility of an actual infinite chain of causes. The author imagines pairing elements of two sets from the same series after removing ten steps. The logical outcome forces the series to be finite, proving that an infinite regress is impossible. We then consider a philosophical objection and the author's brief response.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
Read the beginning of the passage where the author describes an infinite chain of causes and effects from a given effect backwards. This is the initial set.
The chain is assumed to be actually infinite for the sake of argument.
2
Follow the mental operation
The author then removes ten steps from the finite (effect) side, creating a second set. Then, in the mind, each element of the second set is paired with an element of the first set in order (first with first, second with second, etc.).
This pairing is key to uncovering the contradiction.
3
Analyze the two possibilities
After pairing, either no difference appears between the sets or a difference appears. If no difference, the greater set (with ten extra steps) equals the lesser, which is impossible. If a difference appears, it must be on the far end, implying that the far end is finite—thus the series cannot be infinite.
Both possibilities lead to the conclusion that an actual infinite regress is impossible.
4
Consider the objection and response
The objector claims this proof would force philosophers to accept finitude of events and souls, which they deny. The author responds by referring to a previous discussion, but does not elaborate further here.
This objection shows that the proof has broader implications that need to be addressed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الرابع : إنا إذا اعتبرنا هذا المعلول الآخر ( ثم اعتبرنا علته ) (١) ثم اعتبرنا علة علته إلى ما لا نهاية له ، فهذه جملة . فإذا حذفنا من هذا الجانب المتناهي عشر مراتب ، واعتبرناها بعد حذف هذه العشرة عنها ، كانت جملة أخرى . ثم طبقنا في الوهم كل واحد من أحاد إحدى الجملتين ، على واحدة من آحاد الجملة الأخرى ، بحسب مراتب التطبيق . والمراد من قولنا بحسب مراتب التطبيق : أن الأخير من هذه الجملة بتقابل بالأخير من الجملة الثانية ، والثاني من هذه الجملة ، يتقابل بالثاني من تلك الجملة ، والثالث من هذه بالثالث من هذه ، وقس على هذا الترتيب فنقول : إما أن يظهر التفاوت بين الجملتين أو لا يظهر ( فإن لم يظهر (١) ، لزم كون الزائد مساويا للناقص ، وهو محال . وإن ظهر التفاوت فذلك التفاوت ، إما أن يظهر من الجانب الذي يلينا ، وهو محال . لأنا فرضنا التطبيق بحسب مراتب الأعداد حاصلا من هذا الجانب ، فوجب أن يظهر التفاوت من الجانب الآخر ، وذلك يوجب التناهي من الطرف الآخر . وذلك يمنع من القول بكونها أمورا غير متناهية ، فإن قالوا : هذا الدليل يوجب على الفلاسفة أن يحكموا بتناهي الحوادث ، وأن يحكموا بتناهي ( أعداد النفوس الناطقة ، مع أنهم لا يقولون بها . فنقول : الفرق بين هذه المسألة ، وبين ) (٢) هاتين الصورتين قد ذكرناه في باب تناهي الأبعاد
The fourth proof: If we consider this last effect, then consider its cause, then consider the cause of its cause, and so on ad infinitum, this is a set. Then if we remove from this finite side ten steps, and consider it after the removal of these ten, it becomes another set. Then we mentally pair each individual of one set with one individual of the other set according to the order of correspondence. By 'order of correspondence' we mean that the last of this set corresponds to the last of the second set, the second of this set corresponds to the second of that set, the third of this corresponds to the third of that, and so on. Then we say: Either a difference appears between the two sets or it does not appear. If it does not appear, then the greater is equal to the lesser, which is impossible. If a difference appears, then that difference must appear on the side away from us, because we assumed the correspondence in order is complete on our side. Thus the difference would appear on the other side, which implies finitude on the other side. That prevents saying that they are infinite things. If they say: This proof forces the philosophers to judge the finitude of events and the finitude of the number of rational souls, while they do not affirm that. We say: The difference between this issue and those two cases has been mentioned in the chapter on the finitude of dimensions.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) for the impossibility of an actual infinite regress, thus establishing the need for a first cause. The author presents a thought experiment. Suppose we have an infinite chain of causes and effects extending from a given effect back to infinity. This entire chain is a set. Now, from the finite end (the effect), we remove ten steps. This yields a second set that is shorter by ten. Then we mentally pair each element of the shorter set with an element of the original set in a one-to-one correspondence in order (first with first, second with second, etc.). Since the original set is infinite, removing ten should still leave an infinite set. Now, when we compare the two sets in this way, one of two possibilities: either no difference appears (they match perfectly) or a difference appears. If no difference appears, then the set with ten removed is equal to the original infinite set, meaning the finite removal made no difference—this would imply that the original was not actually larger, which is absurd because the original has ten more elements. If a difference appears, it must appear on the far end (away from us), because the pairing from our side is exact up to a point. That difference indicates that the far end is finite, because the difference is only possible if the series terminates. Therefore, the series cannot be infinite. The objector (the philosophers) claims that this proof would also force them to say that events (like the world's duration) are finite and that the number of rational souls is finite, which they deny. The author responds that the difference between this case and those two has been explained elsewhere (in the chapter on finitude of dimensions). So the proof stands.

Key Terms
تطبيق
تفاوت
غير متناه
تناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof assumes the existence of an actual infinite and uses it to derive a contradiction, when it is a reductio ad absurdum that assumes infinity for the sake of argument.
Correction: The proof does not commit to the existence of an actual infinite; it shows that if such an infinite existed, a logical contradiction follows, so the initial assumption (actual infinite regress) must be false.

Checkpoint
In the fourth proof, what does the author conclude if no difference appears between the two sets after removing ten steps?
A) That the infinite set is actually finite.
B) That the greater equals the lesser, which is impossible.
C) That the infinite set is greater.
D) That the removal changes the set's nature.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof shows that an actual infinite regress is impossible because it leads to a logical contradiction when you remove a finite part and compare.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen a logical proof that an infinite regress of causes is impossible. Now think: Are there areas in your life where you allow an 'infinite regress' of excuses to avoid facing the ultimate Cause? What first step will you take today to break that chain?
?
The proof shows that removing finite steps from an infinite series leads to contradiction. Consider your own spiritual journey: Are you removing 'finite steps' in your worship but expecting infinite mercy without confronting the First Cause? How does this proof push you to re-evaluate your relationship with Allah?
?
If the universe cannot go on forever, then there must be a beginning—a First Cause. This means your existence is not accidental but intended. How does this truth demand a response from you today, not just as an intellectual assent but as a personal commitment?

The Fifth Proof: Argument from Multiplicity of Infinite Series
Author proves finitude of causal chains via comparison of two infinite series.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how comparing two infinite causal series reveals that each series must be finite, thereby proving the necessity of a first cause.

Core Idea
Comparing two infinite series shows a contradiction, proving each causal chain is finite.

The fifth proof uses a clever comparison between two possible beings and their causal chains. If each chain were infinite, then the total of the two would be greater than one alone, but infinity cannot be exceeded. This contradiction forces us to conclude that each chain must be finite, leading to an ultimate first cause.

Guided Path
1
Understand the assumption
Start by assuming one possible existent with its causes forms an infinite set. This is the hypothesis we aim to disprove.
The proof uses a reductio ad absurdum structure.
2
Consider a second possible being
Now consider another possible being, also with its own infinite causal chain. The total number of entities across both chains is compared.
Each chain is assumed infinite, so the total must be larger than one chain.
3
Recognize the contradiction
If one chain is infinite, it cannot be less than any other number, yet the total of two chains is greater. This contradicts the nature of infinity. Therefore, the initial assumption is false.
This forces the conclusion that each chain is finite, establishing a first cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الخامس : إذا فرضنا موجودا واحدا من الممكنات ، فهو مع علله أعداد غير متناهية ، ثم إذا اعتبرنا واحدا آخر فهو أيضاً مع جملة علله أعداد أخرى غير متناهية ، وإذا كان كذلك فعدد الجملتين أكثر لا محالة من عدد الجملة الواحدة، وكل ما كان أقل من غيره ، فهو متناه فعدد الجملة الواحدة متناه ، وقد فرضناه غير متناه . هذا خلف .
The fifth proof: If we assume one possible existent, then along with its causes it is an infinite number. Then if we consider another existent, it too along with its entire causes is another infinite number. If that is the case, then the number of the two sets is necessarily greater than the number of the one set. And everything that is less than something else is finite. So the number of the one set is finite, while we assumed it infinite. This is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is another proof for the finitude of causal chains. The author argues by comparing two possible beings, each with its own infinite chain of causes. If each chain is infinite, then the sum of the two chains would be a larger infinity. But the concept of 'larger than infinite' is contradictory because infinity cannot be greater or lesser; any comparison implies finitude. Therefore, the claim that a single chain is infinite leads to a contradiction when we consider two such chains. Hence, each chain must be finite, implying a first cause. The proof is a concise reductio ad absurdum: assume a possible being has an infinite causal series; then consider another possible being with its infinite series; the total of the two series would be greater than either alone, but the first series being infinite means it is not finite and cannot be less than something, yet it is less than the total. Thus the assumption is false.

Key Terms
ممكن
علة
غير متناه
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that infinite sets can have different sizes (as in modern set theory), so the comparison is invalid.
Correction: The classical Islamic theologians held that infinity cannot be exceeded or compared in magnitude; any such comparison implies finitude. This proof relies on that classical notion, not on Cantorian set theory.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the fifth proof?
A) The number of possible beings is finite.
B) The number of possible beings is infinite.
C) Each possible being's causal chain is finite.
D) Two infinite series can be combined without contradiction.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof shows that causal chains cannot be infinite by creating a contradiction when comparing two such chains.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This argument confronts you with the reality that something outside the chain must exist. Have you personally confronted the implications that your own existence is not self-sufficient, or do you still live as though you are the first cause of your own life?

The Seventh Proof: Dependence and Necessity
Argues that contingent beings require an independent necessary existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the logical dependence of contingent beings in an infinite chain requires the existence of a self-sufficient Necessary Being.

Core Idea
A chain of dependents cannot exist without an independent source.

This proof builds on the earlier distinction between necessary and contingent. The contingent cannot exist by itself; it only becomes existent through its cause. If we imagine an endless chain of such dependent causes and effects, each link's existence is borrowed. But a chain of borrowers cannot stand without an original giver. The seventh proof establishes that there must be a being that is necessary in itself, not dependent on another.

Guided Path
1
Review the premise
Understand the key premise: a contingent being cannot exist unless its cause makes it necessary. This was established in the earlier chapter.
The premise is crucial for the proof.
2
Consider an infinite chain
Imagine an endless series of causes and effects. Each one's existence is dependent (tabi'). If any were independent, it would be necessary in itself, contradicting the assumption.
3
Draw the conclusion
If all are dependent, they all depend on something else. But a dependent cannot exist without an independent. Therefore, there must be a self-sufficient necessary being.
This refutes infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان السابع : أن نقول : إنا قد بينا في باب خواص الواجب والممكن أن الممكن لذاته ، ما لم يصر واجب الوجود عن سببه ، امتنع أن يدخل في الوجود ، وإذا كان كذلك كانت ضرورة الممكن لذاته أمراً واجباً لا يحصل له أمر إلا بتبعية الغير، إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : لو فرضنا أسبابا ومسببات لا نهاية لها ، لكان وجوب كل واحد منها تابعا ، لا أصليا ، إذ لو فرضنا واحداً منها يكون أصليا في الوجود ، لكان ذلك الواحد واجبا لذاته ، وقد فرضنا أنه ليس كذلك ، وإذا كانت وجوباتها بأسرها تابعة لوجوب شيء آخر ، فلا بد من متبوع، لأن حصول التابع من حيث هو تابع بدون المتبوع محال ، فيثبت أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بوجود شيء يكون واجب الوجود ، على سبيل الأصالة ، لا على سبيل التبعية للغير وكل ما كان كذلك فهو واجب الوجود لذاته ، فيثبت أنه لا بد في الموجودات من موجود واجب الوجود لذاته ، وهو المطلوب . فهذه جملة البراهين المعتبرة في إبطال التسلسل وبالله التوفيق .
The seventh proof: We say: We have already explained in the chapter on the properties of the Necessary and the Contingent that the contingent in itself, as long as it does not become necessary of existence by its cause, it is impossible for it to enter into existence. And if this is so, the necessity of the contingent in itself is a necessary matter; it does not obtain anything except by dependence on another. If you have understood this, we say: If we suppose causes and effects with no end, then the necessity of each one of them would be dependent, not original. For if we suppose one of them to be original in existence, that one would be necessary in itself, but we have supposed that it is not so. And if all their necessities are dependent on the necessity of something else, then there must be an independent (matbu'), because the occurrence of the dependent as dependent without the independent is impossible. Thus it is established that there must be an acknowledgment of the existence of something that is necessary of existence in the way of originality, not in the way of dependence on another. And everything that is so is necessary of existence in itself. Thus it is established that among existents there must necessarily be an existent that is necessary of existence in itself, and that is what is sought. So this is the totality of the considered proofs for refuting infinite regress, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof, which builds on the distinction between the necessary and the contingent established earlier. The contingent does not exist by itself; it requires a cause to make it necessary. In an infinite regress of causes and effects, each link's necessity is dependent (tabi') not original (asli). If any link were original, it would be necessary in itself, which contradicts the assumption that all are contingent. Therefore, all are dependent. But a chain of dependents cannot exist without an independent (matbu') upon which they rely. Hence, there must be a being that is necessary in itself, not dependent on another. This is the required conclusion. The author concludes by noting that these are the considered proofs for refuting infinite regress (tasalsul).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite chain of dependent causes can exist without any independent being.
Correction: The proof shows that 'dependent' logically requires an independent; without it, the chain cannot exist.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh proof, what must be true if all causes and effects in an infinite chain are dependent?

B) There must be an independent upon which they depend.
C) At least one of them must be original.
D) They are all necessary in themselves.

Key Takeaway
An infinite chain of dependent beings cannot exist without a self-sufficient, necessary being as its foundation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The proof shows that a chain of dependents cannot stand without an independent. In your daily life, where do you place your ultimate trust—in things that are themselves dependent (people, possessions, status) or in the Only Independent? Be honest: what does your anxiety reveal?
?
If you truly internalized that nothing in creation possesses original necessity, how would that change the way you react when something you rely on is taken away? Can you commit to letting go of one false independent today and leaning solely on the Self-Sufficient?

The Seventh Proof: Original vs. Dependent Necessity
The author presents the seventh proof against infinite regress based on original versus dependent necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between original and dependent necessity proves that an infinite regress of causes is impossible and that a self-necessary being must exist.

Core Idea
Dependence cannot be infinite; it must terminate in an independent source.

This lesson presents the seventh proof against infinite regress from the kalam tradition. The argument builds on the distinction between original and dependent necessity, showing that an endless chain of dependencies would be impossible without a self-necessary being. Follow along as the author deduces the necessary existence of God.

Guided Path
1
Recall the distinction between possible and necessary beings
The proof begins with a premise from earlier: a possible being only becomes existent through a cause. Understand this foundational idea before proceeding.
2
Distinguish original from dependent necessity
The author argues that if a being's existence depends on another, its necessity is dependent (tabi'i). Without an independent source, dependence is impossible.
This is the key insight of the proof.
3
Apply the reasoning to an infinite chain
Suppose an infinite series of causes. Each member's necessity is dependent. But a chain of dependencies requires an original source. Therefore, a self-necessary being must exist.
Notice how the proof avoids circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان السابع : أن نقول : إنا قد بينا في باب خواص الواجب والممكن أن الممكن لذاته ، ما لم يصر واجب الوجود عن سببه ، امتنع أن يدخل في الوجود ، وإذا كان كذلك كانت ضرورة الممكن لذاته أمراً واجباً لا يحصل له أمر إلا بتبعية الغير، إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : لو فرضنا أسبابا ومسببات لا نهاية لها ، لكان وجوب كل واحد منها تابعا ، لا أصليا ، إذ لو فرضنا واحداً منها يكون أصليا في الوجود ، لكان ذلك الواحد واجبا لذاته ، وقد فرضنا أنه ليس كذلك ، وإذا كانت وجوباتها بأسرها تابعة لوجوب شيء آخر ، فلا بد من متبوع، لأن حصول التابع من حيث هو تابع بدون المتبوع محال ، فيثبت أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بوجود شيء يكون واجب الوجود ، على سبيل الأصالة ، لا على سبيل التبعية وكل ما كان كذلك فهو واجب الوجود لذاته ، فيثبت أنه لا بد في الموجودات من موجود واجب الوجود لذاته ، وهو المطلوب . فهذه جملة البراهين المعتبرة في إبطال التسلسل وبالله التوفيق
The seventh proof: We say: We have already explained in the chapter on the properties of the Necessary and the Possible that the possible in itself, unless it becomes necessary in existence from its cause, is impossible to enter into existence. If that is so, then the necessity of the possible in itself is something obligatory; it does not acquire anything except through dependency on another. If you know this, we say: if we suppose causes and effects without end, then the necessity of each one of them would be dependent, not original. For if we suppose one of them to be original in existence, that one would be necessary in itself, but we have supposed it is not so. And if their necessities are all dependent on the necessity of something else, then there must be a followed (matbuʿ), because the occurrence of the follower as a follower without the followed is impossible. So it is established that it is necessary to acknowledge the existence of a thing that is necessary in existence in an original way, not in a dependent way. And everything that is like that is necessary in existence in itself. So it is established that there must be among existents an existent that is necessary in existence in itself, and that is the desired conclusion. These are the sound proofs in refuting infinite regress (and with Allah is success).
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof against infinite regress (tasalsul). The argument is built on the concept of original (asli) versus dependent (tabiʿi) necessity. The author first recalls the fundamental distinction: a possible being (mumkin) does not exist by itself; it requires a cause to become necessary in existence. If we posit an infinite chain of causes and effects, every member of the chain would have an existence that is dependent on its cause. None would be original in existence. But a chain of dependent entities requires an independent source (matbuʿ) to ground the dependency. Without such an original source, the chain cannot exist. Therefore, there must be a being that is necessary in itself (wajib al-wujud bi-dhatihi). The proof concludes that the negation of an infinite regress leads to the affirmation of a self-necessary being. The author then notes that these are the sound proofs against infinite regress.

Correction: The proof does not assume a necessary being; it starts from the accepted fact that contingent beings require a cause and deduces that an infinite regress is impossible, thus a first cause must exist.

?

?
The proof says a dependent thing cannot exist without an independent source. Look at your own life: what are you depending on that is merely a follower of other followers? Will you now consciously trace your dependency back to the Original, or will you keep clinging to what is itself dependent?

The Second Argument: Contingency Necessitates a Specific Cause
Presents the main proof from contingency to a specific efficient cause and necessary existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why contingency (imkan) logically requires a specific, determinate efficient cause (mu'aththir) and how this leads to the conclusion of a Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud).

Core Idea
Contingency's single essence demands a single, specific cause.

In this second argument, the author builds on the concept of contingency to demonstrate that all contingent beings must depend on one specific cause. He first rules out an indeterminate cause because it cannot exist in reality, then shows that the specific cause cannot be contingent itself, forcing the conclusion of a Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument's initial dichotomy
The author presents two possibilities: either contingency causes the need for an indeterminate cause or for a specific cause. Read carefully to see how he rejects the first.
2
Follow the rejection of the indeterminate cause
Notice that an indeterminate cause has no external existence, so it cannot satisfy the need.
This uses the principle that only specific things exist in the external world.
3
Understand how a single essence leads to a single cause
Since contingency has the same essence in all contingent beings, the specific cause must be the same for all.
Equality in cause necessitates equality in effect.
4
See the conclusion that the specific cause cannot be contingent
If it were contingent, it would be self-causing, which is absurd. Therefore, it must be necessary.
This is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن الإمان علة للحاجة إلى المؤثر ، فإما أن يكون علة الحاجة إلى مؤثر مبهم لا بعينه ، وإما أن يكون علة الحاجة إلى مؤثر متعين في نفسه ، والأول باطل ، لأن غير المعين من حيث إنه غير معين لا وجود له في الخارج، لأن كل ما كان موجودا في الخارج فهو معين ، فما لا يكون معينا يمتنع أن يكون موجودا في الخارج، وكل ما لا يكون موجودا في الخارج امتنع تحقق الحاجة إليه في الخارج، ولما بطل هذا القسم ، ثبت أن ماهية الإمان ، علة للحاجة إلى شيء معين، وماهية الإمان واحدة ، من حيث إنها ماهية الإمان ، فوجب احتياج الممكنات إلى ذلك المعين ، لأن التساوي في العلة ، يوجب التساوي في المعلول. إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لو كان ذلك الشيء المعين ممكنا في نفسه ، لزم كونه محتاجا إلى نفسه ، وكونه علة لنفسه . وذلك محال فثبت أن جميع الممكنات محتاجة إلى شيء واحد بعينه وثبت أن ذلك الشيء المعين يمتنع كونه ممكناً في نفسه ، والموجود الذي لا يكون ممكن الوجود لذاته ، يكون واجب الوجود لذاته . فثبت أن جميع الممكنات مستندة إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته ، وهو المطلوب. فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الإمان علة للحاجة إلى السبب من حيث إنه سبب ، والأسباب الكثيرة إذا أخذت من حيث إنها أسباب ، كان المفهوم من مجرد كونها أسبابا مفهوما واحدا . والإمان ، وإن كان مفهوماً واحدا إلا أنه يحوج إلى السبب من حيث إنه سبب ، فلم يلزم إسناد كل الممكنات إلى سبب واحد ؟ قلنا : السبب إما أن تعتبر ذاته المخصوصة ، أو يعتبر مجرد كونه سببا ومؤثرا في الغير، أما الاعتبار الثاني فمن المستحيل أن يكون الإمان سببا للحاجة إليه ، ويدل عليه وجهان : - الأول : إن المفهوم من السبب حالة نسبية إضافية . والأحوال النسبية تكون من اللواحق والعوارض وكل ما كان من اللواحق والعوارض فهو معلول ، وكونه سببا ومؤثرا معلول فيعود الإلزام فيه. الثاني : إنا قد دللنا على أن السبب لا يوجب المعلول من حيث إنه سبب ومؤثر ، لأن السببية والمؤثرية من مقولة المضاف والمضافات معا ، فالسببية والمؤثرية متأخرة بالرتبة عن ذات الأثر ، فيمتنع كونها مؤثرة في ذات الأثر ، بل المؤثر في ذات المعلول إنما هو الذات المخصوصة التي للسبب ، وإذا كان كذلك كان الإمان علة للحاجة إلى تلك الذات المخصوصة ، وعلة للاستناد إلى تلك الذات المخصوصة ، وحينئذ يجب أن يكون كل ممكن مستندا إليه . وهذا برهان حسن في إثبات واجب الوجود
The second argument: Contingency (imkan) is the cause of the need for the efficient cause (mu'aththir). Either it is the cause of the need for an indeterminate efficient cause (mu'aththir mubham) not specified, or it is the cause of the need for a specific efficient cause (mu'aththir muta'ayyin fi nafsihi). The first is invalid because that which is unspecified, insofar as it is unspecified, has no existence in the external world (kharij); for everything that exists in the external world is specific. So whatever is not specific is impossible to exist in the external world, and everything that does not exist in the external world cannot be genuinely needed in the external world. Since this alternative is invalid, it is established that the essence (mahiyya) of contingency is the cause of the need for something specific. And the essence of contingency is one insofar as it is the essence of contingency, so it is necessary that all contingent beings (mumkinat) need that specific thing, because equality in the cause necessitates equality in the effect. Once this is established, we say: if that specific thing were contingent in itself, it would follow that it is in need of itself and that it is a cause of itself, which is impossible. So it is established that all contingent beings are in need of one specific thing, and that that specific thing cannot possibly be contingent in itself; and that which is not possible of existence in itself is necessary of existence in itself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi). So it is established that all contingent beings depend on an existent that is necessary of existence in itself, and that is the desired conclusion. If they say: Why is it not permissible to say that contingency is the cause of the need for the cause (sabab) insofar as it is a cause, and that multiple causes, when taken insofar as they are causes, have a single concept from the mere fact of being causes, and contingency, even though it is one concept, nevertheless necessitates the cause insofar as it is a cause, so it does not require that all contingent beings be attributed to one cause? We say: The cause is either considered in terms of its specific essence (dhat makhsusa), or considered merely as being a cause and effective agent (mu'aththiran fi al-ghayr). As for the second consideration, it is impossible for contingency to be the cause of the need for it, and two points indicate this: The first: The concept of the cause is a relative, attributive state (hala nisbiyya idafiyya). Relative states are among the consequences and accidents (al-lawahiq wa al-awarid), and everything that is among the consequences and accidents is an effect; so its being a cause and effective agent is an effect, and the necessary consequence returns. The second: We have demonstrated that the cause does not necessitate the effect insofar as it is a cause and effective agent, because causality (sababiyya) and effectivity (mu'aththiriyya) belong to the category of relation and relative things (al-mudaf wa al-mudafat ma'an); so causality and effectivity are posterior in rank to the essence of the effect, so it is impossible for them to be effective in the essence of the effect. Rather, the effective agent in the essence of the effect is only the specific essence that belongs to the cause. And if this is so, then contingency is the cause of the need for that specific essence and the cause of dependence on that specific essence. In that case, it must be that every contingent being depends on it. And this is a good proof (burhan hasan) for establishing the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud).
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-hujja al-thaniyya) for the existence of the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud). The argument is based on the concept of contingency (imkan) and its relationship to the need for an efficient cause (mu'aththir). The author first sets out a dichotomy: either contingency is the cause of the need for an indeterminate efficient cause (i.e., any cause) or for a specific, determinate efficient cause. He rejects the first alternative because an indeterminate, non-specific thing cannot exist in the external world (kharij); only specific things exist. Since the need is for something that exists externally, the cause must be specific. Furthermore, contingency has a single essence (mahiyya) in all contingent beings, so the same specific thing must be needed by all contingents. Next, the author argues that this specific thing cannot itself be contingent, because that would lead to a vicious circle (self-causation). Therefore, it must be necessary in itself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi). Thus, all contingent beings depend on one Necessary Existent. The author then anticipates an objection: the opponents might claim that contingency causes the need for a cause only insofar as it is a cause, and since 'causehood' is a common concept, many different causes could satisfy the need without all pointing to a single cause. In reply, the author distinguishes between considering the cause in terms of its specific essence and considering it merely as a cause. The latter (mere causehood) is an accident and relative state, which itself is an effect, so it cannot be the ultimate object of need. Moreover, the author argues that causality and effective agency (sababiyya wa mu'aththiriyya) are relational properties that are ontologically posterior to the essence of the effect; they cannot be the true efficient cause. The true efficient cause is the specific essence of the cause. Since contingency requires a specific essence, it is that specific essence which is the object of need for all contingent beings, and that specific essence cannot be contingent—it is the Necessary Existent. The author concludes that this is a sound proof.

Key Terms
إمان
مؤثر
ماهية
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that contingency could be satisfied by any cause, even an indeterminate one.
Correction: The argument shows that an indeterminate cause has no external existence, so it cannot be the object of a real need. The cause must be specific.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the possibility that contingency requires an indeterminate efficient cause?
A) Because an indeterminate cause would be self-contradictory.
B) Because an indeterminate cause does not exist in the external world.
C) Because the opponents assumed a specific cause.
D) Because the essence of contingency is multiple.

Key Takeaway
Contingency logically entails a single, specific efficient cause that must be necessary in itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is purely contingent and depends on a single specific cause that is necessary, why do you so often live as if you are self-sufficient? When will you truly internalize that every breath is a gift from that One Necessary Being?
?
Contemplate the fact that your very essence (mahiyya) demands a specific cause. In what moments of your day do you feel that need most acutely? Commit to acknowledging that dependence silently before each action tomorrow.
?
The proof shows that the cause cannot be indeterminate—it must be a specific, knowable reality. Are you seeking that specific reality with the same urgency as this argument demands? Or are you content with vague spirituality? Let this argument call you to the One who is specifically your Lord.

The Second Argument: Refuting the Objection on Generic Causality
Refutes the objection that contingency requires only a generic cause, using two arguments about the nature of causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that contingency might only require a cause insofar as it is a cause, and the two-part refutation showing that the efficient cause must be a specific essence.

Core Idea
Causality as a concept is an effect, not a true cause.

After establishing the main proof, the author anticipates an objection: perhaps contingency only necessitates the need for a cause considered as a cause, not a specific one. He replies by analyzing the nature of causality itself, showing that the concept of 'causehood' is a relational accident logically posterior to the effect, and thus cannot be the real efficient cause. The true cause is the specific essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector suggests that contingency requires a cause only in its universal aspect, not a particular one.
This would allow many different causes to satisfy the need.
2
Understand the first refutation
The author argues that 'causehood' is a relative state, which is an accident and therefore an effect itself, so it cannot be the ultimate object of need.
This shows that the objector's cause is also contingent.
3
Follow the second refutation
Causality and effectivity are relational properties that are ontologically posterior to the effect's essence. Hence, they cannot be the efficient cause.
Only the specific essence of the cause can be the real agent.
4
See the final conclusion
Since contingency needs a specific essence, that specific essence must be the same for all contingent beings, leading again to the Necessary Existent.
This reinforces the original proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن الإمان علة للحاجة إلى المؤثر ، فإما أن يكون علة الحاجة إلى مؤثر مبهم لا بعينه ، وإما أن يكون علة الحاجة إلى مؤثر متعين في نفسه ، والأول باطل ، لأن غير المعين من حيث إنه غير معين لا وجود له في الخارج، لأن كل ما كان موجودا في الخارج فهو معين ، فما لا يكون معينا يمتنع أن يكون موجودا في الخارج، وكل ما لا يكون موجودا في الخارج امتنع تحقق الحاجة إليه في الخارج، ولما بطل هذا القسم ، ثبت أن ماهية الإمان ، علة للحاجة إلى شيء معين، وماهية الإمان واحدة ، من حيث إنها ماهية الإمان ، فوجب احتياج الممكنات إلى ذلك المعين ، لأن التساوي في العلة ، يوجب التساوي في المعلول. إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لو كان ذلك الشيء المعين ممكنا في نفسه ، لزم كونه محتاجا إلى نفسه ، وكونه علة لنفسه . وذلك محال فثبت أن جميع الممكنات محتاجة إلى شيء واحد بعينه وثبت أن ذلك الشيء المعين يمتنع كونه ممكناً في نفسه ، والموجود الذي لا يكون ممكن الوجود لذاته ، يكون واجب الوجود لذاته . فثبت أن جميع الممكنات مستندة إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته ، وهو المطلوب. فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الإمان علة للحاجة إلى السبب من حيث إنه سبب ، والأسباب الكثيرة إذا أخذت من حيث إنها أسباب ، كان المفهوم من مجرد كونها أسبابا مفهوما واحدا . والإمان ، وإن كان مفهوماً واحدا إلا أنه يحوج إلى السبب من حيث إنه سبب ، فلم يلزم إسناد كل الممكنات إلى سبب واحد ؟ قلنا : السبب إما أن تعتبر ذاته المخصوصة ، أو يعتبر مجرد كونه سببا ومؤثرا في الغير، أما الاعتبار الثاني فمن المستحيل أن يكون الإمان سببا للحاجة إليه ، ويدل عليه وجهان : - الأول : إن المفهوم من السبب حالة نسبية إضافية . والأحوال النسبية تكون من اللواحق والعوارض وكل ما كان من اللواحق والعوارض فهو معلول ، وكونه سببا ومؤثرا معلول فيعود الإلزام فيه. الثاني : إنا قد دللنا على أن السبب لا يوجب المعلول من حيث إنه سبب ومؤثر ، لأن السببية والمؤثرية من مقولة المضاف والمضافات معا ، فالسببية والمؤثرية متأخرة بالرتبة عن ذات الأثر ، فيمتنع كونها مؤثرة في ذات الأثر ، بل المؤثر في ذات المعلول إنما هو الذات المخصوصة التي للسبب ، وإذا كان كذلك كان الإمان علة للحاجة إلى تلك الذات المخصوصة ، وعلة للاستناد إلى تلك الذات المخصوصة ، وحينئذ يجب أن يكون كل ممكن مستندا إليه . وهذا برهان حسن في إثبات واجب الوجود
The second argument: Contingency (imkan) is the cause of the need for the efficient cause (mu'aththir). Either it is the cause of the need for an indeterminate efficient cause (mu'aththir mubham) not specified, or it is the cause of the need for a specific efficient cause (mu'aththir muta'ayyin fi nafsihi). The first is invalid because that which is unspecified, insofar as it is unspecified, has no existence in the external world (kharij); for everything that exists in the external world is specific. So whatever is not specific is impossible to exist in the external world, and everything that does not exist in the external world cannot be genuinely needed in the external world. Since this alternative is invalid, it is established that the essence (mahiyya) of contingency is the cause of the need for something specific. And the essence of contingency is one insofar as it is the essence of contingency, so it is necessary that all contingent beings (mumkinat) need that specific thing, because equality in the cause necessitates equality in the effect. Once this is established, we say: if that specific thing were contingent in itself, it would follow that it is in need of itself and that it is a cause of itself, which is impossible. So it is established that all contingent beings are in need of one specific thing, and that that specific thing cannot possibly be contingent in itself; and that which is not possible of existence in itself is necessary of existence in itself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi). So it is established that all contingent beings depend on an existent that is necessary of existence in itself, and that is the desired conclusion. If they say: Why is it not permissible to say that contingency is the cause of the need for the cause (sabab) insofar as it is a cause, and that multiple causes, when taken insofar as they are causes, have a single concept from the mere fact of being causes, and contingency, even though it is one concept, nevertheless necessitates the cause insofar as it is a cause, so it does not require that all contingent beings be attributed to one cause? We say: The cause is either considered in terms of its specific essence (dhat makhsusa), or considered merely as being a cause and effective agent (mu'aththiran fi al-ghayr). As for the second consideration, it is impossible for contingency to be the cause of the need for it, and two points indicate this: The first: The concept of the cause is a relative, attributive state (hala nisbiyya idafiyya). Relative states are among the consequences and accidents (al-lawahiq wa al-awarid), and everything that is among the consequences and accidents is an effect; so its being a cause and effective agent is an effect, and the necessary consequence returns. The second: We have demonstrated that the cause does not necessitate the effect insofar as it is a cause and effective agent, because causality (sababiyya) and effectivity (mu'aththiriyya) belong to the category of relation and relative things (al-mudaf wa al-mudafat ma'an); so causality and effectivity are posterior in rank to the essence of the effect, so it is impossible for them to be effective in the essence of the effect. Rather, the effective agent in the essence of the effect is only the specific essence that belongs to the cause. And if this is so, then contingency is the cause of the need for that specific essence and the cause of dependence on that specific essence. In that case, it must be that every contingent being depends on it. And this is a good proof (burhan hasan) for establishing the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud).
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-hujja al-thaniyya) for the existence of the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud). The argument is based on the concept of contingency (imkan) and its relationship to the need for an efficient cause (mu'aththir). The author first sets out a dichotomy: either contingency is the cause of the need for an indeterminate efficient cause (i.e., any cause) or for a specific, determinate efficient cause. He rejects the first alternative because an indeterminate, non-specific thing cannot exist in the external world (kharij); only specific things exist. Since the need is for something that exists externally, the cause must be specific. Furthermore, contingency has a single essence (mahiyya) in all contingent beings, so the same specific thing must be needed by all contingents. Next, the author argues that this specific thing cannot itself be contingent, because that would lead to a vicious circle (self-causation). Therefore, it must be necessary in itself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi). Thus, all contingent beings depend on one Necessary Existent. The author then anticipates an objection: the opponents might claim that contingency causes the need for a cause only insofar as it is a cause, and since 'causehood' is a common concept, many different causes could satisfy the need without all pointing to a single cause. In reply, the author distinguishes between considering the cause in terms of its specific essence and considering it merely as a cause. The latter (mere causehood) is an accident and relative state, which itself is an effect, so it cannot be the ultimate object of need. Moreover, the author argues that causality and effective agency (sababiyya wa mu'aththiriyya) are relational properties that are ontologically posterior to the essence of the effect; they cannot be the true efficient cause. The true efficient cause is the specific essence of the cause. Since contingency requires a specific essence, it is that specific essence which is the object of need for all contingent beings, and that specific essence cannot be contingent—it is the Necessary Existent. The author concludes that this is a sound proof.
