The Fourth Objection: Necessary Existent Would Be Composite
Presents objection that Necessary Existent entails composition due to description distinct from essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection to the Necessary Existent: that the distinction between essence and attribute implies composition, making the Necessary Existent contingent.

Core Idea
If the Necessary Existent has a description distinct from its essence, it becomes composite, hence possible, not necessary.

Before the refutation is presented, the objection itself must be clearly understood. This lesson examines the fourth objection against the Necessary Existent, which argues that the very concept of 'necessary of existence' implies composition. The objection claims that since the attribute 'necessity' is distinct from the essence, the Necessary Existent would be a composite of essence and attribute, and every composite is possible — contradicting its necessity. Grasping this objection is crucial to appreciate the subsequent answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the objection's flow.
Notice the objection's structure: claim, then explanation (بيان الملازمة).
2
Spot the key logical moves
Identify the premises: (1) necessity is a description distinct from essence, (2) description and described are distinct, (3) the Necessary Existent is a sum of essence and attribute, (4) every composite is possible.
Trace how the conclusion follows: so the Necessary Existent would be possible, contradicting its necessity.
3
Understand the term 'composite'
Grasp that 'composite' here means having parts, which implies dependence and thus contingency. The objection equates composition with possibility.
In Islamic theology, composite entities require their parts to exist, hence they are contingent.
4
Ask: why is this a challenge?
Reflect on how this objection undermines the concept of a Necessary Existent. If valid, it shows that the very notion is self-contradictory.
The answer (not in this passage) will address this by showing that the attribute is not really distinct from the essence.

الشبهة الرابعة : لو وجد واجب الوجود لذاته لكان مركبا ، وهذا محال فذاك محال . بيان الملازمة : أن واجب الوجود ، يجب أن يكون وجوده مغايرا لذاته ، بدليل أنه يصح أنه واجب الوجود لذاته ، والوصف مغاير للموصوف ، وإذا كان كذلك ، فواجب الوجود عبارة عن مجموع تلك الذات مع تلك الصفة وكل مركب ممكن فواجب الوجود لذاته ، ممكن الوجود لذاته ، وهو محال
The fourth objection: If a Necessary Existent by essence existed, it would be composite, and this is impossible, so that (the existence of the Necessary Existent) is impossible. Explanation of the implication: The Necessary Existent must have its existence distinct from its essence, as evidenced by the fact that it is correctly described as 'necessary of existence by essence', and the description is distinct from the described. This being the case, the Necessary Existent is a sum of that essence and that attribute, and every composite is possible, so the Necessary Existent by essence would be possibly-existent by essence, which is absurd.

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection (shubha rabi'a) against the Necessary Existent. The objection argues that the concept of a Necessary Existent implies composition. The reasoning is that the description 'necessary of existence' is distinct from the essence, so the Necessary Existent is a composite of essence and this attribute. Since every composite is contingent, the Necessary Existent would be contingent, contradicting its necessity. The objection is stated here without an explicit refutation in the provided text; it serves as a challenge to be answered.

The Composition Argument Against the Necessary of Existence
Presents the fourth doubt and its logical explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth doubt that argues the Necessary of Existence would be composite and therefore impossible, and why it relies on existence being a distinct attribute.

Core Idea
Composition implies contingency, so the Necessary cannot be composite.

This lesson presents a key objection from opponents of the Necessary of Existence: if it existed, it would be composed of essence and existence, making it contingent. Understanding this doubt is essential for grasping later refutations. The argument hinges on the assumption that existence is a distinct attribute added to essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the conditional structure: 'If the Necessary existed, it would be composite, and that is impossible, so its existence is impossible.'
The word 'muḥāl' (impossible) appears twice: once for compositeness and once for existence.
2
Understand the explanation of the implication
The author explains why compositeness follows: existence must be distinct from essence because the description 'necessary by itself' is distinct from the described. Thus, the Necessary is a composite of essence and attribute.
Consider the premise that existence is a real attribute added to essence.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the argument: existence distinct from essence → composite → every composite is possible → the Necessary would be possible → contradiction with its necessity.
The conclusion is that the existence of the Necessary is impossible (muḥāl).
4
Identify the hidden assumption
The doubt assumes existence is a positive attribute added to essence. This assumption will be challenged in later responses.
This is crucial for evaluating the strength of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : لو وجد واجب الوجود لذاته لكان مركبا وهذا محال فذاك محال . بيان الملازمة : أن واجب الوجود يجب أن يكون وجوده مغايرا لذاته بدليل أنه يصح أنه واجب الوجود لذاته والوصف مغاير للموصوف وإذا كان كذلك فواجب الوجود عبارة عن مجموع تلك الذات مع تلك الصفة وكل مركب ممكن فواجب الوجود لذاته ممكن الوجود وهو محال
The fourth doubt: If the Necessary of Existence (wājib al-wujūd) were to exist by itself, it would be composite (murakkab), and this is impossible (muḥāl), so that [i.e., its existence] is impossible. Explanation of the implication: that the Necessary of Existence must have its existence (wujūd) distinct from its essence (dhāt), evidenced by the fact that it is valid that it is necessary of existence by itself, and the description (waṣf) is distinct from the described (mawṣūf). And if this is so, then the Necessary of Existence is a combination of that essence and that attribute. And every composite is possible (mumkin), so the Necessary of Existence by itself is possible of existence, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection (shubha) against the existence of the Necessary of Existence. The objection is structured as a conditional argument: If the Necessary of Existence existed, it would be composite, but composition is impossible, therefore its existence is impossible. The author then explains the logical link (bayān al-mulāzama) between the antecedent and consequent: The Necessary of Existence must have its existence distinct from its essence because we describe it as 'necessary of existence by itself,' and the description is distinct from the described. Therefore, the Necessary of Existence is a composite of essence and attribute. However, every composite is possible (since it depends on its parts), so the Necessary of Existence would be possible, which contradicts its necessity. Thus the argument concludes that the existence of the Necessary of Existence is impossible. The objection assumes that existence is a positive attribute added to the essence, which is a key point that will be addressed in the answer.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
مركب
ممكن
محال
ذات
صفة
ملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the objection proves that the Necessary of Existence cannot exist at all, unconditionally.
Correction: The objection only shows a contradiction if existence is assumed to be a distinct attribute added to essence. The answer will later deny that existence is a positive attribute, thus defusing the objection.

Checkpoint
What is the logical implication (mulāzama) that the fourth doubt attempts to establish?
A) That the Necessary of Existence must have a distinct essence and existence.
B) That the Necessary of Existence is composite and therefore possible.
C) That all composites are necessary.
D) That existence is identical with essence.

Key Takeaway
The fourth doubt argues that the Necessary of Existence would be composite and thus possible, but this depends on the assumption that existence is an added attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

The Fifth Doubt: Dependence on Time and Duration
Presents the fifth doubt with explanation, objection, and two supporting reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth doubt against the Necessary of Existence, which argues that perpetual existence requires dependence on time, thereby implying contingency.

Core Idea
If the Necessary depends on time, it cannot be necessary.

The fifth doubt targets the concept of perpetual existence. If the Necessary must exist forever, that foreverness seems to require time as a container. But anything dependent on time is contingent, so the Necessary would be contingent. This objection forces us to clarify what we mean by God's eternity. Let's examine the argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: the doubt, its explanation, an anticipated response, and two supporting reasons.
Watch for the shift from 'they say' to 'we say' in the objection-reply part.
2
Identify the core claim
The doubt claims that if the Necessary of Existence exists, it must have perpetual existence, which requires existing in past, present, and future. This, in turn, requires time as a container, making the Necessary dependent on time and thus contingent.
3
Understand the anticipated reply and its rebuttal
The objector preempts a possible defense: that the Necessary simply 'exists without admitting non-existence' without temporal extension. The rebuttal argues that this verbal evasion is irrelevant; the intellect judges that lacking past/present/future existence equals non-existence.
The rebuttal emphasizes intellectual necessity over verbal formulas.
4
Examine the two reasons for impossibility
The passage gives two reasons why dependence on time is impossible for the Necessary: (1) dependence on another implies contingency, and (2) time itself is composed of passing parts, hence contingent, so what depends on it is even more contingent.
5
Reflect on the underlying assumption
Consider what the doubt assumes about time: that it is a 'container' for existence, and that 'perpetual' means temporal extension. These assumptions will be challenged in the refutation.
This connects to the larger discussion of God's eternity as non-temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة : لو حصل واجب الوجود لذاته لكان مفتقرا في دوام وجوده إلى المدة والزمان والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته . بيان الأول : أن واجب الوجود لذاته لا بد وأن يكون دائم الوجود والمقول من الدائم ما يكون موجودا فيما مضى وفي الحاضر وفي المستقبل ولكن الوجود في الماضي والحاضر والمستقبل مشروط بوجود الماضي والحاضر والمستقبل لأن كون الشيء مظروفا لشيء مشروط بوجود ذلك الظرف فيثبت أن دوام الوجود لا يتقرر إلا مع المدة والزمان لا يقال : إنا لا نقول : إن واجب الوجود لذاته موجود الآن وفي الماضي والمستقبل بل نقول : إنه موجود لا يقبل العدم ولا نزيد على ذلك لأنا نقول : لا عبرة في هذا الباب بأن يذكر هذا القول باللسان أو لا يذكر وإنما العبرة بالعقل ونحن نعقل بالضرورة أن الشيء الذي يصح حكم العقل عليه بأنه ما كان موجودا في الماضي وهو غير موجود في الحال الحاضر ولا يكون موجودا في المستقبل فإنه يكون معدوما محضا ونفيا صرفا وإنما قلنا : إن افتقار واجب الوجود لذاته إلى المدة والزمان محال لوجهين : الأول : إن كل مفتقر إلى الغير فهو ممكن لذاته والثاني : إن الزمان مركب من أجزاء متعاقبة منقضية فيكون ممكنا لذاته فالمفتقر في وجوده إليه أولى بالإمكان
The fifth doubt: If the Necessary of Existence by itself occurred, it would be dependent in its perpetual existence (dawām wujūd) on duration (mudda) and time (zamān), and that which depends on another is possible by itself. Explanation of the first: that the Necessary of Existence by itself must be perpetual in existence, and what is meant by perpetual is that which exists in the past, present, and future. But existence in the past, present, and future is conditioned by the existence of the past, present, and future, because a thing being contained in something is conditioned by the existence of that container. So it is established that perpetual existence is only realized with duration and time. It cannot be said: 'We do not say that the Necessary of Existence by itself exists now, in the past, and in the future; rather we say it exists without admitting non-existence, and we do not add to that.' Because we say: There is no consideration in this regard whether one mentions this statement with the tongue or not; rather consideration is of the intellect. And we necessarily understand that a thing which the intellect judges that it did not exist in the past and does not exist in the present and will not exist in the future, then it would be pure non-existence and sheer negation. And we said: That the dependence of the Necessary of Existence by itself on duration and time is impossible for two reasons: First: That everything dependent on another is possible by itself. Second: That time is composed of successive passing parts, so it is possible by itself, so that which depends in its existence on it is more deserving of possibility.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection against the existence of the Necessary of Existence. The objection argues that if the Necessary existed, it would require perpetual existence, which entails existing in the past, present, and future. But existing in those times is conditioned by the existence of time itself as a container (ẓarf). Therefore, the Necessary would depend on time. However, anything that depends on another is possible (mumkin) by itself. Therefore, the Necessary would be possible, contradicting its necessity. The objection then anticipates a possible response: one might say that the Necessary is simply 'existent without admitting non-existence' without specifying past, present, future. The objection counters that this verbal evasion is irrelevant; the intellect necessarily understands that if something has no existence in past, present, or future, it is nothing. So the dependence on time remains. Then the objection strengthens its case by giving two reasons why dependence on time is impossible for the Necessary: (1) dependence on another implies contingency, and (2) time itself is composite of passing parts, hence contingent, so what depends on it is even more contingent. The objection thus concludes that the Necessary cannot exist.

دوام

زمان
ممكن
مفتقر
ظرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'eternal' simply means 'not having a beginning,' and that the Necessary's eternity does not involve temporal extension.
Correction: The doubt assumes that perpetual existence requires existing in past, present, and future, which are temporal. The rebuttal insists that the intellect necessarily understands that lacking temporal existence is non-existence. This forces a clarification of what is meant by God's eternity.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth doubt, why would the Necessary of Existence depend on time?
A) Because time creates all things.
B) Because perpetual existence requires past, present, and future, which are conditioned by time.
C) Because God is within time.
D) Because the Necessary of Existence is composite.

Key Takeaway
The fifth doubt challenges the Necessary's eternity by linking perpetual existence to time, but this assumes time is a container—an assumption that will be refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The doubt argues that perpetual existence requires time as a container. In what ways do you unintentionally think of Allah as dwelling within time, such as when you ask for something 'now' or think of His actions in sequential order?
?
If you truly internalized that Allah is not bound by time, how would that change your relationship with waiting, patience, or regret over past sins?

Answering the First Doubt: Essence as Independence
Author redefines 'being an essence' as independence to answer the doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author reinterprets 'being an essence' as a negative concept (independence) to refute the claim that the Necessary Being's essence would be equal to all other essences.

Core Idea
Essence for God means independence, not a shared positive attribute.

In responding to the first doubt, the author offers a crucial distinction. Instead of accepting that 'being an essence' is a universal positive attribute, he explains it as a negative concept—self-sufficiency. This redefinition blocks the objection that the Necessary Being would be equal to others.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Pay attention to how the author introduces the doubt and then answers it.
2
Identify the doubt and answer
The doubt says: if God exists by essence, His essence is equal to all other essences. Identify the author's response: He questions this and offers an alternative meaning of 'being an essence'.
3
Understand the negative concept
The author says 'being an essence' means independence—a negative concept (mafhum sulbi). Why is this negative? Because it denies need, not affirms a quality.
Negative concepts can be shared without implying identity.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this refutes the doubt: if 'essence' is a negative concept, then it does not make God's essence positively equal to others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : أن نقول : أما قوله (۲) و لو وجب واجب الوجود لذاته، لكانت ذاته مساوية لسائر الذوات ، في كونه ذاتا ، فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن كونه ذاتا ، معناه أنه مستقل بنفسه ، ومعنى الاستقلال أنه لا حاجة به إلى الغير؟ وهذا المعنى مفهوم سلبي (٣) ، وهو صفة مشتركة (٤) فيما بين الحقائق المختلفة التي هي الذوات المخصوصة ، وتحقيق الكلام : أن الماهيات المختلفة لا يمتنع اشتراكها في لوازم متساوية ) وأما الماهيات المتساوية فإنه يمتنع اختلافها في الصفات اللازمة. والذي يحقق ما ذكرناه : أنه ((٥) كما يصح تقسيم الذوات إلى الواجب وإلى الممكن (٦) ، فكذلك يصح تقسيم الصفات إلى الصفات، التي تكون من الممكنات ، وإلى الصفات التي تكون من الكيفيات ، وغيرها فيلزم أن تكون الصفات كلها متساوية ، وإذا كانت الذوات بأسرها متساوية ، وكانت الصفات كلها بأسرها متساوية ، فمن أين حصل الاختلاف ؟
And the answer to the first doubt: we say: As for his statement: 'If the Necessary Being existed by virtue of Its essence, Its essence would be equal to all other essences in being an essence,' we say: Why is it not permissible to say that Its being an essence means that It is independent by Itself, and the meaning of independence is that It has no need of another? This meaning is a negative concept (mafhum sulbi), and it is a common attribute (sifa mushtaraka) among the different realities which are the particular essences. The precise statement is: Different quiddities (mahiyyat) are not prevented from sharing in necessary concomitants that are equal. As for equal quiddities, it is impossible for them to differ in necessary attributes. What verifies what we have mentioned is that just as it is valid to divide essences into the necessary and the possible, so it is valid to divide attributes into attributes that are from possible things and attributes that are from qualities, etc. Then it would follow that all attributes are equal, and if all essences are equal and all attributes are equal, then from where does difference arise?
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The first objection argues that if a Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud) exists, its essence would be equal to all other essences in being an essence, leading to no distinction. The author refutes by stating that being an essence is a negative concept (independence from others), which is a common attribute shared by different realities but does not imply equality in all respects. He further explains that different quiddities can share in equal necessary concomitants without being identical, and equal quiddities cannot differ in necessary attributes but can differ in accidental ones. The author then uses an analogy: just as essences are divided into necessary and possible, attributes can be divided, and if all were equal, difference would not arise, but difference does arise, so the objection fails.

Key Terms
ذات (dhat)
مفهوم سلبي (mafhum sulbi)
صفة مشتركة (sifa mushtaraka)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that 'being an essence' is a positive attribute that makes all essences equal.
Correction: The author shows that 'being an essence' can be interpreted as a negative concept (independence), which is not a positive attribute that would necessitate equality.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does 'being an essence' mean for the Necessary Being?
A) A positive attribute similar to other essences
B) A negative concept meaning independence from others
C) A combination of positive and negative
D) An attribute that necessitates equality with all other essences

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being's essence is not positively equal to others; rather, 'essence' denotes independence, a negative concept that can be shared without implying identity.

Analyzing Equality of Quiddities and Attributes
Author distinguishes different quiddities from equal ones and uses an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's precise analysis of quiddities and necessary concomitants, and how the analogy with attributes completes the refutation of the equality objection.

Core Idea
Different quiddities can share equal necessary properties without being identical.

Having established that 'being an essence' is a negative concept, the author provides a more precise logical analysis. He distinguishes between different quiddities sharing equal necessary concomitants and equal quiddities differing in attributes. Finally, he uses an analogy to expose the fallacy in the objection.

Read the passage

2
Understand the rule about mahiyyat
The author states that different quiddities (mahiyyat) can share in equal necessary concomitants (lawazim), but equal quiddities cannot differ in necessary attributes. Grasp this principle.
3

4

Refuting the Trilemma: Existence as a Negative Concept
Author denies existence is a positive concept to refute the trilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes a trilemma about the existence of the Necessary Being by denying that existence is a positive concept and allowing it to be a negative concept.

Core Idea
Existence can be a negative concept, collapsing the trilemma.

In this passage, the author tackles the second doubt: a trilemma claiming that existence of the Necessary Being must be either identical to, part of, or separate from its essence. Instead of addressing each horn, the author denies the underlying assumption that existence is a positive concept. He suggests existence could be a negative concept (like "not nonexistent"), which invalidates the trilemma entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the trilemma is presented, then the author replies by denying a premise.
The objection is summarized in the first part of the passage.
2
Identify the trilemma's three options
The trilemma says existence of the Necessary Being must be either the entirety of its essence, a part of its essence, or external to it. Understand each option.
3
See the author's strategy: deny the premise
The author points out that the trilemma assumes existence is a positive concept. He denies this, asking why it cannot be a negative concept.
This is a classic move in kalam: attacking the assumption rather than the conclusion.
4
Reflect on the implications
If existence is a negative concept (like independence from nothingness), the trilemma does not apply because the categories of identity, part, or external assume positivity.
Think about how a negative concept can escape such divisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثانية : وهي قوله : ( لو حصل واجب الوجود ، لكان وجوده () ، إما أن يكون تمام ذاته، أو جزء ذاته ، أو خارجاً عنه ، فنقول : هذا بناء على أن الوجود مفهوم ثبوتي ، وهو ممنوع فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه مفهوم عدمي ؟ وبهذا التقدير لا يصح ما ذكرتموه من التقسيم
As for the second doubt: it is his statement: 'If the Necessary Being existed, then its existence would either be the entirety of its essence, or a part of its essence, or external to it.' We say: This is based on the assumption that existence is a positive concept (mafhum thubuti), which is prohibited. So why is it not permissible to say that it is a negative concept (mafhum 'adami)? On this estimation, the division you mentioned would not hold.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer. The objector presents a trilemma: existence of the Necessary Being must be either identical to its essence, a part of it, or separate. The author rejects the premise that existence is a positive concept; it could be a negative concept (like independence from nothingness). If existence is negative, the trilemma collapses. Thus the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
مفهوم ثبوتي (mafhum thubuti)
مفهوم عدمي (mafhum 'adami)
وجود (wujud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is always a positive attribute that can be divided into identity, part, or external.
Correction: Existence can be a negative concept (like 'not nonexistent'), which does not admit such divisions.

Checkpoint

A) That existence is a negative concept
B) That existence is a positive concept
C) That the Necessary Being exists
D) That the trilemma is exhaustive

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the trilemma by denying that existence is a positive concept, offering the alternative that it is a negative concept.

Answer to the Third Doubt: Deferral to Later Discussion
Author defers the third doubt to a later discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author defers the third doubt and that it is a legitimate pedagogical strategy.

Core Idea
Deferring a doubt is not evasion; it is a sign of systematic teaching.

The author addresses a doubt about whether the existence of the Necessary Being is identical to Its essence. However, he postpones the full discussion. This may seem like avoidance, but in a systematic work, it is often better to lay groundwork first. In this lesson, we see the author's method of organizing knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence about the third doubt and its deferral. Notice the structure: the doubt is stated, then the author says it will come later.
The Arabic uses 'أما' to introduce the doubt and 'فنقول' for the response.
2
Understand the doubt
The doubt questions whether the existence of the Necessary Being is identical to Its reality or distinct. This is a deep metaphysical issue.
This issue is about the relationship between essence and existence.
3
Reflect on the deferral
The author defers the answer to a later, more detailed discussion. This shows careful pedagogy: some questions require prior concepts.
Deferring is not ignorance but strategic organization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الشبهة الثالثة : وهي قوله : ( وجود واجب الوجود ، إما أن يكون نفس حقيقته ، أو مغايرا لحقيقته ، فنقول : هذه المسألة ستأتي بعد ذلك على سبيل الاستقصاء . وأما الشبهة الرابعة : وهي قوله : ( لو وجد واجب الوجود لكان مركبا : فجوابه : أن هذا إنما يلزم لو كان الوجوب مفهوما ثبوتيا
As for the third doubt: it is his statement: 'The existence of the Necessary Being is either identical to Its reality or distinct from Its reality.' We say: This issue will come later in detail. As for the fourth doubt: it is his statement: 'If the Necessary Being existed, It would be composite.' The answer to it is that this would only follow if necessity (wujub) were a positive concept.

Commentary
This lesson combines two short objections. The third doubt is deferred to a later discussion. The fourth doubt claims that the Necessary Being would be composite; the author answers that composition would only follow if 'necessity' (wujub) were a positive concept, but it is not—similar to the second doubt's argument.

Correction: Deferring is a pedagogical strategy to postpone complex issues until the necessary foundations are laid.

Author refutes the fourth doubt by denying that necessity is a positive concept.

Core Idea
Composition only follows if necessity were a positive attribute, but it is not.

The fourth doubt argues that if the Necessary Being exists, it must be composed of existence and essence. The author gives a concise answer: this would only be a problem if necessity were a positive concept. In this lesson, we unpack this subtle argument.

Read the passage

Understand the doubt

3
Analyze the answer

Necessity is a negative concept, meaning denial of dependence, not a real attribute.
4

Understanding the nature of necessity is key to many theological issues.

The Fifth Doubt on Time and Infinite Regress
Author refutes the objection that eternal existence requires time, using infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that the Necessary Being's existence requires time by showing it leads to an impossible infinite regress.

Core Idea
Eternal existence cannot depend on time without leading to an impossible infinite chain.

This lesson presents the author's concise refutation of a common objection: that Allah's eternal existence must be in time. The argument uses a simple but powerful logical move: if eternal existence needed time, that time would also be eternal and demand more time, generating an infinite regress. The author concludes this is impossible, thus the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Recognize the doubt being addressed: that the Necessary Being's existence requires a temporal dimension or duration.
This doubt likely arises from comparing divine existence to created temporal things.
2
Follow the conditional reasoning
Trace the logical chain: if eternal existence needed time → that time would be eternal → then that eternal time would itself need another time → leading to infinite regress.
Notice how each step follows necessarily from the previous one.
3
Grasp the conclusion
See why infinite regress (tasalsul) is impossible (muhal) in this context, and how that disproves the original objection.
The impossibility is based on the absurdity of an actual infinite chain of dependent times.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الخامسة : فجوابها : أنه لو افتقر الوجود (۱) الدائم إلى مدة وزمان ، لكان ذلك الزمان دائما ، فكان يلزم افتقاره إلى زمان آخر ، ولزم التسلسل وهو محال ) فهذا تمام الشبهات ) (٢)
As for the fifth doubt: the answer to it is that if eternal existence were in need of a duration and time, then that time would be eternal, and then would require another time, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. So this completes the doubts.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the fifth objection. The objector likely argued that the Necessary Being's existence requires time. The author argues that if eternal existence depends on time, then that time would itself be eternal and require another time, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Therefore, the objection fails.

Key Terms
وجود دائم (wujud da'im)
زمان (zaman)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
محال (muhal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that eternal existence requires a temporal duration like created things.
Correction: The argument shows that positing a time for eternal existence leads to an infinite regress because that time itself would be eternal and need another time. Thus, it is not a temporal requirement but an ontological dependence that is impossible.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if eternal existence required time?
A) Time would be finite
B) Time would be eternal and require another time, leading to infinite regress
C) The existence would become temporal
D) The argument is deferred

Key Takeaway
The necessity of avoiding infinite regress shows that the Necessary Being's eternity does not require time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as though your relationship with Allah is constrained by time—rushing prayers, waiting for 'the right moment'—while this lesson shows that Allah is beyond time? What would change if you truly internalized that He is not bound by your schedule?
?
If time is contingent and cannot be a condition for the Necessary Being, then your past sins, present struggles, and future anxieties are all beneath His timeless mercy. Can you bring yourself to trust Him with your worst moment as if it were present to Him now?
?
The impossibility of infinite regress means that your existence ultimately depends on a Being who needs nothing. Does your life reflect that dependency, or do you act as though you are self-sufficient? What one habit would you need to abandon to acknowledge your complete need for Him?

Refuting the Fifth Doubt: Eternal Existence and Time
Razi refutes the fifth doubt by showing infinite regress if eternal existence required time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasoning behind the fifth doubt about eternal existence and why infinite regress is impossible.

Core Idea
Eternal existence cannot require time, or else time itself would be eternal and cause infinite regress.

After addressing previous doubts about necessity, Razi turns to the fifth doubt concerning eternal existence. Some might think that an eternal being needs a duration or time to sustain its eternity. Razi shows that this leads to an absurd infinite regress, thus refuting the doubt. Let's see his concise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two parts: a preliminary remark about necessity, then the main answer.
The first sentence is a connecting note from a previous doubt.
2
Identify the key claim
What is the consequence of eternal existence requiring time? The passage says that time would be eternal, leading to regress.
3
Trace the regress argument
If eternal existence needs time, then that time is eternal. An eternal time would require another time, ad infinitum, which is impossible.
This is a classic argument against infinite regress (tasalsul).
4
Connect to the overall lesson
Understand that this refutes the doubt. The preliminary remark about necessity being a positive concept is a technical point from earlier discussion.

First Proof: The World's Possibility via Composition
Razi presents the first proof of the world's possibility via composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first proof that the sensible world is possible of existence by itself, based on the composition of bodies from hyle and form.

Core Idea
Every composite is dependent on its parts, thus possible.

Before we can affirm that a necessary being exists distinct from the world, we must first prove that the world itself is not necessary but possible. Razi begins this chapter by stating this necessity and then presents the first of several proofs. The first proof draws on the classic Aristotelian idea that every body is composed of hyle and form, and every composite is dependent on its parts, hence contingent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the full literal translation. Let Razi's own reasoning flow before analyzing.
Notice the logical structure: a conclusion to be proven, then the proof itself.
2
Identify the two premises
The proof has two explicit premises: (1) every body is composite of hyle and form, (2) every composite is possible of existence by itself. Locate these in the text.
The first premise is referenced from an earlier chapter; the second is explained with a reason.
3
Understand why dependency implies possibility
Razi explains that a composite is possible because it depends on its parts, which are other than itself. This dependency means it is not necessary by itself. Reflect on this reasoning.
This connects to the core idea: independence is a mark of necessity, dependence marks possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع عشر
في
بيان أن العالم المحسوس ليس واجب الوجود لذاته.

اعلم أن الدليل الذي ذكرناه ، أفاد أنه حصل في الوجود موجود واجب الوجود لذاته فقط . فأما إن ذلك الواجب ( شيء ) مغاير لهذا العالم المحسوس ، فذلك لا يحصل من ذلك الدليل ، بل يجب علينا إقامة الدلالة على أن هذا العالم المحسوس ممكن الوجود لذاته ، فحينئذ يحصل لنا بعد ذلك أنه لا بد من موجود آخر ، غير هذا العالم المحسوس ، يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ونقول : الذي يدل على أن هذا العالم المحسوس ، ممكن الوجود لذاته وجوه : - ( الحجة ) الأولى : ( إن كل جسم مركب من الهيولي والصورة ، ) وكل مركب ممكن ، ينتج : أن كل جسم ممكن أما قولنا كل جسم مركب من الهيولي والصورة فقد تقدم ذكره في باب الهيولى والصورة على سبيل الاستقصاء ، وأما قولنا : كل مركب فهو ممكن لذاته ، فتقريره : أن كل مركب فهو مفتقر إلى كل واحد من أجزائه ، وكل واحد من أجزائه غيره ، وكل مركب ممكن ، فهو مفتقر إلى غيره . فهو ممكن لذاته
The Fourteenth Chapter: On clarifying that the sensible world is not necessary of existence by itself. Know that the proof we have mentioned established that there exists in existence a being that is necessary of existence by itself only. As for whether that necessary being is something different from this sensible world, that is not obtained from that proof. Rather, we must establish evidence that this sensible world is possible of existence by itself; then after that we obtain that there must be another existent, other than this sensible world, which is necessary of existence by itself. And we say: that which indicates that this sensible world is possible of existence by itself are several aspects: The first proof: (Every body is composed of hyle and form), and every composite is possible, so it follows that every body is possible. As for our statement 'every body is composed of hyle and form', it has been mentioned previously in the chapter on hyle and form exhaustively. And as for our statement 'every composite is possible by itself', its explanation: that every composite is dependent on each one of its parts, and each one of its parts is other than it, and every composite is possible, so it is dependent on other than it. Therefore it is possible by itself.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage begins the fourteenth chapter, which aims to prove that the sensible world is not necessary of existence by itself. Razi first explains that the previous proof only established that there is a necessary being (wājib al-wujūd), but did not show that this necessary being is distinct from the world. Therefore, he must now prove that the world itself is possible of existence (mumkin al-wujūd), so that it is in need of an external necessary being. He then presents the first proof: every body (jism) is composed of hyle (hayūlā) and form (ṣūra), as established earlier. And every composite (murakkab) is possible because it is dependent on its parts. Therefore, every body is possible. The explanation: a composite depends on each of its parts, each part is other than the composite, so the composite is dependent on other than itself; thus it is possible by itself. This establishes the contingency of all bodies, and hence the world.

Misconception: That the mere fact of being composite is sufficient to prove possibility.
Correction: Razi explains that it is the dependency on parts that makes a composite possible. Composition itself is just the structural description; the logical ground of possibility is the need for parts that are other than the whole.

The Need to Prove the World's Contingency and Razi's First Proof
Razi states the need to prove the sensible world is contingent and presents the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that the sensible world must be proven contingent in itself, and how the composition argument (hyle and form) establishes that contingency.

Core Idea
To show the world is not God, we must first prove it is contingent.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi clarifies a critical gap in the previous argument for a Necessary Existent. The earlier proof only demonstrated that a Necessary Existent exists, not that it is distinct from the sensible world. To make that distinction, we need a separate proof that the world itself is contingent. Razi then presents the first of several proofs: every body is composed of hyle and form, and every composite depends on its parts, making it contingent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Razi states that the earlier proof only shows there is a Necessary Existent in itself, but does not show that it is different from the sensible world. Therefore, we must prove the world is contingent to establish that distinction.
Notice that Razi is building on a previous argument; he sees a gap that needs to be filled.
2
Understand the proof structure
Razi presents a syllogism: every body is composed of hyle and form; every composite is contingent; therefore every body is contingent. Trace how these premises lead to the conclusion.
The first premise is assumed from a previous chapter; the second is justified below.
3
Examine the reasoning for the second premise
Razi explains why every composite is contingent: a composite depends on each of its parts, and each part is other than the whole. Anything that depends on something else is contingent in itself.
This reasoning connects dependence with contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع عشر في بيان أن العالم المحسوس ليس واجب الوجود لذاته. اعلم أن الدليل الذي ذكرناه، أفاد أنه حصل في الوجود موجود واجب الوجود لذاته فقط. فأما إن ذلك الواجب شيء مغاير لهذا العالم المحسوس، فذلك لا يحصل من ذلك الدليل، بل يجب علينا إقامة الدلالة على أن هذا العالم المحسوس ممكن الوجود لذاته، فحينئذ يحصل لنا بعد ذلك أنه لا بد من موجود آخر، غير هذا العالم المحسوس، يكون واجب الوجود لذاته. ونقول: الذي يدل على أن هذا العالم المحسوس، ممكن الوجود لذاته وجوه: - الحجة الأولى: إن كل جسم مركب من الهيولي والصورة، وكل مركب ممكن، ينتج: أن كل جسم ممكن. أما قولنا: كل جسم مركب من الهيولي والصورة فقد تقدم ذكره في باب الهيولى والصورة على سبيل الاستقصاء، وأما قولنا: كل مركب فهو ممكن لذاته، فتقريره: أن كل مركب فهو مفتقر إلى كل واحد من أجزائه، وكل واحد من أجزائه غيره، وكل مركب ممكن، فهو مفتقر إلى غيره. فهو ممكن لذاته.
Chapter Fourteen: On Explaining that the Sensible World is not Necessary of Existence in Itself. Know that the proof which we mentioned indicated that there exists in existence an existent that is necessary of existence in itself only. As for whether that necessary existent is something other than this sensible world, that is not obtained from that proof. Rather, we must establish the evidence that this sensible world is possible of existence in itself. Then after that, we will obtain that there must be another existent, other than this sensible world, that is necessary of existence in itself. And we say: That which indicates that this sensible world is possible of existence in itself are several aspects: The first proof: Every body is composed of hyle and form, and every composite is possible. The conclusion: every body is possible. As for our statement 'every body is composed of hyle and form', it has already been mentioned in the chapter on hyle and form in a detailed manner. And as for our statement 'every composite is possible in itself', its explanation is: every composite depends on each one of its parts, and each one of its parts is other than it, and everything that depends on something other than it is possible in itself.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the problem. The author (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) explains that the earlier proof (presumably in the previous chapter) established that there exists a Necessary Existent in itself, but it did not prove that this Necessary Existent is distinct from the sensible world. Therefore, a new proof is needed to show that the sensible world itself is contingent (possible in itself). Once that is established, we can conclude that there must be another existent, distinct from the world, that is necessary. Then Razi presents the first proof: every body is composed of hyle (prime matter) and form, and every composite is contingent. The conclusion is that every body is contingent. He then elaborates: the premise that every body is composed of hyle and form was already proven in a previous chapter. The premise that every composite is contingent is explained by the fact that a composite depends on each of its parts, and each part is other than the composite itself; whatever depends on something else is contingent in itself.

Third Proof: Bodies Are Contingent Because the Necessary Being Is One
Sanusi presents a syllogism proving bodies are contingent from the unity of the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the third kalam proof for the contingency of bodies, which relies on the unity of the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being must be one; bodies are many; therefore no body is necessary.

This lesson presents a concise syllogism that links the contingency of bodies directly to the oneness of God. The argument is elegantly simple but depends on a premise that is proven elsewhere. We will walk through its logic and see why it matters for establishing that all bodies are contingent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own wording form the foundation.
The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating the proof for unity is deferred.
2
Identify the premises and conclusion
Find the two premises: (1) The Necessary Being must be one. (2) Bodies are not one. Then note the conclusion: therefore the Necessary Being cannot be a body, so bodies are contingent.
This is a classic modus tollens argument.
3
Reflect on the deferred proof
The author acknowledges that the proof for the unity of the Necessary Being will be given later. Ask yourself: does this weaken the argument? Why might the author present it here?
Kalam often uses premise acceptance pending later proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة في بيان أن الأجسام ممكنة الوجود لذواتها: إن واجب الوجود يجب أن يكون واحدا، والأجسام ليست واحدة، فواجب الوجود لا يمكن أن يكون جسما، فالجسم لا يمكن أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته، أما بيان أن واجب الوجود يمتنع أن يكون أكثر من واحد، فالدليل عليه سيأتي في
The third proof in demonstrating that bodies are contingent in essence: The Necessary Being must be one, and bodies are not one, so the Necessary Being cannot be a body, so the body cannot be necessarily existent in essence. As for the demonstration that the Necessary Being is impossible to be more than one, its proof will come in [the chapter on the unity of God].
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a brief proof for the contingency of bodies based on the unity of the Necessary Being (wajib al-wujud). The argument is straightforward: (1) The Necessary Being must be one (unique). (2) Bodies are not one (they are multiple). Therefore, the Necessary Being cannot be a body. Consequently, no body is necessarily existent in itself; all bodies are contingent. The author notes that the proof for the uniqueness of the Necessary Being will be given later, so here the argument is presented as relying on that accepted premise. This is a common kalam argument: since the Necessary Being is unique and bodies are divisible and multiple, bodies cannot share in the attribute of necessary existence.

Correction: The author acknowledges this: the proof for unity is deferred to a later chapter. In kalam, it is acceptable to present an argument using a premise that will be proved later, as long as the logical connection is sound.

The Contingency of Bodies Through Existence-Essence Distinction
Continuation of a proof that bodies are contingent via minor and major premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument uses the distinction between existence and essence in bodies to prove that every body is contingent and dependent on an external cause.

Core Idea
If existence is distinct from essence, then existence cannot be self-sufficient.

This passage presents a key syllogistic argument for the contingency of all bodies. The author has already shown that in every body, existence is other than its essence (the minor premise). Now he proves that anything with such a distinction is necessarily possible (contingent) in itself, because its existence requires a cause separate from its essence. Follow the logical steps to see how the argument eliminates self-causation and establishes dependence on another.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the syllogism structure
The argument is a classic Aristotelian syllogism with a minor premise (every body has existence other than its essence) and a major premise (anything with that distinction is contingent). Read the passage to see how these are stated.
The minor premise is not proven here; the author references a previous chapter.
2
Analyze the proof of the major premise
The major premise is proven by a disjunctive argument. Existence is either independent of the essence or dependent on it. Work through both cases to see why only dependence is possible.
Pay attention to the assumption that existence is an accident of the essence.
3
Understand why essence cannot be its own cause
If existence depends on the essence, then the essence would be the cause. But a cause must be prior to its effect, and a thing cannot be prior to itself. This leads to impossibility.
This is a key point: self-causation is logically impossible.
4
Conclude the syllogism
Since the cause must be external, the thing is dependent on another, which is exactly what it means to be possible (contingent) in itself. Thus the conclusion holds: every body is contingent.
5
Note the deferred discussion
The author mentions that the question of whether an essence can be its own cause will be discussed later in the context of God's existence. This is a hint of things to come.
This shows how the argument fits into a broader theological framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجود لذاته ، ينتج : فكل جسم ممكن الوجود لذاته . أما الصغرى : وهي قولنا : كل جسم ، فإن وجوده غير ماهيته ، فالكلام المستقصى فيه ، قد سبق في باب الوجود وأما الكبرى : وهي أن كل ما وجوده غير ماهيته فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته ، فالدليل عليه أن ذلك الوجود، إما أن يكون غنياً عن تلك الماهية ، وإما أن يكون محتاجا إليها ، فإن كان الأول لزم أن لا يكون ذلك الوجود عارضا لتلك الماهية ) بل يكون وجودا قائما بنفسه ، لا تعلق له بتلك الماهية ، لكنا فرضنا أن ذلك الوجود عارض لتلك الماهية (1) وإن كان الثاني فنقول : كل ما كان محتاجاً إلى الغير فهو ممكن لذاته ( وكل ممكن لذاته ((٢) لا بد له ( من سبب (۳)) ، وذلك السبب إما ( تلك ) (1) الماهية أو غيرها ، لا جائز أن يكون السبب هو تلك الماهية ، لأن كل علة فهي متقدمة بوجودها ) على المعلول ، فلو كانت الماهية علة لوجود نفسها ، لكانت تلك الماهية متقدمة بوجودها ((٥)) على وجود نفسها ، وذلك محال ، فبقي أن يكون سبب ذلك الوجود شيئاً غير تلك الماهية ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو ممكن ( الوجود ((٦) لذاته ( مفتقر في الوجود إلى غيره ، فيثبت أن كل جسم فوجوده غير ماهيته ، وثبت أن كل ما كان كذلك فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته (() ، ينتج أن كل جسم فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته واعلم أن الكلام في أنه هل يعقل أن تكون ماهية الشيء علة لوجود نفسه ؟ سيأتي بالاستقصاء (۸) في باب : إن وجود الله ، هل هو نفس ماهيته أم لا ؟
Existence in itself yields: Every body is possible in its existence. As for the minor premise, which is our statement: Every body, its existence is other than its quiddity, the full discussion of it has preceded in the chapter on existence. As for the major premise, which is that everything whose existence is other than its quiddity is possible in its existence, the proof for it is that that existence is either independent of that quiddity or in need of it. If it is the first, then that existence would not be an accident of that quiddity, but would be an existence subsisting by itself, having no relation to that quiddity, but we assumed that that existence is an accident of that quiddity. And if it is the second, we say: Everything that is in need of another is possible in itself, and every possible in itself must have a cause, and that cause is either that quiddity or something else. It is not permissible that the cause be that quiddity, because every cause is prior in its existence to the effect. So if the quiddity were the cause of its own existence, then that quiddity would be prior in its existence to its own existence, and that is impossible. So it remains that the cause of that existence is something other than that quiddity, and everything that is such is possible in existence in itself, dependent in existence on another. So it is established that every body has existence other than its quiddity, and it is established that everything that is such is possible in its existence, yielding that every body is possible in its existence. And know that the discussion of whether it is conceivable for the quiddity of a thing to be the cause of its own existence will come in detail in the chapter on whether God's existence is identical to His quiddity or not.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a proof that bodies are contingent (mumkin al-wujud li dhatihi). It presents a syllogism. The minor premise (كل جسم وجوده غير ماهيته) is stated as already proven in a previous chapter on existence. The major premise (كل ما وجوده غير ماهيته فهو ممكن) is proven here by a disjunctive argument. The existence of a body is either independent of its essence or dependent. If independent, it would not be an accident of the essence, contradicting the assumption that existence is an accident (as per the minor premise). If dependent, then existence is in need of a cause. That cause cannot be the essence itself because that would require the essence to be prior to itself, which is impossible. Therefore, the cause must be external, making the body contingent. The argument concludes that every body is contingent. The author also notes that the question of whether an essence can be its own cause will be discussed later in the context of God's existence.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
ماهية (mahiyya)
ممكن (mumkin)
علة ('illa)
عارض ('arid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument is circular because the minor premise is not proven in this passage.
Correction: The author explicitly states that the minor premise was proven in a previous chapter, so the argument is not circular; it builds on prior established knowledge.

Checkpoint
Why can the essence of a body not be the cause of its own existence, according to the proof?
A) Because existence is an accident and cannot be caused by a substance
B) Because the cause must be prior to the effect, and nothing can be prior to itself
C) Because the essence is only a mental concept and not real
D) Because the essence already contains existence

Key Takeaway
The distinction between existence and essence in a thing entails that its existence depends on an external cause, making the thing contingent.

Third Argument: The Oneness of the Necessary and the Multiplicity of Bodies
A brief deductive argument that bodies are contingent because the Necessary is one and bodies are many.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the Third Argument for the contingency of bodies, which deduces that since the Necessary Existent must be one and bodies are many, no body can be necessary in itself.

Core Idea
Bodies are many, the Necessary is one — therefore bodies cannot be necessary.

The author presents a concise proof that bodies are contingent based on the oneness of the Necessary Existent. The argument is simple: if the Necessary Existent must be one (a premise to be proven later) and bodies are evidently many, then no body can be the Necessary Existent. This lesson unpacks the logic and notes the author's honest admission that a key premise is deferred.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the conclusion and the two premises.
The passage has a clear if-then structure.
2
Identify the two premises
Identify the major premise (the Necessary must be one) and the minor premise (bodies are many). Observe which premise the author says will be proven later.
The major premise is deferred; the minor premise is taken as observed.
3
Assess the logical flow
Consider the argument's validity and strength at this stage. Why does the author present it now if the key premise is unproven?
This argument serves as a provisional inference pending later proof.

This passage presents a short proof that bodies are contingent. The author also notes that the investigations on the oneness of the Necessary Existent are extensive and have their own proper place.

The Fourth Argument: Composition of Corporeality and Individuation
A detailed proof using the distinction between shared corporeality and individualing properties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between shared corporeality and individuation demonstrates that every body is contingent and requires an external cause.

Core Idea
Bodies are contingent because their composition demands an external cause.

This lesson presents a sophisticated proof for the contingency of bodies, building on the idea that each body is composed of a shared nature (corporeality) and a unique individuating property. The argument proceeds by eliminating the possibilities that either component causes the other, leaving only the need for an external cause. This reinforces the central thesis that all bodies are possible in themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without pausing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the terms 'جسمية' (corporeality) and 'تعين' (individuation).
2
Identify the trilemma
The author presents three possibilities: corporeality entails individuation, individuation entails corporeality, or neither entails the other. Find these three in the text.
The Arabic uses 'إما أن تكون ... وإما أن ... وإما أن ...'
3
Understand why the first two are false
The author refutes the first two possibilities. Trace the reasoning: the first leads to all bodies being identical, the second because an attribute cannot cause its subject.
The commentary clarifies these refutations.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since neither component causes the other, their conjunction must be caused by something external. This makes every individuated body contingent.
The author notes that this proof relies on individuation being a positive concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة في بيان أن الأجسام ممكنة الوجود لذواتها : إن الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية ، ومتباينة في التعين ، وما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة ، وكل واحد منها مركب من الجسيمة التي بها يشارك سائر الأجسام ، ومن التعين الذي به يمتاز عن سائر الأجسام. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إما أن تكون الجسمية مستلزمة لذلك التعيين ، وإما أن يكون ذلك التعين مستلزما للجسمية ، وإما أن لا يكون كل واحد منهما مستلزما للآخر . والأول باطل ، وإلا لزم أن يكون كل جسم موصوفا بذلك التعيين ، فيكون كل جسم ذلك المعين ، هذا خلف . والثاني أيضا باطل ، وذلك لأن تعين ذلك الجسم نعت من نعوته ، وصفة من صفاته ، ونعت الشيء يفتقر إلى ذلك الشيء ، والمفتقر إلى الشيء لا يكون علة ، فثبت أنه لا الجسمية علة لذلك التعين ، ولا ذلك التعين علة للجسمية ، ولما بطل هذان القسمان وجب أن يكون اجتماع هذين القيدين معلول علة منفصلة ، وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن يكون كل جسم معين معلولا لعلة منفصلة ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو ممكن لذاته واعلم أن هذه الحجة ، مبينة على أن التعين مفهوم ثبوتي، والكلام فيه قد تقدم في هذا الباب
The fourth proof that bodies are possible in existence in themselves: Bodies are equal in corporeality and different in individuation, and that by which they share is not that by which they differ. Each one is composed of the corporeality by which it shares with other bodies, and the individuation by which it is distinguished from other bodies. If this is established, we say: Either corporeality entails that individuation, or that individuation entails corporeality, or neither entails the other. The first is false, otherwise every body would be described by that individuation, so every body would be that individual, which is absurd. The second is also false, because the individuation of that body is a quality and attribute of it, and the attribute of a thing depends on that thing, and what depends on something cannot be a cause. So it is established that neither corporeality is the cause of that individuation, nor that individuation is the cause of corporeality. Since these two possibilities are false, it is necessary that the conjunction of these two determinations be an effect of a separate cause. And if so, then every individuated body must be an effect of a separate cause, and everything that is such is possible in itself. And know that this proof is based on the fact that individuation is a positive concept, and the discussion about it has preceded in this chapter.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage contains a complex proof for the contingency of bodies based on their composition from a shared nature (corporeality, jismiyya) and individualing properties (ta'ayyun). The argument considers three possibilities: (1) corporeality entails individuation, (2) individuation entails corporeality, (3) neither entails the other. The first is false because if corporeality entailed a specific individuation, then every body would have that same individuation, making all bodies identical—contradicting observation. The second is false because an attribute (individuation) depends on the subject (the body), and what depends on something cannot be the cause of that something. So only the third possibility remains: neither causes the other. Therefore, their conjunction (the body as a unity of corporeality and individuation) must be caused by something external. Hence, every individuated body is an effect of an external cause, making it contingent (mumkin li dhatihi). The author notes that this proof presupposes that individuation is a positive attribute, which was discussed earlier.

Key Terms
جسمية (jismiyya)
تعين (ta'ayyun)
مشاركة (musharaka)
ممايزة (mumayaza)
علة ('illa)
معلول (ma'lul)
ممكن (mumkin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the argument assumes without proof that individuation is a positive attribute.
Correction: The author explicitly notes at the end that this proof depends on individuation being a positive concept, which was established earlier in the chapter.

Checkpoint
In the Fourth Argument, why is it impossible for individuation to be the cause of corporeality?
A) Individuation is a quality that depends on the body, and what depends cannot cause the subject
B) Individuation is not a real attribute
C) Corporeality is more fundamental
D) It would lead to an infinite regress

Key Takeaway
Because neither shared corporeality nor individuation can cause the other, every body's composition requires an external cause, proving its contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Your body is a composite of a shared nature (humanity) and your unique individuality. The argument shows this composition cannot explain itself. Does your life show that you acknowledge this dependence on Allah every moment, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient?

The proof demands that an external cause must bring together your generic humanity and your specific identity. Have you ever truly felt that your existence is a gift sustained by Allah, or do you take it for granted?

If every individuated body requires an external cause, then your very distinctiveness—the 'you' that is different from everyone else—points directly to a Creator. How does that reality change the way you think about your purpose and your accountability to Him?

Fifth Argument: Divisibility Implies Contingency
A three-step syllogism proving bodies are contingent through divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divisibility of bodies leads to the conclusion that they are contingent (possible) in themselves.

Core Idea
Extension leads to divisibility, divisibility to composition, and composition to contingency.

This lesson presents the fifth proof for the contingency of bodies. It is a concise syllogism that connects the physical property of extension to metaphysical possibility. By tracing the logical chain from extension to composition to contingency, we see how Islamic theologians argued that the material world cannot be necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the three premises
The passage presents three premises: (1) Every extended thing is divisible, (2) Every divisible thing is composite, (3) Every composite thing is contingent. Find each one and notice how they are linked.
The third premise refers to an earlier proof.
3
Understand the key terms
Familiarize yourself with the key Arabic terms: mutahayyiz (extended), munqasim (divisible), murakkab (composite), mumkin (contingent). Their meanings are essential for the logic.
The term 'juz' la yatajazza' (indivisible part) refers to atomism.
4
Reflect on the argument's structure
Notice that the proof is a chain: extension → divisibility → composition → contingency. Ask yourself why each step is valid based on the text.
The first step relies on proofs against atomism, not on direct observation.
5
Consider the overall purpose
Think about how this argument fits into the larger project of proving God's existence. By showing bodies are contingent, we establish that they need a cause, which leads to the Necessary Being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة في إثبات أن الأجسام ممكنة لذواتها : أن نقول : إن كل متحيز منقسم بالدلائل المذكورة في مسألة نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ، وكل منقسم فهو مركب ، وكل مركب فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته بالدليل الذي سبق
The fifth proof that bodies are possible in themselves: We say: Every extended thing is divisible by the proofs mentioned in the issue of negating the indivisible part, and every divisible thing is composite, and every composite thing is possible in existence in itself by the proof that preceded.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents a very concise argument for the contingency of bodies. It proceeds in three steps: (1) Every extended body (mutahayyiz) is divisible, as proven in the discussion against atomism (the indivisible part). (2) Everything divisible is composite (murakkab), i.e., composed of parts. (3) Everything composite is contingent (mumkin al-wujud li dhatihi), a conclusion that relies on a previous proof (presumably the earlier argument that composites require a cause and are therefore possible). The argument thus reduces the contingency of bodies to their extension and divisibility.

Key Terms

منقسم (munqasim)
مركب (murakkab)
ممكن (mumkin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it uses an earlier proof for the contingency of composites.
Correction: The earlier proof is a separate argument; this proof simply invokes it as established, not as part of the same chain.

Checkpoint

B) Proofs against the existence of indivisible parts (atomism)

Key Takeaway
The contingency of bodies is deduced from their extension and divisibility, showing that the material world is not necessary.

Sixth Proof: Bodies Are Possible (Imkān al-Ajsām)
Sixth proof for possibility of bodies via attribute reception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical proof that bodies are possible in themselves based on their quiddities accepting attributes and the principle that from one only one can issue.

Core Idea
A quiddity that causes two effects violates the principle ‘from one only one can issue’.

This lesson presents the sixth proof for the possibility of bodies, used by philosophers. The argument is based on the observation that the quiddities of bodies receive attributes, which implies they are not necessary. By applying the principle that a single cause cannot produce two distinct effects, the proof demonstrates that bodies must be possible in themselves. Understanding this argument helps clarify the metaphysical foundations of contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the proof is structured as a conditional argument.
The proof relies on three premises.
2
Identify the two effects
Identify what two things would be necessitated if the quiddity were also necessary.
Reception of attributes and necessary existence.
3
Understand the principle al-wāḥid lā yaṣduru ʿanhu illā al-wāḥid
This principle states that a single cause can produce only one effect. Reflect on why this is used here.
This is a key metaphysical axiom.
4
Review the three premises
List the three premises on which the proof is based. They are all essential for the argument to work.
The proof fails if any premise is rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة في إثبات أن الأجسام ممكنة الوجود لذواتها : أن نقول : لا شك أن ماهيات الأجسام قابلة للصفات والأعراض ، فهذه الماهيات مقتضية لهذا القبول ، فلو كانت مقتضية لوجوب الوجود أيضاً ، لزم كون تلك الماهيات مقتضية لأمرين : أحدهما : قبول الصفات . والثاني : وجوب الوجود . وهذا محال لما ثبت أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد ، وهذا الدليل مبني على أن القبول صفة وجودية وعلى أن الوجوب صفة وجودية وعلى أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد .
The sixth proof for establishing that bodies are possible in existence in themselves: we say: there is no doubt that the quiddities (māhiyyāt) of bodies are receptive to attributes and accidents, so these quiddities necessitate this reception. If they also necessitated necessary existence, then those quiddities would necessitate two things: one: reception of attributes, and two: necessary existence. This is impossible because it has been established that from the one only one can issue. This proof is based on the fact that reception is an existential attribute, and that necessary existence is an existential attribute, and that from the one only one can issue.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof for the possibility of bodies (imkān al-ajsām) in themselves, used by philosophers. The argument begins by noting that the quiddities (māhiyyāt) of bodies are receptive to attributes and accidents. This reception is necessitated by the quiddity itself. If, in addition, the quiddity also necessitated necessary existence (wujūb al-wujūd), then the quiddity would be the cause of two distinct effects: (1) the reception of attributes, and (2) necessary existence. However, a single essence cannot produce more than one effect, as the principle 'from the one only one can issue' (al-wāḥid lā yaṣduru ʿanhu illā al-wāḥid) has been established. Therefore, it is impossible for the same quiddity to necessitate both reception and necessary existence. Since it does necessitate reception, it cannot necessitate necessary existence; hence bodies are not necessary but possible. The proof rests on three premises: that reception is an existential attribute (ṣifa wujūdiyya), that necessary existence is an existential attribute, and the principle of emanation that from one only one can issue.

Key Terms
ماهيات (quiddities)
مقتضية (necessitating)
قبول الصفات (reception of attributes)
وجوب الوجود (necessary existence)
الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد (from the one only one can issue)
صفة وجودية (existential attribute)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of two effects from one essence applies only to God.
Correction: The principle 'from the one only one can issue' is used here as a general metaphysical principle, not limited to the divine.

Checkpoint
What is the central premise of the sixth proof that prevents a quiddity from necessitating both reception of attributes and necessary existence?
A) Bodies are composite and thus contingent.
B) From the one only one can issue.
C) Change is impossible for necessary beings.
D) Attributes are not real.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of bodies is proven by the fact that their quiddities cause one effect (reception of attributes), and a single cause cannot produce two effects, so they cannot cause necessary existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your existence is necessary and self-sufficient, forgetting that you are contingent upon Allah every moment? What would change if you truly internalized that your essence does not necessitate existence?
?
The proof shows that a single essence cannot produce two effects. How does this principle reflect tawhid? Examine your worship: are you striving to make it solely for Allah, or are you trying to serve two masters without realizing it?
