Seventh Proof: Bodies Need an External Cause
Seventh proof: bodies are possible because they are never free from externally caused accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh proof that bodies are possible because they are never free from accidental defects that depend on an external cause.

Core Idea
Bodies cannot exist without accidental defects that point to an external cause.

The seventh proof builds on the idea that bodies are never free from certain accidental deficiencies like quantity and location. Since these accidents depend on something external to the body's essence, the body itself is never free from dependence. This leads to the conclusion that bodies are possible (mumkin) in themselves, requiring a separate cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow and key terms.
2
Identify the key premises
The argument has two main premises: (a) every body is never free from accidental defects (quantities and location), and (b) the cause of these defects is not the bodily essence itself.
3
Understand why the cause is not the essence
If the bodily essence caused the defects, all bodies would have exactly the same attributes, which is false. Therefore, the cause must be external.
4
Trace the conclusion
Since the body is never free from these externally caused defects, it depends on an external cause itself, making it possible (mumkin) rather than necessary.
5
Reflect on the proof's significance
This proof shows that bodies are contingent, pointing to the need for a Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة في بيان أن الأجسام ممكنة الوجود لذواتها : أن تقول : كل جسم فإنه يمتنع خلوه عن نقص في الأعراض ، وهي المقادير ، والحصول في الأحياز، والمقتضى الحصول هذه الأعراض ليست هي الجسمية ، وإلا لزم مساواة الجسمية للأجسام في هذه الصفات ، فالمقتضى لها غير ذواتها . فنقول : الجسم ممتنع الخلو عن هذه الصفات ، وحصول هذه الصفات متوقف على الغير، فالجسم ممتنع الخلو عما يفتقر إلى السبب المنفصل ، وما كان كذلك كان مفتقرا إلى سبب منفصل ، فيكون ممكنا لذاته . فهذا مجموع الطرق التي يقدر الفيلسوف على التمسك بها في إثبات أن الأجسام ممكنة الوجود لذواتها .
The seventh proof for explaining that bodies are possible in existence in themselves: that you say: every body is impossible to be free from deficiency in the accidents, which are: quantities, and existing in places, and the cause of the occurrence of these accidents is not bodily essence (al-jismiyya), otherwise it would necessitate that bodily essence be equal to the bodies in these attributes. So the cause of them is other than their essences. Then we say: the body is impossible to be free from these attributes, and the occurrence of these attributes depends on something else, so the body is impossible to be free from what is in need of a separate cause, and whatever is like that is in need of a separate cause, so it is possible in itself. So this is the sum of the methods that the philosopher is able to employ in establishing that bodies are possible in existence in themselves.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof for the possibility of bodies, again attributed to the philosophers. The argument proceeds in steps: (1) Every body is necessarily never free from certain accidental deficiencies (naqṣ fī al-aʿrāḍ), specifically quantities (al-maqādīr) and being located in places (al-ḥuṣūl fī al-aḥyāz). (2) The cause that brings about these accidents cannot be the essence of body itself (al-jismiyya), because if it were, then every body would have exactly the same attributes (since the essence would be equal in all), which is not the case. Therefore, the cause of these accidents is external to the body's essence. (3) Since the body is never free from these accidents (they always accompany it), and these accidents depend on an external cause, the body itself is never free from that which depends on a separate cause. (4) Anything that necessarily coexists with dependence on an external cause is itself dependent on that cause. (5) Thus the body is dependent on a separate cause, making it possible (mumkin) in itself rather than necessary. The passage concludes by noting that these (the sixth and seventh proofs) are the methods available to the philosopher for proving the possibility of bodies.

Key Terms
يمتنع خلوه
نقص في الأعراض
المقادير
الحصول في الأحياز
المقتضى
الجسمية
سبب منفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only the accidents are caused, not the substance itself.
Correction: The argument shows that the body is never free from caused accidents, so it indirectly depends on the same external cause, making the substance itself dependent.

Checkpoint
Why does the seventh proof claim that the cause of the accidents (quantities and location) is not the bodily essence itself?
A) Because the essence is simple and cannot produce multiplicity.
B) If it were, all bodies would possess the same attributes, which is false.
C) Because accidents are not real.
D) Because the essence is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof demonstrates that bodies are possible because they are always accompanied by accidental defects that depend on an external cause, thereby establishing their need for a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body is never free from accidental defects like size and location. Are you more attached to your changing physical form than to the One who sustains it? How does this proof challenge your daily obsession with your body's appearance and health?
?
The proof shows that the cause of your body's attributes is not the body itself. How often do you attribute the successes and conditions of your life to your own efforts rather than recognizing the external cause (Allah) that grants them moment by moment? What would it take for you to live in constant awareness of that dependence?
?
If your body is possible (mumkin) and needs a cause to exist, then your entire existence is a gift sustained by another. Do you feel the weight of that dependence, or do you take your existence for granted? Write one practical action you will take today to remind yourself of your utter contingency upon Allah.

The Mutakallimun Method: Origination Implies Possibility
Presents the eighth proof for possibility of bodies via the mutakallimūn method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimūn prove the possibility of bodies by first establishing their temporal origination and then applying the universal principle that every originated thing is possible in itself.

Core Idea
Origination implies possibility — the mutakallimūn's key insight.

In this final proof for the possibility of bodies, the mutakallimūn (speculative theologians) take a distinct approach. Rather than appealing to the intrinsic nature of quiddities or accidents, they first demonstrate that bodies are originated (muḥdatha) — that they come into existence after not existing. Only then do they apply the universal principle: every originated thing is possible in itself. This passage not only presents that proof but also concludes the entire section on establishing the Necessary Existent through the possibility of essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the proof that bodies are originated, then the principle linking origination to possibility, and finally the summary and conclusion.
The passage is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the two-stage reasoning
The mutakallimūn first prove bodies are originated (using their own arguments from accidents and change), then they use the principle that 'every originated thing is possible in itself'. See how these two steps together establish the possibility of bodies.
This distinguishes their method from the philosophical proofs.
3
Understand the closure of the section
The passage ends with a summary and a supplication, marking the conclusion of the entire section on proving the Necessary Existent through the possibility of essences. Reflect on why the author includes this proof after the philosophical ones.
Think about the different methodologies within Islamic theology.

الحجة الثامنة وهي طريقة المتكلمين : وذلك أنهم يقيمون الأدلة على أن الأجسام محدثة . ثم يقولون : وكل محدث فإنه ممكن الوجود لذاته . فهذه جملة الوجوه التي يمكن الرجوع إليها في إثبات كون الأجسام ممكنة الوجود لذواتها وههنا آخر الكلام في شرح إثبات العلم بواجب الوجود ، بالاستدلال بإمكان الذوات ، والله ولي التوفيق
The eighth proof, and it is the method of the mutakallimūn: that they provide proofs that bodies are originated (muḥdatha). Then they say: and every originated thing is possible in existence in itself. So this is the sum of the aspects that can be referred to in establishing that bodies are possible in existence in themselves. And here ends the discourse on explaining the establishment of knowledge of the Necessary Existent (wājib al-wujūd), by inferring through the possibility of essences (imkān al-dhawāt). And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth and final proof for the possibility of bodies, which is specifically the method of the mutakallimūn (speculative theologians). Unlike the previous philosophical arguments, the mutakallimūn first establish that bodies are originated (muḥdatha), meaning they come into existence after not existing. They have their own independent proofs for the origination of bodies (likely based on accidents and change). Once they have shown that bodies are originated, they then apply the principle: every originated thing is possible in existence in itself (kull muḥdath fa-innahu mumkin al-wujūd li-dhātih). Therefore, bodies are possible. The passage then concludes the entire section on proving the existence of the Necessary Existent (wājib al-wujūd) through the argument from the possibility of essences (imkān al-dhawāt). The author notes that these are all the ways that can be used, and ends with a supplication.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the mutakallimūn assume bodies are possible without prior proof, or confuse their method with philosophical proofs that rely on quiddities or accidents.
Correction: The mutakallimūn first prove temporal origination (ḥudūth) through separate arguments (e.g., change in accidents), and then use origination as the basis for possibility. This two-step reasoning distinguishes their method from philosophical approaches that begin with the nature of quiddities.

Key Takeaway

The Eighth Proof: The Method of the Mutakallimun
Al-Razi summarizes the eighth proof that bodies are contingent and concludes the section.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the mutakallimun's proof for the Necessary Existent, which relies on the contingency of bodies.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun prove God by showing that bodies are contingent, requiring a necessary cause.

Al-Razi presents the eighth and final proof in this section, which is the classic argument of the kalam theologians. It is elegantly simple: bodies are created (hadith), and everything created is contingent in itself (mumkin al-wujud). Consequently, bodies require a necessary being to bring them into existence. This lesson unpacks that succinct argument and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two premises and the concluding statement.
The passage is short but dense. Pay attention to the terminology.
2
Identify the two premises of the proof
The proof has two explicit premises: (1) bodies are originated (hadith), and (2) every originated thing is possible in itself (mumkin al-wujud li-dhatihi). Identify these in the text.
These premises lead to the conclusion that bodies are possible, which then requires a Necessary Existent.
3
Reflect on the conclusion and the invocation
Al-Razi says this is the sum of the aspects used to prove bodies are possible, and here ends the discourse. The invocation 'And God is the grantor of success' signals the completion of this section.
This helps to see the overall structure of the volume.

Objection to the Equality of Bodies in Quiddity and the Response
Al-Razi presents an objection to the equality of bodies in quiddity and begins a response by analyzing three aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the premise that all bodies are equal in quiddity, and how the response uses a threefold analysis of magnitude, receptive essence, and relation.

Core Idea
The objection challenges the key kalam premise by comparing bodies to accidents, which differ despite sharing a generic category.

This passage presents a crucial objection to the argument that all bodies share the same quiddity, which is a cornerstone of the kalam proof for God's existence. Al-Razi first explains the difficulty, then introduces a philosophical objection using the analogy of accidents, and finally begins to outline a response by distinguishing three aspects of bodily existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Try to identify the main objection being raised against the proof.
Notice the analogy with accidents (الصفات) that differ in specific quiddity despite sharing the category 'accident'.
2
Understand the analogy with accidents
The objector argues that just as accidents (e.g., black, white) share the generic property of being accidents but differ in their specific quiddities, bodies may share corporeality (الحجمية) and occupying space (التحيز) yet differ in their specific corporeal natures.
3
Note the strengthening via magnitudes
The objection is strengthened by noting that, according to philosophers, magnitudes (المقادير) are accidents and body is the receptive essence for them.
This reinforces the idea that bodies can be differentiated by their specific receptivities.
4
Examine the threefold analysis
The response begins by distinguishing three things: the magnitude itself (المقبول), the essence's receptivity to that magnitude (القابلية ككون ذات الجسم قابلة), and the relation between them. This analytical framework is the starting point of the response.
The passage cuts off before completing the response, but the threefold distinction is key.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this objection undermines the premise that all bodies are equal in complete quiddity, which is essential for the kalam proof. The response attempts to salvage the proof by analyzing the components of body.
This shows the dialectical nature of kalam argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الخامس عشر: اعلم أن مدار هذا الدليل على إثبات أن الأجسام متساوية في تمام ماهياتها، وهذا مطلوب صعب الإلزام. فإن لقائل أن يقول: كما أن الصفات متساوية في كونها صفات، ثم إنها مختلفة بحسب ماهياتها المخصوصة، مثل كونها سوادا وبياضا وحلاوة وحموضة، فكذلك لا يبعد كون الأجسام متساوية في عموم الحجمية والتحيز، ثم إنها تكون مختلفة بحسب ماهياتها المخصوصة، فجسمية النار مخالفة لجسمية الأرض، وجسمية كل واحد منهما مخالفة لجسمية الفلك، وإذا كان هذا الاحتمال قائما، فإنه يلزم استواء الأجسام في عموم الجسمية استواؤها في ماهياتها المخصوصة، والذي يقوي هذا السؤال: أن المقادير من باب الأعراض عند الفلاسفة، والجسم عبارة عن الذات القابلة لهذه المقادير. وإذا ظهر هذا فنقول: حصل ههنا أمور ثلاثة: المقبول وهو المقدار، وكون ذات الجسم قابلة لذلك المقدار، وهذه القابلية نسبة مخصوصة بين ذات القابل وبين هذا المقدار، وتلك الذات المحكوم عليها بكونها قابلة لهذا المقدار. فنقول: أما المقادير فلا شك أنها
The fifteenth objective: Know that the pivot of this proof is establishing that bodies are equal in their complete quiddity, and this is a difficult claim to enforce. For a proponent may say: Just as accidents are equal in being accidents, then they differ according to their specific quiddities, such as being black, white, sweet, and sour, so too it is not unlikely that bodies are equal in the generality of corporeality and occupation of space (tahayyuz), then they differ according to their specific quiddities. Thus the corporeality of fire is different from the corporeality of earth, and the corporeality of each of them is different from the corporeality of the celestial sphere. If this possibility stands, then it necessitates that the equality of bodies in the generality of corporeality is [also] their equality in their specific quiddities. And what strengthens this question is that magnitudes (maqadir) belong to the category of accidents according to the philosophers, and body is an expression for the essence receptive to these magnitudes. When this is clear, we say: Three things obtain here: the receptacle, which is the magnitude; the fact that the essence of the body is receptive to that magnitude; and this receptivity is a specific relation between the essence of the receptacle and this magnitude; and that essence is judged to be receptive to this magnitude. So we say: As for magnitudes, there is no doubt that they...
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the key premise that all bodies are equal in their complete quiddity (tamam al-mahiyya), which is central to the kalam proof for the existence of God. The objector uses an analogy with accidents: even though all accidents share the generic category of being accidents, they differ in their specific quiddities (e.g., black vs. white). Similarly, bodies may share in the generic properties of corporeality (al-hajmiyya) and occupying space (al-tahayyuz), but could differ in their specific corporeal natures (e.g., fire, earth, celestial sphere). If this possibility holds, then the argument that all bodies are equal in quiddity is undermined. The author then strengthens the objection by noting that, according to philosophers, magnitudes are accidents, and the body is the substance receptive to these magnitudes. This leads to an analysis of three things: the magnitude itself, the essence of the body being receptive, and the relation of receptivity. The passage ends with the beginning of a response, stating 'as for magnitudes, there is no doubt that they...', which is cut off at the end of the page.

Correction: The objection does not deny that bodies are contingent; it questions the premise that all bodies are identical in quiddity.

The Pivot of the Proof and the Difficulty of Essence Identity
The author states the difficulty of proving bodies equal in essence, presents an objection, and analyzes three aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key premise of a proof for God's existence that depends on bodies being identical in essence and why this premise is contested by an objection from the analogy of attributes.

Core Idea
The proof that bodies are identical in essence is not straightforward; an objector can argue from attributes and accidents.

The author introduces a proof for God's existence that hinges on establishing that all bodies share the same essential nature. He immediately warns that this claim is difficult to impose on an opponent. In this lesson, we examine the objection and the author's initial analysis, which reveals the complexity of the problem.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pivot of the proof
Read the first sentence and note that the entire proof rests on establishing that bodies are equal in their complete essences (تمام ماهياتها).
This is the central claim that the author will problematize.
2
Understand the objection from attributes
An objector argues that just as attributes share a general category but differ in specific essences (e.g., blackness vs. whiteness), bodies might share extension but differ in their specific body-natures (fire, earth, celestial sphere).
The objector strengthens this by noting that magnitudes are accidents, and body is the substance receptive to them.
3
Analyze the three aspects
The author identifies three things: the magnitude (المقدار), the body's receptivity (قابلية), and the relation between them. He argues that different things can share some concomitants, so bodies could differ in specific realities yet share receptivity.
This analysis shows that the objection does not necessarily force equality in essence.
4
Grasp why the claim is extremely difficult
The author concludes that what is known about body is only its reception of three dimensions; its specific essence is unknown. Hence, we cannot judge similarity or difference by original disposition, making the verification of the premise extremely difficult.
This sets the stage for the author's own resolution in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القصات الخامس عشر إثباتها إليه العالم يروي حول بناء على التمسك بامكان الصفات اعلم : أن مدار هذا الدليل على إثبات أن الأجسام متساوية في تمام ماهياتها ، وهذا مطلوب صعب الإلزام . فإن لقائل أن يقول : كما أن الصفات متساوية في كونها صفات ، ثم إنها مختلفة بحسب ماهياتها المخصوصة ، مثل كونها سوادا وبياضا وحلاوة وحموضة ، فكذلك لا يبعد كون الأجسام متساوية في عموم الحجمية والتحيز ، ثم إنها تكون مختلفة بحسب ماهياتها المخصوصة ، فجسمية النار مخالفة الجسمية الأرض ، وجسمية كل واحد منهما مخالفة الجسمية الفلك ، وإذا كان هذا الاحتمال قائما ، فإنه يلزم استواء الأجسام في عموم الجسمية استواؤها في ماهياتها المخصوصة ، والذي يقوي هذا السؤال : أن المقادير من باب الأعراض عند الفلاسفة ، والجسم عبارة عن الذات القابلة لهذه المقادير . وإذا ظهر هذا فنقول : حصل ههنا أمور ثلاثة : المقبول وهو المقدار ، وكون ذات الجسم قابلة لذلك المقدار ، وهذه القابلية نسبة مخصوصة بين ذات القابل وبين هذا المقدار ، وتلك الذات المحكوم عليها بكونها قابلة لهذا المقدار . فنقول : أما المقادير فلا شك أنها ماهيات متساوية ، في كونها مقادير ، وأما كون ذوات الأجسام قابلة لهما . فهذا أيضاً معقول مشترك فيها بين الكل، إلا أنه ثبت في علوم العقل أن الأشياء المختلفة لا يمتنع اشتراكها في بعض اللوازم ، فيمتنع كون ذات الأجسام مختلفة بحقائقها المخصوصة ، ومتباينة بماهياتها المعينة ، ومع ذلك فإنها تكون مشتركة في هذه القابلية ، فثبت بهذه الدلالة أنه لا يلزم تساوي في الأجسام في القبول الذي هو الحجمية والمقدار، وفي كونها بأسرها قابلة لـه ، وكون ذواتها المعينة وحقائقها المخصوصة متساوية . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : المعلوم عندنا من الجسم أنه شيء يلزمه قبول المقادير الثلاثة ، فأما أن ذلك بحسب ماهيته المخصوصة ما هو ؟ فغير معلوم عندنا ، وإن لم يكن معلوما عندنا لم نقـدر على أن نحكم بمقتضى الفطرة الأصلية بكونها متماثلة ومختلفة . فيثبت بهذا البيان ان إثبات كون الجسم متماثل في تمام حقائقه المخصوصة، أمر في غاية الصعوبة . والذي حصلناه في هذا الباب : أن نقول : لا شك أن الأجسام متساوية في طبيعة الحجمية والمقدار ، وهذا القدر أمر معلوم بالبديهة ، ثم نقول : هذه الأجسام التي علمنا كونها متساوية في طبيعة الحجمية نعلم أيضاً من حالها كون كل واحد منها مغاير للآخر ، ومخالفا لـه في تعينه الشخصي ، وهذا أيضاً معلوم . ثم نقول : هذه الأشياء المتساوية في الحجمية ، المتباينة في التعين والتشخص ، إما أن تكون مختلفة بالماهية ، أو لا تكون . فإن لم تكن مختلفة بالماهية فحينئذ يصح قولنا : إن الأجسام متماثلة في تمـام مـاهـيـاتهـا وحقائقها . وذلك هو المطلوب . وأما إن قلنا : إنها مختلفة بالماهية ، فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير حصل الاستواء بينهما في الحجمية ، والاختلاف في الحقيقة ، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة ، فوجب أن تكون بحجميتها وبحيزها مغايرة لتلك الحقائق المخصوصة التي لأجلها حصل الاختلاف فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير ينقسم ذلك إلى ثلاثة أقسام، لا مزيد عليها ، لأنه إما أن يقال :
The fifteenth section: Proving [the existence] of the Creator of the world, based on adhering to the possibility of attributes. Know that the pivot of this proof is on establishing that bodies are equal in the entirety of their essences, and this is a difficult claim to impose. For an objector may say: Just as attributes are equal in being attributes, then they differ according to their specific essences, such as being blackness, whiteness, sweetness, and sourness, so likewise it is not unlikely that bodies are equal in the generality of being extended and occupying space, then they differ according to their specific essences. So the bodyness of fire is different from the bodyness of earth, and the bodyness of each one of them is different from the bodyness of the celestial sphere. And if this possibility stands, then it would necessitate that the equality of bodies in the generality of bodyness [implies] their equality in their specific essences. And what strengthens this question is that magnitudes are of the category of accidents according to the philosophers, and the body is an expression of the substance receptive to these magnitudes. When this is clear, we say: Three things occur here: the receptive, which is magnitude; the fact that the body's substance is receptive to that magnitude; and this receptivity is a specific relation between the receptive substance and this magnitude; and that substance judged as being receptive to this magnitude. So we say: As for magnitudes, there is no doubt that they are essences equal in being magnitudes. And as for the fact that the substances of bodies are receptive to them, this too is a common intelligible among all of them, except that it has been established in the rational sciences that different things are not prevented from sharing in some concomitants. So it is prevented that the substances of bodies are different in their specific realities and distinct in their particular essences, and yet they are shared in this receptivity. So it is established by this indication that it does not necessitate equality in bodies in the receptivity which is extension and magnitude, and in all of them being receptive to it, and their particular substances and specific realities being equal. If you have understood this, we say: What is known to us about the body is that it is a thing that necessitates the reception of the three magnitudes. As for whether that is according to its specific essence, what is it? It is unknown to us. And if it is unknown to us, we are not able to judge by the original disposition that they are similar or different. So it is established by this explanation that establishing that the body is similar in all its specific realities is extremely difficult. And what we have attained in this chapter is that we say: There is no doubt that bodies are equal in the nature of extension and magnitude, and this amount is known intuitively. Then we say: These bodies that we know are equal in the nature of extension, we also know from their state that each one of them is other than the other and different from it in its individual determination, and this too is known. Then we say: These things that are equal in extension and distinct in determination and individuation, either they are different in essence or not. If they are not different in essence, then our saying is correct: that bodies are similar in the entirety of their essences and realities. And that is the desired [conclusion]. But if we say that they are different in essence, then we say: On this supposition, equality between them in extension occurs, and difference in reality, and that by which there is participation is other than that by which there is difference. So it must be that by their extension and occupation of space they are other than those specific realities because of which difference occurs. Then we say: On this supposition, it divides into three divisions, no more than that, because either it is said:
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the difficulty of proving that bodies are identical in their complete essences, which is a key premise in a proof for the existence of God. The author begins by stating that the proof relies on establishing that bodies are equal (متساوية) in their entire essences (تمام ماهياتها), and notes that this is difficult to impose on an opponent. He then presents an objection: just as attributes share the general category of being attributes but differ in specific essences, so too bodies might share in general extension (الحجمية) and occupation of space (التحيز) but differ in specific body-essences, like fire, earth, and celestial sphere. The objector strengthens this by noting that magnitudes (المقادير) are accidents, and body is the substance receptive to them. The author then analyzes three aspects: the magnitude itself, the substance's receptivity, and the relation between them. He argues that while magnitudes are equal in being magnitudes, and receptivity is a common intelligible, it is established in rational sciences that different things can share in some concomitants (لوازم). So it does not necessarily follow that bodies are equal in receptivity—they can be different in specific realities yet share in extension. The author concludes that what is known about body is only its necessary reception of three dimensions; the specific essence is unknown, so we cannot judge similarity or difference by original disposition. Thus establishing similarity in all specific realities is extremely difficult. However, the author then offers his own resolution: we know intuitively that bodies are equal in the nature of extension and also that each is distinct in individual determination. These things, equal in extension but distinct in individuality, either are different in essence or not. If not, then they are identical in essence—the desired conclusion. If they are different, then extension is other than the specific realities, leading to a division into three cases (which are not fully listed here). The passage thus presents the problem and a logical way forward, largely following the author's own argumentative order.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument for God's existence via equality of bodies is simple and universally accepted.
Correction: The author shows it is heavily contested and requires careful analysis; the premise is extremely difficult to establish.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the pivot (مدار) of the proof that relies on bodies?
A) Establishing that bodies are composed of atoms
B) Establishing that bodies are equal in the entirety of their essences
C) Establishing that bodies are contingent
D) Establishing that bodies have specific magnitudes

Key Takeaway
The proof for God's existence based on bodily homogeneity faces a serious objection from the analogy of attributes, requiring careful analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult objection to your belief in God, do you tend to dismiss it or wrestle with it? This lesson shows the author engaging honestly with a serious challenge. What would it take for you to adopt that same intellectual honesty?
?
The author admits that the specific essence of bodies is unknown to us. How comfortable are you with not knowing the full reality of things you rely on for faith? Are you willing to let uncertainty deepen your trust in God rather than shake it?
?
You have just read a complex argument about bodies and essences. If someone asked you to explain why proving God's existence is not straightforward, would you be able to do so? What does your answer reveal about your own commitment to understanding your faith?

The Author's Resolution: Bodies Equal in Extension but Distinct in Individuality
The author presents his resolution: bodies equal in extension, distinct in individuality, leading to a tripartite division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's alternative approach, which uses the known equality in extension and distinct individuality to propose a logical division of possibilities for sameness or difference in essence.

Core Idea
Bodies are known to be equal in extension and distinct in individuality; this leads to a division into three cases regarding essence identity.

After showing the difficulty of the original claim, the author offers his own solution. He starts from what is intuitively known: bodies share extension but each has a unique individual determination. From this, he asks whether they are different in essence or not. This lesson presents his reasoning and the resulting division into three possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two known facts
The author asserts that bodies are known intuitively to be equal in the nature of extension and magnitude, and also each body is distinct in its individual determination (تشخص).
These are the starting premises that are not contested.
2
Understand the disjunction
The author sets up a disjunction: either bodies are different in essence or they are not. If not, then they are identical in essence—the desired conclusion.
This is a logical way to resolve the difficulty.
3
Consider the case of difference
If they are different in essence, then extension and specific reality are separate. Therefore, extension must be other than the specific realities that cause difference.
This leads to a division into three possible cases.
4
Note the tripartite division
The author states that on this supposition, the issue divides into three cases (no more), but the passage ends before explaining them.
The incomplete ending suggests the argument continues beyond the provided chunk.

Answer to the First Objection: Mutual Causation
The author answers the infinite regress objection with mutual causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the infinite regress objection is resolved by positing mutual causation between body quiddity and determination.

Core Idea
Mutual causation can break the regress without requiring an external cause.

The first objection claims that if bodies differ in essence, the determination of each body would require an infinite chain of causes. The author offers a clever solution: the quiddity of body and the quiddity of determination can mutually cause each other when connected. This lesson examines that answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the author presents the objection and then the answer.
The answer begins with 'لم لا يجوز'.
2
Understand the regress problem
The objection contends that determination requires a cause, leading to infinite regress. Identify why this is problematic.
3
Analyze the mutual causation argument
The author says that body and determination become each other's cause when connected. Think about how this stops the regress.
This is a non-standard causal relation.
4
Consider the implications
Reflect on how this answer supports the view that bodies differ in essence. What does it assume about the nature of determination?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذات بهذه الصفة دون سائر الذوات ، ودون سائر الصفات يكون رجحانا لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا المرجح ، وهو محال ، فيثبت أن القول بتماثل الأجسام يفضي إلى هذا المحال . أما إذا قلنا : إنها ذوات مختلفة لذواتها والحقائقها ، زال هذا الإشكال ، فكان هذا القول أولى
والجواب عن الشبهة : أن نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال ماهية الجسم وماهية التعين ، إذا اتصل كل واحد منهما بالآخر ، صار كل واحد منهما علة لتعين الآخر ؟ وبهذا الطريق ينقطع التسلسل
والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أن نقول : إن التعين ، وإن كان قيدا زائدا لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون قيدا عدميا ؟ وعلى هذا التقدير يستغنى عن العلة
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : إن الفلاسفة يقولون : كل حالة حاصلة في محل فهي مسبوقة بحصول حالة أخرى ، ويكون الحالة السابقة تتوجب استعداد المادة لقبول الحالة المتأخرة على التعين .
وأما المتكلمون فإنهم يقولون : الفاعل المختار لا يمتنع أن يخصص بعض الذوات ببعض الصفات لا لمرجح . فهذا منتهى الكلام في هذا الباب والله ولي النعم والإحسان
The essence with this attribute to the exclusion of other essences and other attributes would be a preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other, not the preponderator, which is absurd. So it is established that the statement of the similarity of bodies leads to this absurdity. But if we say that they are essences different in their essences and realities, this problem disappears, so this statement is more appropriate.
Answer to the objection: we say: why is it not permissible to say that the quiddity of body and the quiddity of determination, when each one of them is connected to the other, each one of them becomes a cause for the determination of the other? And by this way the sequence is cut off.
Answer to the second objection: we say: determination, although it is an additional condition, yet why is it not permissible for it to be a privative condition? On this assumption, there is no need for a cause.
Answer to the third objection: the philosophers say: every state occurring in a substrate is preceded by the occurrence of another state, and the preceding state necessitates the preparedness of matter to accept the subsequent state for determination. As for the theologians, they say: the free agent is not prohibited from specifying some essences with some attributes without a preponderator. This is the end of the discussion in this chapter. And God is the possessor of favors and beneficence.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage defends the view that bodies are essentially different, after having shown that the view of body similarity leads to absurdity. The author first reiterates that if bodies were similar, their differing attributes would require a preponderator (murajjih) without cause, which is absurd. Therefore, the view that bodies differ in essence is preferable. Then he provides three answers to objections:
1. First objection: The argument involves an infinite regress (tasalsul). The answer: The quiddity of body and the quiddity of determination (ta'ayyun) can mutually cause each other when connected, thus cutting the regress.
2. Second objection: Determination is an additional attribute requiring a cause. The answer: It could be a privative (adami) condition, needing no cause.
3. Third objection: Philosophers argue that every state in a substrate requires a prior state to prepare matter. The answer: Theologians (mutakallimun) hold that a free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) can specify essences with attributes without any preponderator. The passage concludes the chapter.

Key Takeaway
Mutual causation between body and determination can resolve the infinite regress objection.

Answer to the Second Objection: Determination as Privative Condition
The author answers the objection that determination requires a cause by suggesting it is privative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how allowing determination to be a privative condition removes the need for a cause.

Core Idea
A privative condition needs no cause, so determination can be explained without a preponderator.

The second objection argues that determination is an additional attribute (qayd za'id) that requires a cause. The author responds by suggesting that determination could be a privative condition (qayd 'adami), which does not require a cause. This lesson unpacks that response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the key phrase 'قيدا عدميا'.
2
Understand the objection
The objection is that determination, if real, must have a cause. The author counters by redefining determination as a privation.
3
Analyze privative conditions
Consider what it means for something to be 'adami' (privative). How does that differ from a positive attribute? Why would it not need a cause?
In Islamic philosophy, privations do not require efficient causes.
4
Connect to the overall argument
This answer supports the view that bodies can differ in essence without requiring an external cause for their determination.

Source Text

الذات بهذه الصفة دون سائر الذوات ، ودون سائر الصفات يكون رجحانا لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا المرجح ، وهو محال ، فيثبت أن القول بتماثل الأجسام يفضي إلى هذا المحال . أما إذا قلنا : إنها ذوات مختلفة لذواتها والحقائقها ، زال هذا الإشكال ، فكان هذا القول أولى

Answer to the Third Objection: Free Agency and Specification
The author contrasts the philosopher's causal chain with the theologian's free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological response that a free agent can specify without a preponderator.

Core Idea
A free agent can arbitrarily specify attributes without needing a reason.

The third objection comes from philosophers who argue that every state in a substrate requires a prior state to prepare it. The author presents the theologians' view: a free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) can specify essences with attributes without any preponderator. This lesson explores that answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the contrast between philosophers and theologians.
2
Understand the philosopher's argument
Philosophers hold that states are preceded by other states that prepare the matter. This implies determination must have a cause.
3
Analyze the theologian's response
Theologians assert that a free agent can act without a reason (la li-murajjih). How does this bypass the need for a preparatory state?
This relies on the concept of free will.
4
Evaluate the debate
Consider the strengths and weaknesses of each view. How does this answer defend the difference of bodies in essence?

Proof of God's Existence Through the Contingency of Attributes (Beginning)
The author begins the method of proving God's existence by the contingency of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial steps of the argument from the contingency of attributes, including the principle of equal receptivity and the classification of possible causes.

Core Idea
The contingency of attributes forces us to look beyond the physical for a cause.

This lesson introduces a classical kalam argument for God's existence that starts with observable differences in bodies. By examining why bodies have particular attributes, we uncover a logical necessity for an external cause. The passage lays the groundwork for proving that this cause must be non-physical.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation side by side. Note how the argument begins with the observation that bodies share corporeality but differ in accidents.
2
Identify the key divisions
The passage divides the specification of attributes into caused or uncaused. Identify why the uncaused option is rejected.
Look for the phrase 'لا لأمر'.
3
Understand the principle of equal receptivity
The argument relies on the principle that similar quiddities have equal receptivity to attributes. Grasp why this makes uncaused specification impossible.
The term is 'قابلية الصفات'.
4
Trace the classification of causes
After establishing a cause, the passage categorizes it into three types. Note the third type's further subdivision.
The text cuts off during the third subdivision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس عشر
في
بيان كيفية الاستدلال بإمكان الصفات على وجود الإله القادير
وتقرير هذه الطريقة أن يقال : الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية ومختلفة في الكيفيات والأحياز ، والمقادير ، فاختصاص كل واحد منها بحالته المعينة وصفته المعينة إما أن يكون لأمر ، أو لا لأمر
،
والقسم الثاني باطل ، لأن الأجسام لما كانت متساوية في تمام الماهية ، فكل ما صح على بعضها وجب أن يصح على الباقي ، لما ثبت أن المتماثلات في تمام الماهية ، يجب كونها متساوية في قابلية الصفات والأعراض ، ولما ثبت استواء الكل في القبول ، فلو اختص كل واحد منها بصفته المعينة دون الباقي لا لأمر ، لزم وقوع الممكن لا المرجح ، وهو محال ، وقد ثبت فساده . ولما ثبت فساد هذا القسم وجب أن يكون اختصاص كل جسم بصفته المعينة بسبب . فنقول :
وذلك السبب أن يكون شيئاً حالا في الجسم، أو شيئاً يكون محلا له، أو شيئا لا يكون حالا فيه ولا محلا له ، وهذا القسم الثالث ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام : لأنه إما أن يكون جسما ، أو جسمانيا ، أو لا يكون جسما ولا
Chapter Sixteen
On
Explaining the method of inference from the contingency of attributes to the existence of the powerful God
And the formulation of this method is to say: Bodies are equal in corporeality and differ in qualities, places, and magnitudes. So the specification of each one of them to its particular state and particular attribute is either due to something, or not due to something.
The second division is invalid, because since bodies are equal in their complete quiddity, everything that is valid for some of them must be valid for the rest, since it has been established that things similar in their complete quiddity must be equal in the receptivity of attributes and accidents. And since it has been established that all are equal in receptivity, if each one of them were specified to its particular attribute without the rest not due to something, the occurrence of the possible without a preponderator would be necessary, which is absurd, and its corruption has been established. And since the corruption of this division has been established, it must be that the specification of each body to its particular attribute is due to a cause. So we say:
That cause is either something inherent in the body, or something that is a place for it, or something that is neither inherent in it nor a place for it. And this third division is divided into three divisions: for it is either a body, or a bodily thing, or neither a body nor
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage begins Chapter Sixteen, which explains how to use the contingency of attributes (imkan al-sifat) to prove the existence of God. The argument proceeds as follows:
1. Bodies are equal in their essential property (jismiyyah) but differ in accidental attributes like qualities, places, and sizes.
2. The particular state and attribute of each body must be either caused (bi-amr) or uncaused.
3. The uncaused option is impossible: if bodies are identical in quiddity, they have equal receptivity (qabiliyyah) to attributes. If one body had an attribute without cause, it would be a possible without a preponderator (murajjih), which is absurd.
4. Therefore, each body's specification must be due to a cause.
5. The cause (sabab) can be of three types: (a) something inherent in the body (hal fih), (b) something that is a substrate for the body (mahall lahu), or (c) something neither inherent nor a substrate.
6. The third type (c) is further divided into three: (i) a body (jism), (ii) a bodily thing (jismani), or (iii) neither body nor bodily.
The text cuts off here, but the argument is intended to conclude that the ultimate cause must be neither a body nor bodily, i.e., the non-physical God.

Key Terms
إمكان الصفات
الإله القادير
الجسمية
المتماثلات
قابلية الصفات
المرجح
حال في الجسم
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is a variation of the cosmological argument from motion.
Correction: The argument relies on the contingency of attributes, not motion. It uses the principle of equal receptivity in quiddity, not change.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why is the uncaused specification of a body's attribute impossible?
A) Because it would require an existing cause.
B) Because it would lead to an infinite regress.
C) Because it would involve an occurrence without a preponderator, which is absurd.
D) Because bodies cannot have attributes without a substrate.

Key Takeaway
The contingency of attributes necessitates an external cause that is neither a body nor a bodily thing, pointing to a non-physical Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see the vast differences in people's circumstances—health, wealth, talent—do you genuinely believe each detail is specified by God, or do you default to attributing it to chance? How does your belief (or doubt) shape your gratitude and trust in His wisdom? What would it mean for you to live today as if every attribute you have is a direct gift from the Cause?
?
The argument states that if left to themselves, all bodies would be identical. Reflect on a specific trait in your character (e.g., patience, anger, faith) that could have been otherwise. Have you ever thanked the One who specified it for you? If that trait is flawed, are you asking the Preponderator to change it?
?
The proof forces you to look beyond the physical for an explanation. In your life, when you face a situation that seems random or unfair, do you rush to natural explanations or stop to consider the non-physical Cause? What would change in your heart if you truly saw every event as a sign of a powerful God who specifies all things?

The Necessity of a Cause for Bodies' Attributes
Argument that specification requires a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the diversity of attributes in bodies cannot be unexplained and must be attributed to a cause, based on the principle that similar essences have equal receptivity.

Core Idea
Sameness of essence demands sameness of capacity; diversity demands a cause.

This lesson introduces the first step of a classical kalam proof for God's existence. The author establishes that bodies, though sharing the same essence of corporeality, differ in their qualities and positions. He then argues that this difference cannot be without a cause, because if it were, it would violate the rational principle that identical essences have identical potentials. This sets the stage for searching for an external cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the premise carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the observation that bodies are equal in corporeality but differ in attributes.
Note that 'corporeality' (الجسمية) is the shared essence.
2
Understand the principle of similars
The author uses the principle that things with identical complete essences must be equal in their capacity to receive attributes. This is key to the argument.
The word 'تماثل' (similarity) is crucial.
3
Grasp the impossibility of a possible without a preponderant
If bodies differ without a cause, it would mean a possible event occurred without a sufficient reason, which is rationally impossible. This establishes the need for a cause.
The term 'المرجح' (preponderant) refers to the cause that tips the balance.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس عشر في بيان كيفية الاستدلال بإمكان الصفات على وجود الإله القادر وتقرير هذه الطريقة أن يقال : الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية ومختلفة في الكيفيات والأحياز، والمقادير، فاختصاص كل واحد منها بحالته المعينة وصفته المعينة إما أن يكون لأمر، أو لا لأمر، والقسم الثاني باطل، لأن الأجسام لما كانت متساوية في تمام الماهية، فكل ما صح على بعضها وجب أن يصح على الباقي، لما ثبت أن المتماثلات في تمام الماهية، يجب كونها متساوية في قابلية الصفات والأعراض، ولما ثبت استواء الكل في القبول، فلو اختص كل واحد منها بصفته المعينة دون الباقي لا الأمر، لزم وقوع الممكن لا المرجح، وهو محال، وقد ثبت فساده. ولما ثبت فساد هذا القسم وجب أن يكون اختصاص كل جسم بصفته المعينة بسبب. فنقول: وذلك السبب أن يكون شيئاً حالا في الجسم، أو شيئاً يكون محلا له، أو شيئا لا يكون حالا فيه ولا محلا له، وهذا القسم الثالث ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام: لأنه إما أن يكون جسما، أو جسمانيا، أو لا يكون جسما ولا جسمانيا، فهذه بأسرها سوى القسم الأخير باطلة. فبقي أن يكون الحق هو هذا القسم. وأما القسم الأول: وهو أن يقال: المقتضى الحصول هذه الصفات قوى حالة في هذه الأجسام: فنقول: هذا محال. لأن الأجسام كما أنها بعد تماثلها في ماهياتها قد اختلفت في الأعراض، فكذلك قد اختلفت في القوى الموجبة لتلك الأعراض وذلك يوجب أن تكون تلك القوى موجبة لقوى أخرى إلى غير النهاية. وهو محال. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يقال: الموجب: لهذه الصفات المختلفة مواد الأجسام وقوابلها، فهذا أيضا محال. لأنا أقمنا البرهان اليقيني على أن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون حالا في محل، وحاصلا في قابل. والقسم الثالث: وهو أن يقال: المقتضى لهذه الصفات جسم آخر مباین. فهذا أيضا محال. لأن هذا الجسم يكون لا محالة مخصوصا بالصفات التي لأجلها صار مؤثرا في سائر الأجسام ومديرا لها، والتقسيم الأول يعود في سبب اختصاص ذلك الجسم بتلك الصفات. والقسم الرابع: وهو أن يقال: المقتضى الحصول هذه الصفات في هذه الأجسام صفات أخرى حالة في الأجسام الأخر، فهذا: أيضا باطل لعين الدليل المذكور، ولما بطل القول بهذه الأقام، ثبت أن الحق هو القسم الثاني، وهو أن اختصاص كل جسم بصفته المعينة، إنما كان لأجل موجود مباين، ليس بجسم ولا جسماني.
Chapter Sixteen: On explaining the method of inference from the possibility of attributes to the existence of the Powerful God. The exposition of this method is as follows: Bodies are equal in corporeality and differ in qualities, places, and magnitudes. So the specification of each one of them to its particular state and its particular attribute is either due to something or not due to something. The second division is invalid, because since bodies are equal in their complete essence, everything that is valid for some of them must be valid for the rest, since it is established that things similar in complete essence must be equal in the receptivity of attributes and accidents, and since the equality of all in receptivity is established, if each one were specified with its particular attribute without the rest without a cause, the occurrence of a possible without a preponderant would follow, which is impossible, and its corruption has been established. And since the corruption of this division is established, it becomes necessary that the specification of each body with its particular attribute is due to a cause. We say: That cause is either something inherent in the body, or something that is a locus for it, or something that is neither inherent in it nor a locus for it. And this third division is divided into three divisions: for it is either a body, or a corporeal, or neither a body nor a corporeal. All of these except the last division are invalid. So it remains that the truth is this division. As for the first division: namely, that it is said: the requirer of the occurrence of these attributes is powers inherent in these bodies. We say: This is impossible. Because bodies, just as they have differed in accidents despite their similarity in essences, so too have they differed in the powers necessitating those accidents, and that necessitates that those powers require other powers ad infinitum, which is impossible. As for the second division: namely, that it is said: the necessitator of these different attributes is the matters of bodies and their receptivities. This too is impossible. For we have established the certain proof that a body is impossible to be inherent in a locus and to occur in a receptacle. The third division: namely, that it is said: the requirer of these attributes is another separate body. This too is impossible. Because this body will inevitably be specified with the attributes by which it becomes an effect in the rest of the bodies and a director of them, and the first division recurs concerning the cause of the specification of that body with those attributes. The fourth division: namely, that it is said: the requirer of the occurrence of these attributes in these bodies is other attributes inherent in other bodies. This too is invalid for the same proof mentioned. And when the statement with these divisions is invalidated, it is established that the truth is the second division, namely that the specification of each body with its particular attribute is only due to a separate existent, not a body nor a corporeal.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of a non-corporeal cause for the diversity of attributes in bodies. The author begins by stating the observation: bodies share the same essence (corporeality) yet differ in qualities, places, and magnitudes. Therefore, each body's specific attributes must have a cause (otherwise a possible would occur without a preponderant, which is impossible). The cause is then divided into three possibilities: (1) something inherent in the body (like an accident), (2) something that is a locus for the body (like substance?), (3) something neither inherent nor a locus. The third possibility is further divided into three: (a) a body, (b) a corporeal (like a subtle body?), (c) neither body nor corporeal. All except (c) are invalid, leaving that the cause is a non-bodily, non-corporeal existent. The author then refutes four specific alternative candidates: (i) inherent powers (leads to infinite regress), (ii) the material substrate (impossible because a body cannot be inherent in a receptacle), (iii) another separate body (that body would itself require a cause, leading to regress), (iv) attributes in other bodies (also regress). After refuting these, the author concludes that the true cause is a separate existent that is neither body nor corporeal, which is the intended proof for a non-material agent (God).

Key Terms
التماثل في تمام الماهية
المرجح
قابلية الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What principle does the author use to argue that bodies cannot differ in attributes without a cause?
A) Bodies are different in essence
B) Things similar in complete essence must be equal in receptivity
C) Attributes are eternal
D) Causes are unnecessary

Key Takeaway
The diversity of attributes in bodies cannot be accidental; it proves the existence of a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the countless differences in the world around you—from the colors of flowers to the shapes of mountains—do you genuinely pause to recognize that each detail requires a cause? How often do you live as if these differences are just 'how things are' without acknowledging the One who specified them? Commit today to seeing every distinction as a sign pointing to its Maker.
?
This lesson argues that if bodies were uncaused in their specifics, the impossible would happen. In your own life, where have you been assuming that your habits, strengths, or weaknesses are simply 'part of you' without considering that Allah has specified them for a purpose? What would change if you truly believed that every attribute you have is caused by Him?
?
The principle here is that identical essences cannot produce diversity without a cause. Reflect on your own heart: when you find yourself drifting from obedience, do you blame circumstances or do you recognize that your soul, which is essentially the same as the righteous, needs a cause (divine grace) to incline towards good? Ask Allah tonight to provide that cause for you.

The Cause Must Be Non-Corporeal
Classification and elimination of possible causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how through a process of elimination, the only viable cause for bodies' attributes is a non-corporeal, non-bodily existent.

Core Idea
The cause of diversity is neither inside the body nor another body; it must be immaterial.

Having established that bodies' attributes require a cause, the author now narrows down what that cause could be. He classifies possible causes into categories and systematically refutes all except one: a separate existent that is neither a body nor a corporeal entity. This is the classical kalam proof for a transcendent Creator.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three initial categories of cause
The cause could be inherent in the body, a locus for the body, or neither. Note the third category is further subdivided.
The term 'حالا' means inhering accident, 'محلا' means substrate.
2
Follow the sub-division of the third category
The 'neither' category is divided into three: a body, a corporeal entity, or neither. Only the last survives.
A 'corporeal' (جسماني) refers to a subtle body like a spirit.
3
Analyze the refutation of each false candidate
The author refutes four specific proposals one by one: inherent powers (leads to infinite regress), material substrate (body cannot inhere in a receptacle), another separate body (that body would need a cause), and attributes in other bodies (same problem).
Each refutation uses a reductio ad absurdum.
4
See the conclusion
After eliminating all alternatives, the only remaining possibility is a separate existent that is neither body nor corporeal – the intended proof for God.
The final phrase 'القسم الثاني' is likely a scribal error; the context shows it refers to the non-corporeal option.

Commentary

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The text concludes that the cause is the second division (القسم الثاني).

?

Necessitator by Essence or Agent by Choice
Classification of the effecter as necessary or free

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key distinction between a necessitator by essence and an agent by choice, and the initial argument for rejecting necessary causation in the divine context.

Core Idea
The cause of the world is either necessary or free — rejecting necessity begins with its non-corporeal nature.

The mutakallimūn now turn to a crucial classification: the cause of all existence must be either a necessitator by essence (like a natural cause) or an agent by choice. The passage introduces this dichotomy and begins the argument against necessary causation by noting that the cause is not a body. This sets the stage for proving divine free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly, noting the technical terms 'موجب بالذات' and 'فاعل بالاختيار'. Then read the translation.
The passage cuts off; we only have the beginning of the argument.
2
Understand the two categories
Identify the two possibilities for the cause: necessary by essence (causation without will) vs. agent by choice (causation with will). Notice that the text immediately declares the first invalid.
The reason given is that the cause is not a body.
3
Reflect on the incomplete argument
The passage ends mid-sentence. Consider why a non-corporeal cause cannot be a necessitator. (Hint: necessary causation is linked to material natures.)
This lesson only introduces the dichotomy; the full proof continues later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال المتكلمون في هذا المقام : ذلك المؤثر، إما أن يكون موجبا بالذات أو فاعلا بالاختيار، والأول باطل، لأن ذلك الموجود لما لم يكن جسما
Then the mutakallimūn said in this context: That effecter is either a necessitator by essence or an agent by choice, and the first is invalid, because that existent, since it is not a body...
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the next step in the argument. Having established that the cause of attributes is a non-corporeal existent, the mutakallimūn now consider whether that cause acts necessarily (like a natural cause) or by free choice. They state the two possibilities: 'موجب بالذات' (necessitator by essence) or 'فاعل بالاختيار' (agent by choice). They declare the first invalid, giving as a reason that the existent is not a body. The sentence cuts off, but the implication is that a non-bodily cause cannot be a necessary cause (because necessary causation is characteristic of material natures). The full argument is not present in this window; it continues beyond page 186. This passage functions as the thesis statement for the following discussion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the passage concludes that the cause is a free agent (agent by choice).
Correction: The passage only states that necessary causation (necessitator by essence) is invalid, leaving choice as the remaining option but not yet proving it. The full argument continues beyond this excerpt.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimūn classify the cause as either necessitator by essence or agent by choice, and they begin to argue against necessary causation by noting the cause is not a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were a necessitator by essence, causing the world without will, how would your prayers be any different from hoping a rock falls? But He is an agent by choice—He hears and can choose to respond. When was the last time you prayed with the deep conviction that He is personally listening and free to act, not a mechanical force? What will you do today to turn your next supplication into a plea to a free, loving Agent?
?
The mutakallimūn begin to prove God's freedom from the fact that He is not a body. But you know this intellectually—does your heart live as if God is a distant, impersonal necessity? Where in your life do you act as though God's actions are predictable or limited, forgetting that He is a free agent? This week, choose one situation where you have been assuming God will act a certain way, and instead surrender to His free choice, saying truly, 'Your will be done.'

Proof that the Non-Corporeal Cause is a Free Agent
The author proves that the non-corporeal cause must be a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) not a necessary agent (mūjib bi-al-dhāt).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the non-corporeal cause of the world must be a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) and cannot be a necessary agent (mūjib bi-al-dhāt).

Core Idea
Uniform causation would eliminate diversity; only free choice explains variety.

The theologians have already established that the cause of bodies' qualities is a non-corporeal being. Now they ask: does this being act necessarily or freely? This passage presents a powerful argument that necessary agency is impossible because it would produce uniform effects, contradicting the observed diversity in the world. By working through this argument, you will see why God must be a free agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Do not analyze yet. Let the structure of the argument sink in.
The passage is dense but logical.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The author starts by stating the two possibilities for the cause: necessary agent or free agent. Notice how he presents them.
These are mutually exclusive.
3
Follow the reductio argument
The author assumes necessary agency and derives a contradiction: all bodies would receive the same effect, but they are diverse. This preponderance without preponderator is impossible.
This is a standard kalam argument.
4
Conclude the proof
Once necessary agency is refuted, free agency is established. Connect this to the overall goal of proving God's existence and attributes.
The conclusion also reaffirms God's non-corporeality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال المتكلمون في هذا المقام (۱) : ذلك المؤثر، إما أن يكون موجبا بالذات أو فاعلا بالاختيار، والأول باطل ، لأن ذلك الموجود لما لم يكن جسما
ولا جسمانيا كانت نسبة حقيقته إلى جميع الأجسام نسبة واحدة ، ولما كانت الأجسام بأسرها متساوية ) في تمام الماهية كانت بأسرها متساوية )(۱) في قبول الأثر عن ذلك المباين ) وإذا كان الأمر كذلك كان قبول كل واحد من تلك الأجسام، لأثر خاص من ذلك المباين (۳) المفارق (۳) رجحانا لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح ، وهو محال ، ولما بطلت (4) هذه الأقسام ، ثبت : أن إله العالم موجود ، ليس بجسم ولا بجسماني ، وأنه فاعل مختار وليس موجبا بالذات. فهذا تمام تقرير هذه الحجة .
Then the theologians said in this context: that effective cause is either a necessary agent by essence (mūjib bi-al-dhāt) or a free agent by choice (fāʿil bi-al-ikhtiyār). The first is false, because that existent since it is not a body nor corporeal, its relation to all bodies is one single relation. And since all bodies are equal in the completeness of essence, they are all equal in receiving the effect from that separate (being). If the matter is so, the reception of each one of those bodies of a specific effect from that separate, transcendent (being) would be a preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible without a preponderator, which is impossible. And when these divisions became false, it was established that the God of the world exists, not a body nor corporeal, and that He is a free agent and not a necessary agent by essence. This is the complete formulation of this proof.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous conclusion that the cause of bodies' qualities is a non-corporeal being. The theologians then debate whether this being acts necessarily (mūjib bi-al-dhāt) or freely (fāʿil mukhtār). The author argues that necessary agency is impossible. Since the being is non-corporeal, its relation to all bodies is uniform. Bodies are all similar in essence, so they are equally receptive to its influence. If it were a necessary agent, it would produce the same effect in all bodies. But bodies have different qualities, so the specific effect each receives would be an arbitrary preponderance (tarjīḥ) of one possibility over another without a preponderator (murjīḥ), which is impossible. Therefore, the being must be a free agent who chooses to give different qualities. The conclusion: God exists, non-corporeal, and free.

Key Terms
موجب بالذات (mūjib bi-al-dhāt)
فاعل بالاختيار (fāʿil bi-al-ikhtiyār)
رجحان (tarjīḥ)
مرجح (murjīḥ)
ممكن (mumkin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary agency (mūjib bi-al-dhāt) is compatible with divine power and can produce a diverse world.
Correction: The author argues that necessary agency would require uniform effects due to the cause's uniform relation to all bodies. Diversity would then be an arbitrary preponderance without a preponderator, which is impossible. Hence necessary agency cannot explain the observed diversity.

Checkpoint
Why is necessary agency (mūjib bi-al-dhāt) impossible for the non-corporeal cause?
A) Because it would require a body
B) Because it would result in all bodies having the same qualities
C) Because it would lead to infinite regress
D) Because the cause is not a body

Key Takeaway
The non-corporeal cause cannot be a necessary agent because that would lead to impossible preponderance; it must be a free agent who chooses the diverse qualities of bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the vast diversity in creation, do you see it as a sign of God's free choice or as a mechanical necessity? How would your faith change if God were bound to act uniformly?
?
If God were a necessary agent, your prayers would be futile because outcomes would be fixed. Knowing He is a free agent, do you truly approach Him with expectation that He can choose to respond? Or do you pray as if it's a ritual rather than a plea to a willing Lord?
?
The proof shows that preponderance without a preponderator is impossible. In your life, when you face two choices and cannot decide, do you seek the Preponderator (God's guidance) or rely on your own reason? What would change if you consciously attributed every preference to His will?

The Value of the Premise of Bodily Similarity
The author enumerates three theological benefits of the bodily similarity premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the premise of bodily similarity serves as a foundation for proving God's existence, prophethood, and the afterlife.

Core Idea
The similarity of bodies premise is key to proving God, miracles, and resurrection.

The author highlights that the argument from bodily similarity is a noble premise with great benefit. It underpins major theological doctrines: proving God as a free agent, establishing prophethood through miracles, and affirming the resurrection. Let's explore how this premise works across these domains.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the premise's centrality
Begin by reading the Arabic passage that explains the pivot of the argument on the similarity of bodies. Note the author's emphasis on its nobility and benefit.
The premise is established earlier; here its importance is highlighted.
2
Identify the three derived doctrines
The passage lists three areas: proving God, proving prophethood, and proving the afterlife. Write them down and see how each is linked to the premise.
The link for God is direct; for prophethood it goes via possibility of miracles; for afterlife via possibility of cosmic events.
3
Trace the logical chain for prophethood
The premise implies that breaking of customs is possible, which allows for miracles. Understand why this is necessary for prophethood.
Miracles confirm prophets; if impossible, prophethood cannot be proven.
4
Trace the logical chain for the afterlife
The premise makes it possible for the heavens to move and the sun and moon to be folded. Realize that this grounds the Quranic descriptions of resurrection.
5
Reflect on the author's statement of completion
The passage ends with 'that is the complete discourse in formulating this method.' This signals the end of the positive exposition.
Next lesson covers limitations.

As for establishing prophethood, because this premise indicates that the breaking of customs is possible, and that indicates the possibility of miracles. As for establishing the resurrection, because this premise indicates that moving the heavens is possible, and the folding up of the sun and moon is possible, and everything that the Quran has brought concerning the states of the resurrection is possible. So this is the complete discourse in formulating this method. Commentary
This passage reflects on the broader significance and limitations of the argument based on the similarity of bodies (tamāthul al-ajsām). The author first highlights its importance: it is the foundation for proving God's existence and attributes (specifically that He is a free agent), the possibility of prophethood (through miracles as breaking of customs), and the possibility of the afterlife and resurrection (since events like moving the heavens are possible). However, the author then notes limitations of this particular argument. The first demand: the argument does not prove that bodies themselves are originated (ḥādith) or contingent (mumkin); an opponent could claim bodies are eternal and necessary but that God merely composes them. The second demand is cut off but likely continues to point out that the argument does not prove that the director (the being responsible for composition) is necessarily the Creator or something else. The author acknowledges that while the argument shows the existence of a powerful God, it does not answer all theological questions.

The premise of bodily similarity is foundational for proving God's existence, the possibility of prophethood through miracles, and the possibility of the afterlife.

?

?

?
When you face a situation that seems impossible (like a broken habit or a difficult trial), do you remember that God can break natural customs? What one situation in your life right now needs you to trust in His power to break the norm?

Limitations of the Argument from Bodily Similarity
The author presents two limitations of the bodily similarity argument; the second is incomplete.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the limitations of the argument from bodily similarity, specifically that it does not prove the origination or contingency of bodies' essences.

Core Idea
The argument from bodily similarity proves composition, not the contingency of bodies themselves.

After showcasing the value of the bodily similarity argument, the author now turns to its limitations. He acknowledges that while the argument shows the existence of a powerful God who composes the world, it does not answer every theological question. In particular, it does not prove that bodies themselves are originated or contingent. Let's examine these limits closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on limitations
Read the Arabic passage from 'Wa'lam anna bil-bayān' to the end. Note that the second demand is cut off.
The passage is incomplete; we will discuss what is available.
2
Understand the first limitation (first demand)
The argument does not prove that bodies are originated (ḥādith) or contingent (mumkin). An opponent could claim bodies are necessary by themselves and that God only arranges them.
This is a significant gap; the argument only shows composition, not creation.
3
Examine the second limitation (second demand - incomplete)
The second demand, though cut off, starts by saying the argument does not prove that the director (al-mudabbir) is something. This likely means it does not prove that the director is the creator or that he is one.
The source is cut off, so we can only speculate on the full argument.
4
Reflect on the overall message
The author balances the utility of the argument with its limits. This reminds us that no single proof covers all theological truths.
