Proof of God's Transcendence and Free Agency
The author concludes the proof that God is not a body or corporeal and is a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that God is neither a body nor corporeal, and that He is a free agent (faʿil mukhtar) rather than a necessitator by essence.

Core Idea
God is not a body, not corporeal, and acts freely without constraint.

In this passage, the author completes a proof that establishes key divine attributes. The argument uses the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator to show that God cannot be a body or corporeal, and concludes that He is a free agent. This lesson unpacks the logical steps of that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the flow. Notice the conditional structure.
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Understand the conditional premise
The passage starts with a conditional: if God were not a body nor corporeal, then His essence's relation to all bodies would be uniform. Identify this premise.
3
Grasp the equality of bodies
The argument then states that since all bodies are equal in their essence (quiddity), they would equally receive influence. Recognize that this leads to a problem with specific effects.
4
Identify the absurdity and conclusion
The specific effect would be a preponderance without a preponderator, which is impossible. Therefore, the assumption is false, and it is established that God is not a body nor corporeal, and He is a free agent.
This conclusion also refutes the idea of God being a necessitator by essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا جسمانيا كانت نسبة حقيقته إلى جميع الأجسام نسبة واحدة ولما كانت الأجسام بأسرها متساوية في تمام الماهية كانت بأسرها متساوية في قبول الأثر عن ذلك المباين وإذا كان الأمر كذلك كان قبول كل واحد من تلك الأجسام لأثر خاص من ذلك المباين المفارق رجحانا لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح وهو محال ولما بطلت هذه الأقسام ثبت أن إله العالم موجود ليس بجسم ولا بجسماني وأنه فاعل مختار وليس موجبا بالذات. فهذا تمام تقرير هذه الحجة.
And if it were not a body nor corporeal, the relation of His essence to all bodies would be one; and when all bodies are equal in complete essence, they are all equal in receiving the effect from that distinct separate being; and if that is the case, then the acceptance by each of those bodies of a particular effect from that distinct separate being would be a preponderance of one of the two possibilities of the contingent without a preponderator, which is impossible; and since these divisions are false, it is established that the God of the world exists, not a body and not corporeal, and that He is a free agent (faʿil mukhtar) and not a necessitator by essence (mujib bi al-dhat). This is the complete exposition of this proof.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents the conclusion of a proof. It begins by stating a conditional: if God were not a body or corporeal, then His essence's relation to all bodies would be uniform. Then it argues that since all bodies are equal in their quiddity (essence), they are equally receptive to influence from that distinct being. If that were the case, then a particular body receiving a specific effect would be a preponderance without a preponderator, which is absurd. Therefore, the divisions are false, and it is established that God is not a body or corporeal, and He is a free agent, not a necessitator by essence.

جسم
جسماني
فاعل مختار
موجب بالذات
تماثل الأجسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof directly proves God's existence.
Correction: The passage assumes God's existence and instead proves specific attributes: that He is not a body, not corporeal, and is a free agent. The proof is about God's nature, not His existence.

Checkpoint

A) He is a body
B) He is a free agent
C) He is necessary by essence
D) He is corporeal

Key Takeaway
God is not a body, not corporeal, and acts as a free agent, not by necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every specific blessing you receive—health, wealth, guidance—is a direct choice of God, not just a result of general causality? How would your gratitude change if you internalized that?
?
If God were a necessitator by essence, your existence would be compulsory. But He is a free agent who chose to create you. What does that mean for your relationship with Him? Are you living as if you are a free individual responding to a free Creator?
?
The proof shows that chance (preponderance without a preponderator) is impossible. In your life, do you sometimes attribute events to luck or accident? How can you replace that with the conviction that every specific effect is chosen by God?

First Demand: Bodies Not Proven Originated or Contingent
First demand: proof fails on bodies' hadith and imkan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof does not establish that bodies are originated (hadith) or contingent (mumkin), and how an opponent could claim bodies are necessary in themselves.

Core Idea
The proof fails to show bodies are created; they might be necessary.

The author begins by pointing out a crucial gap in the proof: it does not prove that the essences of bodies are either originated or contingent. An objector could simply assert that bodies are necessary in themselves, and that God merely arranges them. This first demand sets the stage for the need for a more fundamental argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first demand
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Note the two things the proof does not prove: huduth (origination) and imkan (contingency) of bodies.
The passage also includes the objector's alternative claim.
2
Identify the objector's position
The objector says bodies could be wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi (necessary in themselves), while God only composes them. Understand how this challenges the proof's conclusion.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if bodies are necessary, then the proof does not show they depend on God for existence. This shows the proof's limitation.
The demand is not a refutation but a gap to fill.

فالمطلب الأول: هذا الدليل لا يدل على حدوث ذوات الأجسام ولا على إمكان ذواتها. فإن لقائل أن يقول: إن هذه الأجسام واجبة الوجود لذواتها، إلا أن إله العالم قادر على تركيب العالم الأعلى والأسفل منه. والمطلب الثاني: إن هذا الدليل لا يدل على أن ذلك المدير المركب لهذا العالم واجب الوجود لذاته، بل يبقى احتمال أنه ممكن الوجود لذاته، فمن أراد إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته، وجب عليه أن يقيم الدليل على أن ممكن الوجود حال بقائه يحتاج إلى المؤثر، ثم يقيم الدليل على بطلان الدور والتسلسل، وحينئذ يحصل له القطع بوجود موجود واجب الوجود لذاته. والمطلب الثالث: إن هذا الدليل لا يدل على أن واجب الوجود لذاته قادر مختار فإن لقائل أن يقول: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: هذا الموجود الذي تولى إمكان تركيب هذا العالم وتأليفه، موجود ممكن الوجود لذاته، وهو معلول موجود واجب الوجود لذاته، وذلك الواجب أوجب هذا الفاعل المختار إيجاباً بالذات؟ وعلى هذا التقدير، فهذا الدليل لا يفيد كون المبدأ الأول فاعلا مختارا. فهذه جملة ما يجب التنبيه عليه في معرفة هذا الدليل، والله أعلم.
Thus the first requirement: This proof does not indicate the origination (huduth) of the essences of bodies, nor their contingency (imkan). For someone might say: These bodies are necessary of existence by themselves, except that the God of the world is able to compose the upper and lower world from them. The second requirement: This proof does not indicate that that director and composer of this world is necessary of existence by itself; rather, the possibility remains that it is contingent in itself. So whoever wants to establish the necessary of existence by itself must provide a proof that the contingent, while remaining, needs an effecter, and then provide a proof of the impossibility of regress (dawr) and circularity (tasalsul), and then he will obtain certainty of the existence of a being necessary of existence by itself. The third requirement: This proof does not indicate that the necessary of existence by itself is a free agent. For someone might say: Why is it not possible that this being which undertook the composition and arrangement of this world is a being contingent in itself, and it is an effect of a being necessary of existence by itself, and that necessary necessitated this free agent by necessity of essence? On this assumption, this proof does not establish that the first principle is a free agent. So this is a summary of what must be noted in understanding this proof, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents three demands or objections that the earlier proof does not satisfy. The first is that the proof does not show that the essences of bodies are originated or contingent; they could be necessary in themselves. The second is that it does not prove that the director of the world is necessary of existence; he could be contingent. The third is that even if he is necessary, it does not prove that the first principle is a free agent rather than a necessitator. Each demand shows a limitation of the proof, requiring additional arguments.

Correction: The demand does not refute the proof but identifies a gap: the proof does not address the origin of bodies, so further argument is needed.

A) Their existence and unity

C) Their composition and arrangement
D) Their necessity and eternality

Key Takeaway
The first demand shows that the proof does not rule out bodies being necessary in themselves, so it cannot prove their dependence on God.

Second Demand: Director's Necessary Existence Not Proven
Second demand: proof fails to establish director's necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof does not establish that the director of the world is necessary of existence, and what additional arguments are needed to reach certainty.

Core Idea
The proof leaves open the possibility that the world's director is contingent.

The second demand targets the very core of the proof: even if we accept that the world has a director, that director could still be contingent in itself. To prove a necessary existent, one must first establish that a contingent being always needs an effecter, and then rule out infinite regress and circularity. This demand points to the deeper logical structure required.

Guided Path
1
Read the second demand passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the core claim: the proof does not show the director is wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi.
The text then outlines what would be needed to prove it.
2
Identify the missing steps
The author says one must prove that a contingent being needs an effecter while it remains, and then prove the impossibility of dawr (circularity) and tasalsul (regress).
3
Understand the outcome
Only after these steps can one be certain of a wujub al-wujud bi-dhatihi. This demand shows the proof is incomplete without these foundational principles.
This is a key transition to the core arguments of kalam.

Third Demand: First Principle's Free Agency Not Proven
Third demand: proof fails to show free agency of the First Principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof does not establish that the necessary existent is a free agent (fa'il mukhtar), and how it could be a necessitating cause (mujib bi-dhat).

Core Idea
Even if necessary, God's agency might be necessitated, not free.

The third demand addresses a deeper theological issue: even if we prove a necessary existent, the proof does not show that this being acts with free will. It could be that the necessary being necessarily produces a free agent as an intermediate. This demand forces us to consider the nature of divine agency and the distinction between a free agent and a necessitating cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the third demand passage
Read the Arabic and translation. The claim is that the proof does not show that the necessary existent is a free agent.
2
Understand the objection
An objector proposes that the director of the world is a contingent being, itself caused by a necessary being, and that necessary being necessitated this director necessarily.
This is a possible scenario the proof does not rule out.
3
Grasp the implication
If this scenario holds, then the first principle is not a free agent but a necessitator. The proof does not establish free will.
This demand leads to discussions about divine attributes and the nature of causation.

Contingency Proof and Its Limitation on Free Will
Author explains the contingency proof and its failure to prove free choice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contingency argument for the Necessary Existent and why it does not prove that God is a free chooser.

Core Idea
The contingency argument proves a necessary being, not a free agent.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents the classic contingency argument for the existence of a Necessary Existent, but then highlights an important limitation: the argument alone cannot establish that this Necessary Being is a free chooser. This nuance is crucial for understanding the scope of rational proofs in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Notice the flow: first the proof steps, then the objection.
2
Identify the steps of the proof
The author lists two required demonstrations: that a possible being needs an effecter, and that circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul) are impossible. See how these lead to certainty of a Necessary Existent.
These are the building blocks of the contingency argument.
3
Understand the objection
The author then raises a counterexample: perhaps the first cause is a necessary being that necessitates a free agent, so the first cause itself may not be free. Understand why this shows the proof's limitation.
The objection does not disprove the argument; it only shows the argument does not prove free will.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about what other attributes this argument does and does not prove. This is important for knowing when additional arguments are needed.
This lesson shows the precision required in kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لهذا العالم واجب الوجود لذاته ، بل يبقى احتمال أنه ممكن الوجود لذاته ، فمن أراد إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته ، وجب عليه أن يقيم الدليل على أن ممكن الوجود حال بقائه يحتاج إلى المؤثر، ثم يقيم الدليل على بطلان الدور والتسلسل ، وحينئذ يحصل له القطع بوجود موجود واجب الوجود لذاته · والمطلب الثالث : إن هذا الدليل لا يدل على أن واجب الوجود لذاته قادر مختار فإن لقائل أن يقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : هذا الموجود الذي تولى إمكان تركيب هذا العالم وتأليفه ، موجود ممكن الوجود لذاته ، وهو معلول موجود واجب الوجود لذاته ، وذلك الواجب أوجب هذا الفاعل المختار إيجاباً بالذات ؟ وعلى هذا التقدير، فهذا الدليل لا يفيد كون المبدأ الأول فاعلا مختارا. فهذه جملة ما يجب التنبيه عليه في معرفة هذا الدليل ، والله أعلم
For this world, the existence is necessary in itself, but the possibility remains that it is possible in itself. So whoever wants to prove the Necessary Existent in itself, must establish the proof that the possible being, while remaining, needs an effecter, and then establish the proof of the invalidity of circularity and infinite regress, and then he will obtain certainty of the existence of an existent that is necessary in itself. And the third point: this proof does not indicate that the Necessary Existent in itself is a free chooser, for one could say: why is it not permissible to say that this existent which undertook the possibility of composition and arrangement of this world is a possible existent in itself, and it is an effect of a Necessary Existent in itself, and that Necessary Existent necessitated this free agent by essential necessitation? On this estimation, this proof does not benefit that the first principle is a free agent. This is the sum of what must be noted in understanding this proof, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage explains the proof for the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud) and its limitation. The author first describes the steps of the contingency argument: establishing that a possible being needs an effecter (mu'aththir), then proving the impossibility of circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul), leading to certainty in a Necessary Existent. Then he notes a crucial limitation: this proof does not prove that the Necessary Existent is a free chooser (qadir mukhtar). A detractor could say that the chain might involve a necessary existent that necessitates a free agent, so the first principle might not be free. Thus the proof only establishes a necessary being, not necessarily a free agent. This is a key nuance in kalam.

Misconception: The contingency argument proves that God has free will.
Correction: The argument only proves that there is a necessary being, not that this being is a free chooser. Additional arguments are needed for that.

Existence as a Single Concept Does Not Imply Sameness in All Properties
Second refutation: conceptual unity does not entail sameness in all concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second counter-argument refutes the opponent by accepting that existence is a single concept but showing that the two existences differ in necessary concomitants.

Core Idea
Shared quiddity does not require shared properties.

In this lesson, we examine the second refutation presented by the author. The opponent had argued that existence is a single concept for both God and contingent beings. The author accepts this premise but then draws a crucial distinction: despite sharing the same quiddity, the two existences have different necessary concomitants. This shows that conceptual unity does not entail real unity in all properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author begins by accepting the opponent's premise.
2
Identify the two key premises
First, the author agrees that existence is a single concept. Second, he points out that the existence of Wājib and mumkin are mutually exclusive — each cannot inhere in the other's essence.
These two premises lead to the conclusion.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that the two existences are equal in quiddity but differ in necessary concomitants. This is exactly the point the author intended to make.
The argument does not deny conceptual unity.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this refutation strengthens the author's position: it shows that even if we grant the opponent's premise, the desired conclusion does not follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : إنكم قد دللتم على أن مسمى الوجود في حق واجب الوجود لذاته ، وفي حق ممكن الوجود لذاته، مفهوم واحد. أعني من حيث أنه مسمى الوجود ثم إن وجود واجب الوجود لذاته ، ممتنع الحصول في ذوات الممكنات ، ووجود الممكنات ممتنع الحصول في ذات واجب الوجود لذاته . فهذان الوجودان متساويان في تمام الماهية ، مع أنهما مختلفان في بعض اللوازم . وهو الذي ذكرناه.
Second: You have demonstrated that the meaning of existence (al-wujūd) with respect to the Necessary Existence in itself (wājib al-wujūd li-dhātihī) and with respect to contingent existence in itself (mumkin al-wujūd li-dhātihī) is one concept, i.e., insofar as it is the meaning of existence. Then, the existence of the Necessary Existence in itself is impossible to occur in the essences of contingents, and the existence of contingents is impossible to occur in the essence of the Necessary Existence in itself. So these two existences are equal in the entirety of the quiddity (al-māhiyya), while they differ in some necessary concomitants (al-lawāzim). And that is what we mentioned.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents the second refutation. The opponent claimed that existence (al-wujūd) is a single concept when applied to God (wājib al-wujūd) and contingent beings (mumkin al-wujūd). The author accepts that premise but then points out that despite sharing the same quiddity of 'existence', the two existences differ in their necessary concomitants: God's existence cannot inhere in contingents, and contingent existence cannot inhere in God. Thus equality in quiddity does not require equality in all attributes. The author concludes that this is precisely the point he was making: shared essential concept does not imply shared necessary properties.

وجود
واجب الوجود
ممكن الوجود
ماهية
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that existence is a single concept.
Correction: The argument accepts conceptual unity but denies that it entails sameness in all concomitants.

Checkpoint
What does the second refutation show about two entities sharing the same quiddity?
A) They must be identical in all properties.
B) They can differ in necessary concomitants.
C) They must differ in all properties.
D) Existence is not a single concept.

Key Takeaway
Even if existence is a single concept, the two existences (necessary and contingent) differ in necessary concomitants, so conceptual unity does not imply real unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You likely assume that if two things share a label, they share all important properties. Where in your own life have you conflated conceptual similarity with identical reality? Are you willing to re-examine that assumption?
?
If you claim to believe in God but also treat Him as similar to creatures in some attribute (like love or justice), does this lesson challenge you to reconsider? What step will you take to ensure you don't conflate divine attributes with human ones?
?
The argument shows that even one concept of existence can yield two radically different realities. Does your heart accept that God's existence is utterly unique, or do you secretly imagine Him as 'a bigger version' of you? Honestly: what needs to change in your worship or thinking?

Refuting Objections on Existence and Attributes
Author concludes a proof by refuting objections on attributes, existence, and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes objections regarding attributes being additional to the essence, clarifies the distinction between necessary and possible existence, and addresses infinite regress, all in the context of proving divine existence.

Core Idea
Difference in existence arises from the essence, not from existence itself.

In this concluding passage of a major proof, the author tackles several objections head-on. He first shows that if attributes like occurrence were additional, they would lead to infinite regress. Then he clarifies a crucial point: the difference between the existence of the Necessary Being and possible beings is due to the essence (mahiyya) acting as a locus, not to any difference in the concept of existence. Finally, he responds to challenges about infinite regress and the necessary agent, deferring a full discussion for later. This passage ties together the rational foundation of divine attributes and existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objections
Read the Arabic source and its translation. Notice the three distinct objections being refuted: first about attributes being additional, second about the difference in existence, and third about infinite regress and the necessary agent.
The passage uses the phrase 'قلنا' (we said) to introduce responses.
2
Examine the argument against additional attributes
Focus on the first part regarding occurrence (al-ḥudūth) and permanence (al-baqā'). Understand the infinite regress argument: if occurrence were an additional attribute, it would be originated, requiring another occurrence, leading to a chain.
Note that the author treats both occurrence and permanence similarly.
3
Analyze the distinction between necessary and possible existence
The author argues that existence itself is the same concept for both Necessary and possible, but the difference in necessary consequences (e.g., necessitation) comes from the essence. The essence of the Truth necessitates the existence, making it permanent.
The key phrase: 'فإن ماهية الحق ـ سبحانه ـ لما اقتضت ذلك الوجود'.
4
Understand the responses about infinite regress and necessary agent
The author briefly answers an objection about infinite regress by referencing an earlier proof. He also acknowledges an objection about the necessary agent applying equally to the free agent, but defers a detailed answer to a later section.
This shows the author's method of handling objections systematically.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how each refutation strengthens the proof. The passage concludes the exposition of this particular proof, summarizing key points about essence, existence, and attributes.
The final sentence marks the end of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبقاء فهما ليسا صفتين زائدتين على الذات، ولو كان الحدوث صفة زائدة لكانت حادثة، فيكون حدوثها زائدا عليها. ولزم التسلسل. وكذا القول في البقاء، وأما ( وجود ( (۱) واجب الوجود، ووجود ممكن الوجود. فهما وإن كانا متساويين في مجرد كونه وجودا، إلا أن الاختلاف في اللوازم إنما حصل بسبب الماهية التي هي كالمحل لذلك الوجود، فإن ماهية الحق ـ سبحانه ـ لما اقتضت ذلك الوجود، امتنع زوال ذلك الوجود عن تلك الماهية، فهذا التفاوت إنما حصل بسبب الاختلاف في المحل والقابل. وأما ههنا فقد دللنا على أن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون حالا في المحل(٢)، فظهر الفرق. قوله: « لم قلتم إن التسلسل باطل »؟ قلنا: لما سبق في الدليل الأول. قوله: « الإشكال الذي أوردتموه في الموجب، قائم بعينه في القادر، فنقول: الكلام المستقصى في الفرق بين الموجب وبين المختار، سيجيء في مسألة القدم والحدوث ) وهذا آخر الكلام في تقرير هذا الدليل (٣)
And continuation - both are not additional attributes to the essence, and if occurrence were an additional attribute, it would be originated, so its occurrence would be additional to it, and infinite regress would follow. And similarly for permanence. As for (existence ( (1) of the Necessary of Existence and existence of the possible of existence - although they are equal in mere existence, yet the difference in necessary consequences only arises due to the essence which is like the locus for that existence. For when the essence of the Truth - exalted is He - necessitated that existence, the removal of that existence from that essence became impossible. Thus this disparity only arose due to the difference in locus and recipient. And here we have already shown that the body cannot be an accident in a locus (2), so the difference becomes clear. He said: 'Why did you say that infinite regress is invalid?' We said: Because of what preceded in the first proof. He said: 'The difficulty you raised concerning the necessary agent applies equally to the free agent.' So we say: The detailed discussion of the difference between the necessary agent and the free agent will come in the issue of pre-eternity and origination. And this is the end of the exposition of this proof (3).
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is the concluding section of a proof, responding to objections. It first addresses whether attributes like occurrence and permanence are additional to the essence. If occurrence were an additional attribute, it would be originated, leading to infinite regress. The same applies to permanence. Then it clarifies the distinction between the existence of the Necessary of Existence and possible existence: although both are existence, the difference in consequences arises from the essence (māhiyya) which is like the locus (maḥall) for that existence. The essence of the Necessary of Existence necessitates that existence, making its removal impossible. This disparity is due to the difference in locus and recipient. The author notes that they already proved a body cannot be an accident in a locus, so the distinction holds. Next, it answers an objection about infinite regress, referring to the earlier proof. Finally, it addresses an objection that the difficulty in the necessary agent also applies to the free agent, deferring a detailed discussion of the difference between the necessary agent (mūjib) and the free agent (mukhtār) to a later section on pre-eternity and origination. The passage ends by marking the conclusion of this proof.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the difference between necessary and possible existence is due to a difference in the concept of existence itself.
Correction: The author clarifies that existence is the same concept; the difference in necessary consequences arises from the essence (mahiyya) which acts as a locus.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what necessitates the impossibility of the removal of existence from the essence of the Necessary of Existence?
A) The existence is additional
B) The essence necessitates that existence
C) Infinite regress
D) The body cannot be an accident

Key Takeaway
The difference between necessary and possible existence lies in the essence acting as a locus, not in the existence itself; and objections about additional attributes or infinite regress are refuted with logical arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is not necessary by itself, but only through the Necessary Being, how does this realization affect your sense of dependence on Allah in your daily life? Are you truly living as though your existence is sustained by Him alone?
?
The author argues that attributes like occurrence cannot be additional to the essence to avoid infinite regress. In your own spiritual life, do you sometimes add unnecessary layers between yourself and Allah, making your reliance indirect? How can you remove these intermediaries?
?
The passage states that the essence of Truth necessitates existence, making its removal impossible. Do you have any belief or attachment that you treat as 'necessary'—as if it cannot be removed from your life—when in reality it is contingent? Are you willing to let it go for Allah?

The Eleventh Argument: Specific Natures of Zodiac Signs
Razi's eleventh argument from the specific natures of zodiac signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the specific nature assigned to each zodiac sign reveals contingency and points to a necessary cause.

Core Idea
Even the stars' specific natures are contingent, requiring a divine choice.

Razi presents his eleventh argument from possibility. He points to the fact that each zodiac sign has a particular nature and set of characteristics. Since these could have been different, they are contingent and demand an external cause. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it is a single statement: each zodiac sign has a specific nature and matter among possible things.
The phrase 'among possible things' (min al-ja'izat) is key.
2
Identify the contingent element
Ask yourself: could the nature of a zodiac sign have been different? Yes, because it is a possible thing. That makes it contingent.
3
Reflect on the implication
If the assignment is contingent, it requires a cause. What kind of cause could choose one specific nature over others? A free, purposeful agent.
This argument builds on the principle of sufficient reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحادي عشر : اختصاص كل برج بطبيعة معينة ، وأمر معين من الجائزات . الثاني عشر : ثبت بالدليل وجود خلاء لا نهاية له ، خارج العالم ، فوقوع كرة العالم في الجزء المعين من ذلك الخلاء ، دون سائر الأجزاء من الجائزات .
The eleventh: that each zodiac sign is assigned a specific nature and a specific matter among possible things. The twelfth: it is established by proof the existence of an infinite void outside the world, so the occurrence of the sphere of the world in the specific part of that void rather than the rest of the parts is among possible things.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct arguments from possibility (imkan). The eleventh argument points out that each zodiac sign has a specific nature and particular characteristics, which could have been otherwise, indicating contingency and thus the need for a purposeful agent. The twelfth argument posits the existence of an infinite void outside the world. Given that the world's sphere is located in one specific part of that infinite void rather than any other, this specific placement is a contingent fact requiring a cause.

Key Terms
الجائزات
اختصاص
برج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument presupposes the truth of Ptolemaic astrology.
Correction: Razi uses it dialectically; even within that framework, contingency is evident.

Checkpoint
According to the eleventh argument, what indicates contingency in the celestial realm?
A) The movement of the spheres
B) The assignment of a specific nature to each zodiac sign
C) The infinite void
D) The arrangement of the planets

Key Takeaway
The specific natures of zodiac signs are contingent, proving they need a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live your life according to patterns you did not choose—your temperament, your circumstances. Who assigned them? And are you living in gratitude to the One who gave them their specific nature, or do you take them as given and forget the Giver? Ask yourself today: what specific aspect of your life are you treating as 'just the way it is' rather than as a contingent gift from Allah? Write down one such aspect and sincerely thank Allah for it.
?
If the very stars require a chooser, how much more do the details of your daily life require submission to that choice? What specific nature in your own soul are you attributing to chance rather than to Allah's will? Identify one habit or character trait you have that you assume is fixed. Now, consciously acknowledge that Allah could have made it otherwise, and ask Him to help you align it with His purpose.
?
Consider something in your life you assume is 'just the way it is'—your habits, your characteristics. This argument says every specific nature is contingent. Will you acknowledge that Allah chose it for a purpose, and align yourself with that purpose? Reflect on a time you ignored the divine choice in a matter. What would it look like to submit that specific area to Allah today?

The Twelfth Argument: World's Position in the Infinite Void
Razi's twelfth argument from the world's position in the infinite void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the world's specific location in an infinite void demonstrates contingency and the need for a cause.

Core Idea
The world's place in the void is contingent, not necessary.

Razi's twelfth argument draws on the concept of an infinite void outside the cosmos. He argues that the world's sphere occupies one specific part of that void. Since other parts are equally possible, this specific placement is contingent and requires a cause. This lesson explains the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two claims: (1) there is an infinite void outside the world, and (2) the world is located in a specific part of it.
The existence of the void is taken as established by previous proof.
2
Identify the contingent element
The world could have been placed anywhere in the infinite void. Its actual location is one specific part; that is a contingent fact.
3
Consider the implication
If the location is contingent, it requires a cause that selected it. This points to a free agent who chose that spot.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحادي عشر : اختصاص كل برج بطبيعة معينة ، وأمر معين من الجائزات . الثاني عشر : ثبت بالدليل وجود خلاء لا نهاية له ، خارج العالم ، فوقوع كرة العالم في الجزء المعين من ذلك الخلاء ، دون سائر الأجزاء من الجائزات .
The eleventh: that each zodiac sign is assigned a specific nature and a specific matter among possible things. The twelfth: it is established by proof the existence of an infinite void outside the world, so the occurrence of the sphere of the world in the specific part of that void rather than the rest of the parts is among possible things.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct arguments from possibility (imkan). The eleventh argument points out that each zodiac sign has a specific nature and particular characteristics, which could have been otherwise, indicating contingency and thus the need for a purposeful agent. The twelfth argument posits the existence of an infinite void outside the world. Given that the world's sphere is located in one specific part of that infinite void rather than any other, this specific placement is a contingent fact requiring a cause.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument depends on the physical reality of an infinite void.
Correction: Razi uses it as a hypothetical based on earlier arguments; the contingency is independent of the void's actual existence.

The world's specific location in the infinite void is contingent, necessitating a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world's precise location in the void is a contingent fact that demands a cause, think about the specific circumstances of your own life—your family, your abilities, your opportunities. Are you living as if these are accidents, or as intentional gifts from a Chooser? What would change today if you truly believed each detail was placed by purpose?
?
Consider a belief or habit you hold simply because 'that's how it has always been'—like the world's location seems fixed. This argument exposes that such comfort can be a failure to acknowledge contingency. What is one area of your life where you need to stop assuming necessity and start seeking the Cause behind it?
?
The argument shows that even a 'small' fact like location points to a purposeful agent. How do you respond to the weight of this? Does it make you feel accountable for the details of your day? What specific action will you take today to align your life with the intentionality of the Creator?

The Contingency of Planetary Position
Razi's first aspect of contingency: the depression of the planet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the identity of parts in a celestial sphere to argue that the specific location of a planet is contingent.

Core Idea
Identical parts cannot determine a unique position without a chooser.

Razi begins his thirteenth argument with the premise that celestial spheres are simple, meaning all parts are identical. From this, he deduces that the specific spot where a planet resides cannot be necessary but must be possible, pointing to a free agent. In this lesson we examine the first of four aspects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the argument.
2
Identify the key premise
Note that the premise is that all parts are equal in nature.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand why the planet's specific depression is possible, not necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث عشر : زعموا : أن كل كرة من كرات الأفلاك فهو بسيط وهذا يوجب عليهم الإمكان من وجوه كثيرة : 
أحدها: أنه لما كان جميع أجزاء تلك الكرة متساوية في الطبيعة والماهية ، كان حصول النقرة التي حصل فيها الكوكب في جانب معين من ذلك الفلك دون سائر الجوانب ، من الجائزات 
وثانيها : لما كانت طبائع الكرات بسائط كانت طبيعة كل واحد من المتهمين اللذين ينفصلان عن الفلك المميل بسبب انفصال الفلك الخارج المركب عنه : طبيعة بسيطة . مع أن أحد الجانبين ، اختص بالرقة ، والجانب الثاني ، اختص بالثخن. فيكون ذلك من الجائزات . 
وثالثها : إن طبيعة السطح الأعلى من كل فلك تكون مساوية لطبيعة السطح الأسفل منه ، وكل ما جاز على الشيء ، جاز على مثله . 
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : كما جاز على كرة عطارد أن تماس كرة القمر بمقعرها ، وجب أن يجوز عليها أن تماسها بمحدبها ، ومتى ثبت هذا الجواز كان جواز الخرق والتفرق ، والتمزق لازما 
ورابعها : زعموا أن فلك التدوير مركوز في ثخن الفلك الخارج عن المركز ، كما يكون الفص في الخاتم ، وحينئذ يعود السؤال : إنه لم حصلت كرة التدوير في ذلك الموضع دون سائر المواضع مع أن جميع الأجزاء المفروضة في كرة الفلك متساوية في الطبيعة ؟ 
واعلم أن الاستقصاء في شرح هذه الأحوال سيأتي في باب الاستدلال على الصانع الحكيم بحدوث الصفات. فهذا هو الإشارة إلى معاقد الدلائل المأخوذة من أجرام الأفلاك بحسب إمكان صفاتها
The thirteenth: they claim that every sphere of the celestial spheres is simple, and this necessitates possibility for them from many aspects: 
One of them: that since all parts of that sphere are equal in nature and essence, the occurrence of the depression in which the planet occurs in a specific side of that orb rather than the other sides is among possible things. 
The second: that since the natures of spheres are simple, the nature of each of the two [contiguous?] that separate from the inclined orb due to the separation of the outer composite orb from it is a simple nature, while one of the two sides is assigned thinness and the other side is assigned thickness; so that is among possible things. 
The third: that the nature of the upper surface of each sphere is equal to the nature of its lower surface, and whatever is possible for a thing is possible for its like. 
If this is established, we say: just as it is possible for the sphere of Mercury to touch the sphere of the Moon by its concavity, it must be possible for it to touch it by its convexity, and when this possibility is established, the possibility of breaking, splitting, and tearing becomes necessary. 
The fourth: they claim that the epicycle is embedded in the thickness of the eccentric orb, like the gem in the ring, and then the question returns: why did the epicycle occur in that position rather than others, given that all supposed parts in the sphere's sphere are equal in nature? 
And know that the detailed explanation of these states will come in the chapter of inference to the Wise Creator from the temporality of attributes. So this is the indication to the knots of proofs taken from the celestial bodies in accordance with the possibility of their attributes.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the thirteenth argument, which contends that if celestial spheres are simple (بسيط), then many contingent features arise. The author lists four specific aspects. First, since all parts of a sphere are identical in nature, the planet's specific location in a depression on one side is contingent. Second, with simple natures, the separation of two contiguous parts from the inclined orb results in one being thin and the other thick, a contingent quality. Third, since upper and lower surfaces of a sphere are equal in nature, what is possible for one is possible for the other; thus, if the sphere of Mercury can touch the Moon's sphere from the concave side, it could also from the convex side, leading to the possibility of rupture—a contingent state. Fourth, the epicycle's specific position within the eccentric orb, like a gem in a ring, is arbitrary given all parts are equal, again showing contingency. The author remarks that these arguments are brief indications; full elaboration will come later in discussing proofs from the temporality of attributes.

Key Terms
النقرة
بسيط
الجائزات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These arguments are obsolete astronomical details.
Correction: Razi uses astronomy dialectically to show that any specific configuration requires a free chooser. The astronomical details are secondary to the logical principle.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise of the first aspect?
A) Celestial spheres are made of different materials
B) All parts of the sphere are equal in nature
C) The planet's depression is necessary
D) The sphere is not simple

Key Takeaway
Homogeneity in celestial spheres implies that specific locations are contingent, not necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the celestial spheres, being simple, cannot determine their own specifics, how can you, with all your complexity, claim autonomy in the details of your life? Where do you need to acknowledge a Chooser's hand today?
?
You now know that the specific position of a planet is contingent upon a chooser. Look at your current position in life—your job, your family, your health. Do you treat these as necessary outcomes of cause and effect, or as gifts from a specific Chooser? What would change if you genuinely saw them as contingent and chosen for you?

Contingent Qualities of Simple Spheres
Razi's second aspect: thinness and thickness in separated parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the simplicity of spheres leads to contingent differences in thinness and thickness.

Core Idea
Simple nature cannot explain why one side is thin and the other thick.

In the second aspect, Razi considers that when parts separate from an inclined orb, they retain a simple nature yet one side becomes thin and the other thick. This contrast cannot arise from the simple nature itself, so it is contingent. Let's explore this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the separation and the resulting thinness/thickness.
2
Analyze the logic
Notice that the simple nature is the same on both sides, but the resulting qualities differ. This shows the qualities are not inherent.
The term 'المتهمين' might be a misprint for 'المتماسين' meaning contiguous parts.
3
Draw the conclusion
Understand that this difference is possible, not necessary, pointing to an external cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث عشر : زعموا : أن كل كرة من كرات الأفلاك فهو بسيط وهذا يوجب عليهم الإمكان من وجوه كثيرة : 
أحدها: أنه لما كان جميع أجزاء تلك الكرة متساوية في الطبيعة والماهية ، كان حصول النقرة التي حصل فيها الكوكب في جانب معين من ذلك الفلك دون سائر الجوانب ، من الجائزات 
وثانيها : لما كانت طبائع الكرات بسائط كانت طبيعة كل واحد من المتهمين اللذين ينفصلان عن الفلك المميل بسبب انفصال الفلك الخارج المركب عنه : طبيعة بسيطة . مع أن أحد الجانبين ، اختص بالرقة ، والجانب الثاني ، اختص بالثخن. فيكون ذلك من الجائزات . 
وثالثها : إن طبيعة السطح الأعلى من كل فلك تكون مساوية لطبيعة السطح الأسفل منه ، وكل ما جاز على الشيء ، جاز على مثله . 
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : كما جاز على كرة عطارد أن تماس كرة القمر بمقعرها ، وجب أن يجوز عليها أن تماسها بمحدبها ، ومتى ثبت هذا الجواز كان جواز الخرق والتفرق ، والتمزق لازما 
ورابعها : زعموا أن فلك التدوير مركوز في ثخن الفلك الخارج عن المركز ، كما يكون الفص في الخاتم ، وحينئذ يعود السؤال : إنه لم حصلت كرة التدوير في ذلك الموضع دون سائر المواضع مع أن جميع الأجزاء المفروضة في كرة الفلك متساوية في الطبيعة ؟ 
واعلم أن الاستقصاء في شرح هذه الأحوال سيأتي في باب الاستدلال على الصانع الحكيم بحدوث الصفات. فهذا هو الإشارة إلى معاقد الدلائل المأخوذة من أجرام الأفلاك بحسب إمكان صفاتها
The thirteenth: they claim that every sphere of the celestial spheres is simple, and this necessitates possibility for them from many aspects: 
One of them: that since all parts of that sphere are equal in nature and essence, the occurrence of the depression in which the planet occurs in a specific side of that orb rather than the other sides is among possible things. 
The second: that since the natures of spheres are simple, the nature of each of the two [contiguous?] that separate from the inclined orb due to the separation of the outer composite orb from it is a simple nature, while one of the two sides is assigned thinness and the other side is assigned thickness; so that is among possible things. 
The third: that the nature of the upper surface of each sphere is equal to the nature of its lower surface, and whatever is possible for a thing is possible for its like. 
If this is established, we say: just as it is possible for the sphere of Mercury to touch the sphere of the Moon by its concavity, it must be possible for it to touch it by its convexity, and when this possibility is established, the possibility of breaking, splitting, and tearing becomes necessary. 
The fourth: they claim that the epicycle is embedded in the thickness of the eccentric orb, like the gem in the ring, and then the question returns: why did the epicycle occur in that position rather than others, given that all supposed parts in the sphere's sphere are equal in nature? 
And know that the detailed explanation of these states will come in the chapter of inference to the Wise Creator from the temporality of attributes. So this is the indication to the knots of proofs taken from the celestial bodies in accordance with the possibility of their attributes.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the thirteenth argument, which contends that if celestial spheres are simple (بسيط), then many contingent features arise. The author lists four specific aspects. First, since all parts of a sphere are identical in nature, the planet's specific location in a depression on one side is contingent. Second, with simple natures, the separation of two contiguous parts from the inclined orb results in one being thin and the other thick, a contingent quality. Third, since upper and lower surfaces of a sphere are equal in nature, what is possible for one is possible for the other; thus, if the sphere of Mercury can touch the Moon's sphere from the concave side, it could also from the convex side, leading to the possibility of rupture—a contingent state. Fourth, the epicycle's specific position within the eccentric orb, like a gem in a ring, is arbitrary given all parts are equal, again showing contingency. The author remarks that these arguments are brief indications; full elaboration will come later in discussing proofs from the temporality of attributes.

Key Terms
بسائط
الرقة
الثخن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The thinness/thickness is inherent in the simple nature.
Correction: Since both sides have the same simple nature, the difference must come from an external cause.

Checkpoint
What property do the two separated parts share according to Razi?
A) They have different natures
B) They have a simple nature
C) They are both thick
D) They are both thin

Key Takeaway
Identical simple natures do not explain differing qualities like thinness and thickness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a quality you have that you consider unchangeable or part of your essence. According to Razi's reasoning, if that quality were truly inherent, it would not differ from others with the same nature. What external factors might actually be shaping that quality? How does that change your responsibility toward it?
?
When you see someone's character flaw, do you quickly attribute it to their 'nature'? Razi shows that identical natures cannot produce different outcomes without an external cause. What external factors in that person's life might you be overlooking, and how should that affect your judgment?
?
If your own existence is contingent like the thinness and thickness in Razi's example, what does that imply about your ultimate dependence? Are you actively acknowledging that dependency in your daily life, or are you living as if you are self-sufficient?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
6 min
Possibility of Rupture in Spheres
Razi's third aspect: from surface equality to potential rupture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the equality of upper and lower surfaces leads to the possibility of rupture in celestial spheres.

Core Idea
Equal surfaces imply equal possibilities, opening the door to rupture.

The third aspect uses the principle of similar natures to argue that if a sphere can touch another by its concave surface, it must also be able to touch by its convex surface. This leads to the possibility of breaking and tearing, a contingent state. Let's examine this chain of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the conditional structure 'if this is established, we say...'
2
Understand the equality premise
The upper and lower surfaces of a sphere have equal natures, so what is possible for one is possible for the other.
3
Follow the inference
If touching by concavity is possible (as observed with Mercury and Moon), then touching by convexity is also possible, which would cause rupture. This rupture is thus possible.
Rupture (الخرق والتفرق) is a contingent property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث عشر : زعموا : أن كل كرة من كرات الأفلاك فهو بسيط وهذا يوجب عليهم الإمكان من وجوه كثيرة : 
أحدها: أنه لما كان جميع أجزاء تلك الكرة متساوية في الطبيعة والماهية ، كان حصول النقرة التي حصل فيها الكوكب في جانب معين من ذلك الفلك دون سائر الجوانب ، من الجائزات 
وثانيها : لما كانت طبائع الكرات بسائط كانت طبيعة كل واحد من المتهمين اللذين ينفصلان عن الفلك المميل بسبب انفصال الفلك الخارج المركب عنه : طبيعة بسيطة . مع أن أحد الجانبين ، اختص بالرقة ، والجانب الثاني ، اختص بالثخن. فيكون ذلك من الجائزات . 
وثالثها : إن طبيعة السطح الأعلى من كل فلك تكون مساوية لطبيعة السطح الأسفل منه ، وكل ما جاز على الشيء ، جاز على مثله . 
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : كما جاز على كرة عطارد أن تماس كرة القمر بمقعرها ، وجب أن يجوز عليها أن تماسها بمحدبها ، ومتى ثبت هذا الجواز كان جواز الخرق والتفرق ، والتمزق لازما 
ورابعها : زعموا أن فلك التدوير مركوز في ثخن الفلك الخارج عن المركز ، كما يكون الفص في الخاتم ، وحينئذ يعود السؤال : إنه لم حصلت كرة التدوير في ذلك الموضع دون سائر المواضع مع أن جميع الأجزاء المفروضة في كرة الفلك متساوية في الطبيعة ؟ 
واعلم أن الاستقصاء في شرح هذه الأحوال سيأتي في باب الاستدلال على الصانع الحكيم بحدوث الصفات. فهذا هو الإشارة إلى معاقد الدلائل المأخوذة من أجرام الأفلاك بحسب إمكان صفاتها

The second: that since the natures of spheres are simple, the nature of each of the two [contiguous?] that separate from the inclined orb due to the separation of the outer composite orb from it is a simple nature, while one of the two sides is assigned thinness and the other side is assigned thickness; so that is among possible things.

Key Terms
السطح الأعلى
السطح الأسفل
الخرق والتفرق
التمزق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The possibility of rupture means spheres actually break.
Correction: Razi is arguing that rupture is possible (jā'iz), not actual, which suffices to show contingency.

Checkpoint
What does Razi conclude from the equality of upper and lower surfaces?
A) The spheres cannot touch each other
B) Touching by convexity is impossible
C) Rupture becomes possible
D) The surfaces are made of different materials

Key Takeaway
Equal surfaces imply equal possibilities, making rupture a contingent possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live your life assuming the sun will rise tomorrow. But Razi's argument shows that even the celestial spheres are contingent—they could rupture. Does your heart truly recognize that everything you rely on is held in existence by Allah every moment? What would change if you lived with that awareness today?
?
The possibility of rupture reveals that the universe has no necessity of its own. Yet you often act as if your health, wealth, and relationships are guaranteed. What is one area of your life where you have forgotten this contingency, and how will you remind yourself of it today?
